Workstation Setup / Quick Reference
Reference

Quick Reference

Daily-use commands in one place — no hunting through install steps. This is the panel you open after setup is done.

01

tmux

KeysAction
Ctrl+b %Split pane right
Ctrl+b "Split pane below
Ctrl+b h/j/k/lNavigate panes (vim-style)
Ctrl+b xClose current pane
Ctrl+b dDetach session (leaves it running)
tmux new-session -A -s <name>Create or attach to named session
tmux lsList running sessions
tmux attach -t <name>Re-attach to a session
Ctrl+b yEnter copy mode
v (copy mode)Start selection
y (copy mode)Copy selection to macOS clipboard
02

Neovim

KeysAction
-Open oil.nvim file browser
:NeogitOpen git UI (stage, commit, push)
:lua Snacks.picker.files()Fuzzy file finder
:lua Snacks.picker.grep()Grep across codebase
:LazyOpen plugin manager UI
Space (leader)Leader key
03

Claude Code

CommandAction
claudeStart interactive session in current directory
claude -p "prompt"One-shot non-interactive run
/clearReset context between unrelated tasks
/compactSummarise conversation to free up context
/initGenerate starter AGENTS.md for current project
EscStop Claude mid-action
Esc EscOpen rewind menu — restore to any checkpoint
04

lavish-axi

CommandAction
lavish-axi <file.html>Open artifact in browser for review
lavish-axi poll <file.html>Wait for feedback from browser
lavish-axi end <file.html>End the review session
lavish-axi export <file.html>Export portable self-contained HTML
lavish-axi share <file.html>Publish to ht-ml.app and get a public URL
05

Reference

ResourceWhat's there
Kun Chen — YouTubeAgentic engineering workflow video this entire setup is based on
axi.mdOfficial lavish-axi docs — playbooks, commands, design guidance
github/lavish-axiSource code, releases, issue tracker
06

Agentic layout — standard tmux split

build this layout
┌─────────────────┬──────────────────┐ │ │ 2: shell │ │ 1: nvim │ (git, builds) │ │ ├──────────────────┤ │ │ 3: Claude Code │ └─────────────────┴──────────────────┘ ➜ tmux new-session -A -s work ➜ nvim Ctrl+b % → split right (pane 2) Ctrl+b " → split below (pane 3) ➜ claude Ctrl+b h → back to nvim
Environment

Terminal Setup

Installing and configuring WezTerm on macOS (Apple Silicon), including the Nerd Font, the full wezterm.lua, and the troubleshooting steps that actually got it working.

01

Install the Hack Nerd Font

WezTerm's config references this font by name, so it needs to exist before the config will render correctly.

zsh
➜ brew install --cask font-hack-nerd-font ==> Installing Cask font-hack-nerd-font 🍺 font-hack-nerd-font was successfully installed!
02

Create the config folder

WezTerm looks for its config at ~/.config/wezterm/wezterm.lua on macOS.

zsh
➜ mkdir -p ~/.config/wezterm
03

Install WezTerm itself

Easy to skip since the font and config feel like "the setup" — the app still has to be installed separately.

! Don't skip this one. The font and config can be fully in place while the app itself is still missing — which wezterm will come back empty and nothing will look different.
zsh
➜ brew install --cask wezterm
04

Write the config file

Full contents of ~/.config/wezterm/wezterm.lua — OS-aware, with separate branches for macOS and Windows.

~/.config/wezterm/wezterm.lua
local wezterm = require("wezterm") local config = wezterm.config_builder() local is_windows = os.getenv("OS") and os.getenv("OS"):lower():find("windows") local is_macos = wezterm.target_triple:lower():find("darwin") ~= nil config.color_scheme = "rose-pine-moon" config.max_fps = 120 config.font = wezterm.font("Hack Nerd Font", { weight = "DemiBold" }) config.window_decorations = "INTEGRATED_BUTTONS|RESIZE" config.window_frame = { font = wezterm.font("Hack Nerd Font", { weight = "Bold" }), } config.inactive_pane_hsb = { saturation = 0.0, brightness = 0.5, } if is_windows then config.win32_system_backdrop = "Acrylic" config.window_background_opacity = 0.7 config.window_frame.font_size = 10.0 end if is_macos then config.window_background_opacity = 0.8 config.macos_window_background_blur = 50 config.font_size = 15.0 config.window_frame.font_size = 13.0 end return config

What each part actually does

SettingEffect
color_schemeBuilt-in Rose Pine Moon palette — no separate install needed.
max_fpsCaps rendering at 120fps for smoother scrolling on high refresh-rate displays.
fontHack Nerd Font, DemiBold weight, for the terminal body text.
window_decorationsMerges the close/minimize/zoom buttons into the tab bar instead of a separate title bar.
inactive_pane_hsbDesaturates and dims panes that aren't focused, so the active split stands out.
is_macos block0.8 window opacity, 50pt background blur, 15pt terminal font, 13pt frame font — macOS-only.
is_windows blockAcrylic backdrop, 0.7 opacity, 10pt frame font — only applies on Windows machines.
05

Launch WezTerm

Open it directly rather than assuming a terminal window is already WezTerm — Terminal.app and iTerm will happily run the same commands and show none of this styling.

zsh
➜ open -a WezTerm
i Config changes apply automatically on save. If a change doesn't seem to take, reload manually with Cmd + Shift + R inside the WezTerm window.
06

Troubleshooting checklist

In order — this is the exact sequence that found the actual problem last time (WezTerm was never installed).

zsh
➜ cat ~/.config/wezterm/wezterm.lua # confirms the file actually saved and has all 33 lines
zsh
➜ which wezterm ➜ ls /Applications/ | grep -i wezterm # confirms whether WezTerm is installed at all
zsh
➜ /Applications/WezTerm.app/Contents/MacOS/wezterm # runs WezTerm in the foreground so any config/startup error prints right here
zsh
➜ wezterm ls-fonts --config-file ~/.config/wezterm/wezterm.lua # validates the Lua file directly and surfaces syntax errors
✓ Root cause last time: the font and config were both correctly in place — WezTerm itself just hadn't been installed yet. brew install --cask wezterm fixed it.

WezTerm — done.
Rose Pine Moon, Hack Nerd Font, 15pt body text, 50pt blur.

wezterm
➜ ready

Installing tmux and applying the config, ported from a Nix Home Manager module (common.nix) into ~/.config/tmux/tmux.conf — keeping all tool configs under ~/.config/, consistent with WezTerm.

i Source is a fragment. The screenshot was mid-file (absolute line 210 of a longer common.nix), and a few bind-key lines ran past the edge of the screen. The lines marked below are reconstructed from standard tmux convention, not read verbatim — worth a quick diff against the source file if you want it exact.
01

Install tmux

Via Homebrew, same as WezTerm.

zsh
➜ brew install tmux
02

Create the config folder and file

tmux 3.1+ follows the XDG spec and looks for config at ~/.config/tmux/tmux.conf automatically — same pattern as WezTerm, no extra env vars needed.

zsh
➜ mkdir -p ~/.config/tmux && touch ~/.config/tmux/tmux.conf
03

Write the config

Ported setting-by-setting from the Home Manager module's typed options, plus its extraConfig block copied through as raw tmux syntax.

~/.config/tmux/tmux.conf
# Ported from common.nix (programs.tmux) set -g base-index 1 set -g pane-base-index 1 set -g clock-mode-style 24 set -g focus-events on set -g mouse on set -g default-terminal "screen-256color" setw -g mode-keys vi set -g status-keys vi # extraConfig (verbatim) set -g renumber-windows on set -g escape-time 10 set -g extended-keys on set -g extended-keys-format csi-u set -g status-position top set -g pane-border-lines heavy setw -gu window-style setw -gu window-active-style bind-key -r h select-pane -L bind-key -r j select-pane -D bind-key -r k select-pane -U bind-key -r l select-pane -R bind-key '"' split-window -c "#{pane_current_path}" bind-key % split-window -h -c "#{pane_current_path}" bind-key c new-window -c "#{pane_current_path}" bind-key & kill-window bind-key y copy-mode bind-key -T copy-mode-vi v send-keys -X begin-selection bind-key -T copy-mode-vi y send-keys -X copy-pipe-and-cancel "pbcopy" ← reconstructed bind-key -T copy-mode-vi Enter send-keys -X copy-pipe-and-cancel "pbcopy" ← reconstructed

What each part does

SettingEffect
base-index / pane-base-indexWindows and panes start numbering at 1 instead of 0.
clock-mode-style 2424-hour clock in tmux's built-in clock view (prefix + t).
focus-events onLets apps inside tmux (like Neovim) know when a pane gains/loses focus.
mode-keys / status-keys viVi-style navigation in copy-mode and the command prompt.
mouse onClick to switch panes, drag to resize, scroll to scroll back.
escape-time 10Cuts the delay after pressing Escape — makes Neovim inside tmux feel instant.
pane-border-lines heavyThicker visual divider between panes.
h / j / k / l select-paneVim-style pane navigation, repeatable (-r) without re-pressing the prefix each time.
" and %Split the current pane vertically / horizontally, opening in the same working directory.
copy-pipe-and-cancel "pbcopy"Sends the selection straight to the macOS clipboard when copying in vi copy-mode.
04

Load it

Start a new session, or reload the config into a session that's already running.

zsh
➜ tmux # starts a fresh unnamed session using ~/.tmux.conf
inside tmux
➜ tmux source-file ~/.config/tmux/tmux.conf # reloads config without restarting the session
i Default prefix is still Ctrl + b — this config doesn't remap it, so pane splits are prefix then " or %, and pane navigation is prefix then h/j/k/l.
05

Start a named session

Always give sessions a name — makes it easy to find and re-attach later. The -A flag attaches if the session already exists, or creates it if it doesn't.

zsh
➜ tmux new-session -A -s devops-test # creates session named "devops-test", or attaches if it already exists
! Common typo: the command is tmux, not tumx. Easy to mix up — double-check before hitting Enter.
06

Detach and re-attach

Detaching leaves the session running in the background — everything keeps going. Re-attach from any new terminal window.

Detach (leave session running)

⌨ Ctrl + b, then release both keys, then press d
This is a two-step sequence — press the prefix first, let go, then the key. Not Ctrl+A+D (that's GNU Screen). Not Ctrl+D (that closes the shell entirely).

Check what sessions are running

zsh
➜ tmux ls devops-test: 1 windows (created Wed Jul 2 10:00:00 2025) # if the session appears here, it's alive — just detached

Re-attach

zsh
➜ tmux attach -t devops-test # or use new-session -A -s devops-test — same result
i If detaching closes your terminal window, don't worry — the session is still alive. Open a new terminal and run tmux ls to confirm, then re-attach.
07

Pane splitting & navigation

All splits open in the same working directory (set by the config). Navigate between panes with vim keys — no mouse required.

Split commands

KeysAction
Ctrl+b then %Split right — new pane side by side
Ctrl+b then "Split below — new pane underneath
Ctrl+b then xClose the current pane

Navigate between panes

KeysAction
Ctrl+b then hMove to the pane on the left
Ctrl+b then lMove to the pane on the right
Ctrl+b then kMove to the pane above
Ctrl+b then jMove to the pane below

Standard agentic layout

This is the layout to use when working with Claude Code — editor on the left, shell and agent on the right.

layout diagram
┌─────────────────┬──────────────────┐ │ │ 2: shell │ │ 1: nvim │ (git, builds) │ │ ├──────────────────┤ │ │ 3: Claude Code │ └─────────────────┴──────────────────┘ # How to build it: ➜ tmux new-session -A -s work # start session ➜ nvim # open editor in pane 1 Ctrl+b % # split right → pane 2 (shell) Ctrl+b " # split below → pane 3 (Claude Code) ➜ claude # launch Claude Code in pane 3 Ctrl+b h # jump back left to nvim
i The split commands (% and ") act on whichever pane is currently focused — move to the right pane first before splitting below, or you'll split the left pane instead.

tmux — done.
Core bindings and vi copy-mode confirmed; verify the two copy-pipe-and-cancel lines against the source if exactness matters.

tmux
➜ attached

Installing Neovim and wiring up three plugins from Kun Chen's agentic workflow — oil.nvim for file navigation, neogit for git, and snacks.nvim for fuzzy finding. Plugin manager is lazy.nvim.

01

Install Neovim

Via Homebrew. Installs the latest stable release.

zsh
➜ brew install neovim 🍺 /opt/homebrew/Cellar/neovim/0.12.3: 2,112 files, 29.7MB
zsh
➜ nvim --version NVIM v0.12.3
02

Create the config folder

Neovim looks for its config at ~/.config/nvim/init.lua — same ~/.config/ pattern as WezTerm and tmux.

zsh
➜ mkdir -p ~/.config/nvim
i ~/.config/ now holds all three tools: wezterm/, tmux/, and nvim/ — one place for everything.
03

Write the config — init.lua

Full contents of ~/.config/nvim/init.lua. Bootstraps lazy.nvim on first launch and registers the three plugins.

~/.config/nvim/init.lua
-- Bootstrap lazy.nvim local lazypath = vim.fn.stdpath("data") .. "/lazy/lazy.nvim" if not vim.loop.fs_stat(lazypath) then vim.fn.system({ "git", "clone", "--filter=blob:none", "https://github.com/folke/lazy.nvim.git", "--branch=stable", lazypath, }) end vim.opt.rtp:prepend(lazypath) -- Options vim.opt.number = true vim.opt.relativenumber = true vim.opt.tabstop = 2 vim.opt.shiftwidth = 2 vim.opt.expandtab = true vim.opt.termguicolors = true -- Leader key vim.g.mapleader = " " -- Plugins require("lazy").setup({ -- File navigation (replaces netrw) { "stevearc/oil.nvim", opts = {} }, -- Git: status, diffs, commits { "NeogitOrg/neogit", dependencies = { "nvim-lua/plenary.nvim", "sindrets/diffview.nvim", }, config = true, }, -- File finding and codebase grepping { "folke/snacks.nvim", priority = 1000, lazy = false, opts = { picker = { enabled = true } }, }, })

What each plugin does

PluginRole
lazy.nvimPlugin manager — bootstraps itself on first launch, then installs and loads everything else.
oil.nvimBrowse and edit the filesystem like a buffer. Replaces the built-in netrw file explorer.
neogitFull git UI inside Neovim — stage, commit, diff, push without leaving the editor.
snacks.nvimFuzzy file finder and codebase grep. Find any file or search across the whole project instantly.
04

Launch Neovim — plugins install automatically

On the very first launch, lazy.nvim clones itself and then installs all three plugins. No manual steps.

zsh
➜ nvim lazy.nvim cloning... Installing oil.nvim ✓ Installing neogit ✓ Installing snacks.nvim ✓
✓ After the install completes, press q to close the lazy.nvim window and you're in the editor.
05

Essential key bindings

The three plugins, each opened with one command. Leader key is Space.

KeysAction
-Open oil.nvim — browse files in the current directory
:NeogitOpen neogit — git status, stage, commit, push
:lua Snacks.picker.files()Open snacks file finder — fuzzy search across the project
:lua Snacks.picker.grep()Open snacks grep — search text across the whole codebase
:LazyOpen the lazy.nvim UI — update or inspect plugins
:qQuit Neovim

Neovim — done.
v0.12.3 · lazy.nvim · oil.nvim · neogit · snacks.nvim

nvim
NVIM v0.12.3
Productivity

OpenSuperWhisper

Local speech-to-text on Apple Silicon using OpenAI's Whisper model. No cloud, no subscription — speak anywhere on macOS and the transcription is pasted in place.

01

Install via Homebrew

Requires Apple Silicon and macOS 14 or later. The app ships as a cask — no CLI tool, just the app bundle.

zsh
➜ brew install --cask opensuperwhisper ==> Moving App 'OpenSuperWhisper.app' to '/Applications/OpenSuperWhisper.app' 🍺 opensuperwhisper was successfully installed!
02

Download a Whisper model

On first launch the app asks you to pick a model. Transcription runs entirely on-device using these models — nothing is sent to a server.

ModelDescriptionPick if
Parakeet v3Fastest processing and accurateBest default - start here
Parakeet v2Fastest processing, English-only, higher recallEnglish-only and want max speed
Whisper MediumBalanced speed and accuracyMultilingual or mixed content
Whisper SmallVery fast processingLow memory, speed over accuracy
Whisper V3 LargeHigh accuracy, best qualityLong-form dictation, highest accuracy needed
i Download only one to start — Parakeet v3 is the best default for everyday use on Apple Silicon. You can switch models any time from the same screen.
03

Grant microphone & accessibility permissions

Two permissions are required. macOS will prompt for both on first use — grant them from System Settings if the prompts don't appear.

PermissionWhy it's needed
MicrophoneTo capture your voice while recording
AccessibilityTo paste the transcription into whichever app is in focus
i System Settings → Privacy & Security → Accessibility → enable OpenSuperWhisper. Without this the text is transcribed but cannot be auto-pasted.
04

How to use it

Works in any app — terminal, browser, editor, chat. No mode switching or copy-paste needed.

ActionWhat happens
Hold Right ⌥ OptionRecording starts — speak while holding the key
Release Right ⌥ OptionWhisper transcribes locally, text is pasted at cursor
Change shortcutApp preferences → Shortcut — can switch between Left ⌥ and Right ⌥
✓ Practical use: hold the shortcut, say a long prompt to Claude Code, release — the full text appears in the terminal. Faster than typing long agent instructions.

OpenSuperWhisper — installed.
Local Whisper · on-device · pastes at cursor

OpenSuperWhisper 0.1.0

Agentic Tooling

Claude Code Setup

One-time setup on your machine. Do this once — every project you work on will inherit these settings automatically.

01

Install Claude Code

Via npm. Installs the claude CLI globally.

zsh
➜ npm install -g @anthropic-ai/claude-code
zsh
➜ claude --version # confirms the CLI is on your PATH
02

Agent instruction file structure

There are two levels of global instruction files. Both are read automatically — no config needed.

FileScope
~/AGENTS.mdUniversal — works across Claude Code, OpenCode, and any other agent harness
~/.claude/CLAUDE.mdClaude Code specific — supplements AGENTS.md with Claude-only preferences
./CLAUDE.mdProject — checked into git, shared with the team
./CLAUDE.local.mdProject personal — gitignored, your own per-project overrides
i Keep both files short. For every line ask: "Would removing this cause the agent to make mistakes?" If not, cut it. Bloated instruction files get ignored.
03

Write ~/AGENTS.md

The primary global file. Placed at the home directory so every agent harness picks it up automatically — not just Claude Code.

~/AGENTS.md
# Manjesh's agent instructions These are common instructions for Manjesh's agents across all scenarios. ## General Guidelines - Never use the em dash "—". Use plain dash "-" instead. - When writing commit messages, NEVER auto-add your agent name as co-author. - Never manually modify CHANGELOG.md files or any files marked as auto-generated. - When writing or substantially editing long Markdown files, put each full sentence on its own line. Avoid wrapping multiple sentences onto one line. - When making technical decisions, do not give much weight to development cost. Instead, prefer quality, simplicity, robustness, scalability, and long term maintainability. - When doing bug fixes, always start with reproducing the bug in an E2E setting as closely aligned with how an end user would experience it. - When end-to-end testing a product, be picky about the UI and be obsessed with pixel perfection. If something looks off, try to get it fixed alongside the current task. - Apply that same high standard to engineering excellence: lint, test failures, and test flakiness. If you see one, still get it fixed. ## Manjesh's Opinions When you are working on something that would benefit from being informed by Manjesh's viewpoints, read ~/OPINIONS.md to understand his perspective. ## Voice Profile When you are talking/posting on behalf of Manjesh using his identity, read ~/VOICE.md to see how Manjesh talks.
04

Symlink ~/.claude/CLAUDE.md to ~/AGENTS.md

Instead of maintaining two files, create a symlink — ~/.claude/CLAUDE.md points to ~/AGENTS.md. Edit one file, both agents see the same content automatically.

~/.claude/CLAUDE.md
➜ ln -s ~/AGENTS.md ~/.claude/CLAUDE.md # verify it ➜ ls -la ~/.claude/CLAUDE.md lrwxr-xr-x ~/.claude/CLAUDE.md -> /Users/manjesh/AGENTS.md
✓ One file, two names. Claude Code reads ~/.claude/CLAUDE.md, other agents read ~/AGENTS.md — both resolve to the same file. Edit ~/AGENTS.md and the change is instantly visible to all agents.
05

Full file structure on disk

Everything in one view — where each file lives and what it covers.

home directory layout
~/ ├── AGENTS.md # universal agent instructions (all harnesses) ├── OPINIONS.md # personal viewpoints — referenced by AGENTS.md ├── VOICE.md # writing voice — referenced by AGENTS.md └── .claude/ └── CLAUDE.md # Claude Code specific additions ~/.config/ ├── wezterm/ │ └── wezterm.lua ├── tmux/ │ └── tmux.conf └── nvim/ └── init.lua
06

The agentic workflow loop

The repeatable cycle for every task — plan, implement, validate, ship.

PhaseWhat happens
1. PlanDescribe the task. Claude explores the codebase and creates an implementation plan before touching any code.
2. ImplementClaude codes against the plan. You watch, redirect, or step away.
3. ValidateClaude runs tests, shows evidence. A second Claude session reviews the diff in a fresh context.
4. ShipClaude commits with a conventional message and opens a PR via gh.
i Run /clear between unrelated tasks to reset context. Long sessions with irrelevant history reduce Claude's accuracy.
07

Essential Claude Code commands

CommandWhat it does
claudeStart an interactive session in the current directory
claude -p "prompt"One-shot non-interactive run — useful in scripts and CI
/clearReset context window — run between unrelated tasks
/initGenerate a starter CLAUDE.md for the current project
/compactSummarise conversation to free up context
EscStop Claude mid-action — context preserved, you can redirect
Esc EscOpen rewind menu — restore code and conversation to any checkpoint

Repeat this for every new project. Checked into git so the agent always has full context — content changes per project, structure stays the same.

01

Project-level AGENTS.md

Every project gets its own AGENTS.md at the repo root. The global ~/AGENTS.md sets your personal rules — this file tells the agent what this specific codebase is. Both are read together every session.

i The global ~/AGENTS.md sets your personal rules. The project ./AGENTS.md tells the agent what this specific codebase is. Both are read together every session — the agent combines them automatically.

Full template — copy for every new Python/FastAPI project

./AGENTS.md — project root
# agent instructions ## What this project is <Plain English description of what the service does and who it is for. Include what endpoints exist, what data flows through them, and any non-obvious infrastructure constraints. Write this as if briefing a new engineer on their first day.> This file is the canonical architecture and workflow guide for this project. ## Stack and layout - Python 3.x, FastAPI, uvicorn - main.py - app entry point, FastAPI instance - routers/ - one file per feature / route group - models/ - Pydantic models and schemas - services/ - business logic, called by routers - tests/ - pytest test files - .env - local env vars (never commit) ## How to run - uv pip install -r requirements.txt # install deps - uvicorn main:app --reload # http://localhost:8000 - pytest # run all tests - ruff check . && ruff format . # lint and format ## E2E testing The API is the surface. Proving a change works means hitting the real running server with real HTTP requests and checking the response. - Before an E2E pass, write down the one endpoint behaviour to prove. - Keep the pass narrow - one route, one scenario. - Repeatable checks belong in pytest, not manual curl steps. ### Start the server uvicorn main:app --reload & Wait for it to be ready: until curl -s http://localhost:8000/health; do sleep 1; done ### What E2E can and cannot cover Can: - HTTP request/response correctness (status codes, body, headers) - Behaviour differences between valid and invalid inputs - Side effects visible via subsequent GET requests Cannot: - Internal async task state not exposed via an endpoint - Third-party service calls (mock or skip in E2E) ## Project conventions - Package manager: uv (lockfile is uv.lock) or pip with requirements.txt. - Tests: pytest. Lint/format: ruff check . && ruff format . - Test files live in tests/ mirroring the source layout. - TDD expected for bug fixes and new features. - Do not auto-add an AI co-author to commits. - Never commit .env files or secrets. ## One-time understanding <Non-obvious env vars, credential locations, infra quirks. Remove entries once they are no longer true.> - Copy .env.example to .env and fill in values before running locally.
! TODO - move E2E instructions to a skill. The E2E testing section in the project AGENTS.md loads on every request, burning tokens even when you are not testing. Extract it into a dedicated ~/.agents/skills/e2e-testing/SKILL.md so it only loads when you ask for it.

What each section does

SectionPurposeRequired?
What this project isFull context on the service before the agent touches any code — purpose, endpoints, data flow, infrastructure constraints.Always
Stack and layoutPython/FastAPI folder map so the agent knows where routers, models, services, and tests live without exploring blindly.Always
Key terminologyOnly needed if the project has domain-specific words that must stay consistent. Skip if not applicable.If applicable
How to runuv/pip install, uvicorn start, pytest, ruff — so the agent can run and verify the service on its own.Always
E2E testingProve a change works by hitting the real running server with curl or httpx. Without this, "looks done" is the only signal.Always
E2E - Start the serverHow to start uvicorn in the background and poll until the health endpoint is up before testing.Always
E2E - Can / can't coverScopes what the agent can prove via HTTP (status, body, side effects) vs what it cannot (async internals, third-party calls).Always
Project conventionsuv/pip, pytest, ruff — keeps the agent from using the wrong tool, skipping tests, or committing secrets.Always
One-time understandingNon-obvious env vars, .env setup, infra quirks. Remove entries once stale.As needed

Agentic Tooling

Skills & Plugins

Skills extend Claude Code with reusable workflows and domain knowledge. Installed globally with npx skills — they live at ~/.agents/skills/ and are symlinked into Claude Code automatically.

01

How skills work

A skill is a SKILL.md file that Claude reads when the task matches. It gives Claude domain knowledge and a repeatable workflow — without you having to re-explain the same process every session.

LocationScope
~/.agents/skills/Global — available in every project
.claude/skills/Project — only loaded for that repo
i Skills are symlinked from ~/.agents/skills/ into ~/.claude/skills/ automatically. Edit the source in ~/.agents/ — the change is instantly live in Claude Code.
02

Install the skill-creator skill

The first skill to install — it lets you create, test, and iterate on new skills from inside Claude Code. Installed globally with -g so it works in every project.

zsh
➜ npx skills add anthropics/skills --skill skill-creator -g ◇ Repository cloned ◇ Found 18 skills ● Selected 1 skill: skill-creator ◇ Installation complete ✓ ~/.agents/skills/skill-creator (symlinked → Claude Code)
✓ Skill installs to ~/.agents/skills/skill-creator/ and is symlinked into Claude Code. Review skills before use — they run with full agent permissions.
03

What skill-creator does

A meta-skill for building other skills. It guides you through the full creation loop inside Claude Code.

StageWhat happens
1. DraftDescribe what you want the skill to do — Claude writes a first version of the SKILL.md.
2. TestWrite a few test prompts and run Claude against them to see how the skill behaves.
3. EvaluateReview results qualitatively (does it do the right thing?) and quantitatively (pass/fail metrics).
4. IterateRewrite the skill based on what the evals revealed. Repeat until satisfied.
5. OptimiseRun the description improver script to make the skill trigger more reliably.
i Invoke it by telling Claude what skill you want to build: "I want to make a skill for reviewing FastAPI routes." Claude handles the rest.
04

Installing more skills

Same command, different skill name. Browse available skills at the Anthropic skills repo.

zsh
➜ npx skills add anthropics/skills --skill <skill-name> -g # list all available skills ➜ ls ~/.agents/skills/

Skills — set up.
skill-creator installed · ~/.agents/skills/ · symlinked into Claude Code

~/.agents/skills/
➜ skill-creator
Agentic Tooling

Lavish

HTML is the new Markdown. Lavish turns agent-generated HTML artifacts into interactive review surfaces — annotate elements, queue prompts, and send feedback back to the agent without leaving the browser.

01

Install via npm

Installed globally so it's available in every project. Requires Node 22+ — upgrade Node first, then install lavish.

zsh
➜ npm install -g lavish-axi added 84 packages in 3s ➜ lavish-axi --version 0.1.35
i Node version order matters. Always upgrade Node first, then install lavish — global npm packages don't carry across nvm versions. After switching Node, reinstall with npm install -g lavish-axi.
zsh — upgrade Node first
➜ nvm install 22 && nvm use 22 && nvm alias default 22 Now using node v22.23.1 (npm v10.9.8) default -> 22 (-> v22.23.1 *) ➜ npm install -g lavish-axi added 84 packages in 3s ➜ lavish-axi --version 0.1.35
02

How it fits into the agentic workflow

Instead of reading a wall of text, Lavish opens the agent's response as a live webpage you can annotate and reply from.

CommandWhat it does
lavish-axi <file.html>Serves the HTML locally and opens it in the browser for review
lavish-axi poll <file.html>Waits silently for your feedback or annotations from the browser
lavish-axi end <file.html>Agent ends the session — can be reopened later
lavish-axi export <file.html>Writes a portable self-contained HTML file with all assets inlined
lavish-axi share <file.html>Publishes to ht-ml.app and returns a public URL
03

When the agent uses Lavish

This preference is written into ~/AGENTS.md so every agent session picks it up automatically.

✓ Default behaviour: whenever you ask to plan, discuss options, or review trade-offs, the agent creates an HTML artifact and opens it in Lavish — no need to ask. Say "no lavish" or ask for a Markdown file to get plain text instead.
~/AGENTS.md — Planning and Discussion section
## Planning and Discussion When asked to plan, discuss options, explain trade-offs, or present anything complex, use lavish as the first choice. Create an HTML artifact and open it with `lavish-axi <file>` so Manjesh can visually review and annotate it. Only skip lavish if Manjesh explicitly asks for a Markdown file or says "no lavish".
04

Artifact location

By default Lavish creates artifacts under .lavish/ in the current working directory. Assets referenced by the HTML must be in the same folder — use relative paths, never absolute.

project layout
.lavish/ └── <artifact-name>.html # agent-generated artifact
05

References

Official docs and source. axi.md is the canonical reference — all playbooks, commands, and design guidance live there.

ResourceWhat's there
axi.mdOfficial docs — playbooks, commands, design guidance, full lavish-axi reference
github/lavish-axiSource code, releases, issue tracker
Kun Chen — YouTubeAgentic engineering workflow video this entire setup is based on (ex-Meta L8)

Lavish — installed.
v0.1.35 · HTML artifacts · annotate in browser · feedback to agent

lavish-axi
➜ lavish-axi plan.html
Agentic Tooling

No Mistakes

A local git proxy that sits in front of your real remote. Push to no-mistakes instead of origin and it runs an AI-driven review-test-lint pipeline in a disposable worktree, then opens a clean PR only after every check passes. Nothing reaches the real remote until the gate is green.

01

Install no-mistakes

One-line installer. Puts the binary at ~/.local/bin/no-mistakes and symlinks it from ~/.no-mistakes/bin/.

zsh
➜ curl -fsSL https://raw.githubusercontent.com/kunchenguid/no-mistakes/main/docs/install.sh | sh Downloading no-mistakes v1.31.2 for darwin/arm64... no-mistakes v1.31.2 installed to /Users/you/.no-mistakes/bin/no-mistakes Command path: /Users/you/.local/bin/no-mistakes -> /Users/you/.no-mistakes/bin/no-mistakes

Verify the install:

zsh
➜ no-mistakes --version no-mistakes version v1.31.2
02

Initialize a repo (per project)

Run this once inside each repo you want to gate. It adds a no-mistakes git remote pointing to a local bare repo and installs the /no-mistakes skill for Claude Code at user level.

zsh
➜ no-mistakes init ✓ Gate initialized repo /Users/you/src/my-repo gate no-mistakes → /Users/you/.no-mistakes/repos/abc123def456.git remote git@github.com:you/my-repo.git skill /no-mistakes installed for agents at user level Push through the gate with: git push no-mistakes <branch>

For fork-based repos (push to your fork, PR targets upstream):

zsh
➜ no-mistakes init --fork-url git@github.com:manjesh-raj/repo-name.git
03

Three ways to trigger the gate

Command When to use
git push no-mistakes Already have a committed branch - push through the gate instead of origin
no-mistakes Opens the TUI wizard - confirms branch, commit, then push. Add -y to skip all prompts
/no-mistakes <task> Agent skill - tell Claude Code to do a task and gate it, or bare /no-mistakes to gate existing committed work

When the TUI appears, it shows the current branch and commit state, then asks to confirm the push. Use these keys:

TUI
┌ Setup ──────────────────────────────────────┐ - Branch already on deploy_engine_optimize_code - Commit no uncommitted changes ❙ Push push branch to no-mistakes gate? └─────────────────────────────────────────────┘ y push confirm — start the pipeline n skip cancel this step, continue setup q quit exit the TUI entirely

Press y to push through the gate and start the review pipeline.

04

How the pipeline works

Every push runs through the same pipeline in an isolated worktree. Your working tree is never touched while the pipeline is running.

pipeline
your branch │ git push no-mistakes ▼ ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────┐ │ disposable worktree — your work stays put │ │ review → test → docs → lint → push → PR → CI │ └──────────────────────────────────────────────┘ │ every check green ▼ clean PR, opened for you

Each step produces a finding. Safe mechanical fixes are applied automatically. Anything touching intent is escalated - approve, fix, or skip. Nothing reaches the real remote until every check is green.

05

References

  • Full documentation - kunchenguid.github.io/no-mistakes
  • Upstream - github.com/kunchenguid/no-mistakes
  • Backup fork - github.com/manjesh-raj/no-mistakes
Agentic Tooling

Firstmate

Talk to one agent, ship with a crew. Firstmate turns Claude Code into a fleet orchestrator — you give instructions to a single "first mate" agent, which spawns autonomous crewmates in isolated tmux windows and git worktrees, supervises them to completion, and hands you finished PRs or investigation reports.

01

Prerequisites

Firstmate is not a binary — it is a git repo that you run Claude Code inside. All requirements are already met from earlier panels in this guide:

  • tmux — Terminal Setup panel
  • Claude Code — Claude Code panel
  • GitHub CLI authenticated — used for no-mistakes init

Firstmate also needs four companion tools from the AXI ecosystem. On first launch it detects what is missing and prompts you — select Install all (Recommended). Steps 02–05 below show what each tool does and how to install manually if needed.

02

Install treehouse — git worktree pool

Each crewmate gets its own isolated git worktree with dependencies and build cache intact. No re-cloning between tasks, no parallel work conflicts.

install
➜ curl -fsSL https://kunchenguid.github.io/treehouse/install.sh | sh

Drop into an isolated worktree for any repo — type exit to return and release it back to the pool:

usage
➜ cd ~/your-project && treehouse 🌳 Entered worktree at ~/.treehouse/myproject-a1b2c3/1/myproject. Type 'exit' to return. # run your agent or do work here — fully isolated from main checkout ➜ exit 🌳 Worktree returned to pool.
03

Install gh-axi — GitHub CLI for agents

Wraps gh with token-efficient TOON output and contextual next-step hints. Benchmark: 100% task success vs 86% for raw gh, at lower cost. Installed as a skill — agents load it automatically for any GitHub task.

install skill
➜ npx skills add kunchenguid/gh-axi --skill gh-axi -g # -g installs globally in ~/.claude/skills/ for all projects

Agents use it automatically. You can also call it directly:

usage
➜ npx -y gh-axi pr list prs[2]{number,title,state,author}: 42,fix flaky login test,open,fm-crewmate-k3 41,add dark mode,merged,fm-crewmate-p7
04

Install tasks-axi — backlog manager for agents

Edits a human-readable backlog.md in place at near-zero output-token cost. No markdown regeneration through the model — agents load it automatically for any task or backlog work.

install skill
➜ npx skills add kunchenguid/tasks-axi --skill tasks-axi -g

View the current backlog dashboard:

usage
➜ npx -y tasks-axi summary: queued: 14 ready: 13 in_flight[1]{id,title,kind}: fm-task-k3,fix flaky login test,ship
05

Install chrome-devtools-axi — browser automation for agents

Wraps chrome-devtools-mcp with AXI output — 57% fewer input tokens and 26% lower cost vs raw MCP. Agents load it automatically when a task needs a real browser.

install skill
➜ npx skills add kunchenguid/chrome-devtools-axi --skill chrome-devtools-axi -g

Agents use it automatically. Direct usage:

usage
➜ npx -y chrome-devtools-axi open https://example.com page: {title: "Example Domain", url: "https://example.com"} snapshot: heading "Example Domain" uid=g1:1 link "More information..." help[1]: Run `chrome-devtools-axi click @g1:1` to click the link
06

Clone the repo

Clone once to a permanent home. The repo itself is the orchestrator — do not delete it between sessions.

zsh
➜ git clone https://github.com/kunchenguid/firstmate ~/manjesh/firstmate Cloning into '/Users/you/manjesh/firstmate'...

Already cloned at ~/manjesh/firstmate — pointing to manjesh-raj/firstmate (backup fork).

07

Launch firstmate

Always launch from inside a tmux session so crewmate windows land in your visible session. Then cd into the firstmate repo and run Claude Code — AGENTS.md takes over automatically.

zsh
➜ cd ~/manjesh/firstmate && claude ⚓ firstmate ready — talk to me, captain
08

Give orders — firstmate dispatches the crew

You talk only to firstmate. It spawns crewmates in their own tmux windows and git worktrees, supervises them, and reports back when done.

firstmate
> fix the flaky login test in sre_sentinel and investigate why deploys are slow Spawning fm-fix-login-k3 (ship task → sre_sentinel) Spawning fm-deploy-scout-p7 (scout task → deploy_engine) PR ready for review, captain: https://bitbucket.org/pramata/sre_sentinel/pull-requests/42 Report at data/fm-deploy-scout-p7/report.md

Two task shapes — ship delivers a code change (PR or local merge), scout investigates and leaves a report at data/<id>/report.md.

09

Built-in skills

Skill What it does
/afk Away mode — sub-supervisor self-handles routine events, escalates only what needs you as a batched digest
/updatefirstmate Self-update firstmate and all secondmates to latest from origin, then re-read instructions
/stow Sweep session for uncaptured knowledge, route to disk, file undone steps to backlog — safe to reset after
10

References

  • Upstream - github.com/kunchenguid/firstmate
  • Backup fork - github.com/manjesh-raj/firstmate
  • Architecture docs
  • Configuration docs
Agentic Tooling

Firstmate Cockpit

A native macOS desktop app that gives your Firstmate fleet a cockpit. It shows a live dashboard of the crew — who is working, what needs a decision, which PRs are ready — and embeds the first mate's tmux session as a real, interactive terminal, so you can steer the whole fleet without hunting through terminal tabs. Built with FastAPI + pywebview (no Rust), packaged into a real Firstmate.app. Firstmate itself is never modified — the cockpit only reads it and drives its own bin/ helper scripts.

01

What you get

  • Fleet dashboard — crew status, backlog, projects, PRs ready to merge, and watcher health, at a glance.
  • First Mate console — an embedded live terminal (the real tmux session) with full scroll, arrow-menus, and permission prompts, plus cleaned Transcript and Conversation views.
  • One-click actions — message the first mate, merge a PR, answer a prompt.
  • Light / dark themes, with the captain's identity front and centre.
02

Prerequisites

You need a first mate to point it at, and Python 3.12. tmux and Claude Code are covered by earlier panels; Firstmate is the previous panel.

  • A first mate running inside tmux (see the Firstmate panel) — the cockpit talks to it through its tmux pane, so a plain terminal session cannot be reached.
  • Python 3.12 — brew install python@3.12.
03

Clone and run (dev)

Clone the repo, create a virtualenv, install, and start the local server.

setup
➜ git clone https://github.com/manjesh-raj/firstmate-cockpit ➜ cd firstmate-cockpit ➜ python3.12 -m venv .venv && .venv/bin/pip install -e . ➜ .venv/bin/python -m uvicorn backend.app:app --port 8765 # then open http://127.0.0.1:8765 — reading the fleet works with zero config
04

Build the macOS app

Package it into a real Firstmate.app — pywebview for the native window, py2app for the bundle. No Rust, no extra toolchain.

build
➜ ./build_app.sh ✓ Built: dist/Firstmate.app ➜ open dist/Firstmate.app # first launch: right-click → Open (unsigned app, one-time Gatekeeper prompt)
05

Connect to your first mate

Open the console (click Captain in the top bar), hit the gear → Detect, and pick the pane flagged "firstmate home" — or set FIRSTMATE_TARGET yourself. The choice persists to ~/Library/Application Support/firstmate-cockpit/config.json. tmux window indexes vary by base-index, so Detect finds the real target (often firstmate:1.1) rather than you guessing.

optional
➜ export FIRSTMATE_TARGET=firstmate:1.1 # or just use the in-app gear → Detect (recommended)

Now the Terminal tab mirrors your live first mate — scroll, arrow-menus, and prompts, all fully interactive, reflected in your real terminal because it is the same tmux session.

06

Source

Full code, README (API surface, py2app notes, configuration), and design docs live on GitHub: github.com/manjesh-raj/firstmate-cockpit.

Agentic Tooling

Keeping Tools Updated

The setup panels install the kunchenguid crew — firstmate and its companion CLIs. This panel keeps them current. Every tool lives at github.com/kunchenguid/<name>, and none of these updates need sudo (the Homebrew node prefix and the binary install dirs are all user-writable). Each tool below shows how to check whether it is behind and the exact command to bring it current — update only what has actually fallen behind.

01

firstmate — git repo, self-update

The firstmate home is a git clone (origin is your personal fork manjesh-raj/firstmate). The preferred update is the built-in command, which fast-forwards this repo plus any secondmate homes and re-reads its instructions.

preferred
➜ /updatefirstmate # fast-forwards the firstmate home + secondmate homes, then re-reads instructions

If it has diverged (you have local commits, so a plain fast-forward is refused), merge from upstream instead:

diverged
➜ git -C <firstmate-home> remote add upstream https://github.com/kunchenguid/firstmate # once; ignore "already exists" ➜ git -C <firstmate-home> fetch upstream ➜ git -C <firstmate-home> merge --no-edit upstream/main

Check what's behind: compare local HEAD against upstream main.

check
➜ git -C <firstmate-home> log -1 # installed ➜ git ls-remote https://github.com/kunchenguid/firstmate main # latest
! Never run git clean. projects/, data/, config/, and state/ are gitignored, so a fetch/merge never touches your cloned projects or local fleet state — but git clean would delete exactly those ignored dirs. After updating, re-read AGENTS.md; the operating instructions may have changed. Optionally mirror to your fork with git -C <firstmate-home> push origin main (a clean fast-forward, no force needed).
02

axi CLIs — global npm packages

Four CLIs — gh-axi, chrome-devtools-axi, tasks-axi, and lavish-axi — are published to npm from the axi monorepo. Update them all in one command:

update
➜ npm install -g gh-axi@latest chrome-devtools-axi@latest tasks-axi@latest lavish-axi@latest

Check what's behind: compare the published version against your installed one for any package.

check
➜ npm view <pkg> version # latest published ➜ npm ls -g --depth=0 | grep axi # installed
i lavish-axi needs a restart to activate. The npm install does not disturb running review sessions, but the new version only goes live once the lavish server restarts: run lavish-axi stop (it relaunches on next use). That drops live review connections, so do it when you are not mid-review.
03

no-mistakes — self-contained binary

Installed as a binary in ~/.no-mistakes/bin, it ships its own updater. No npm, no reinstall script.

update
➜ no-mistakes update

Check what's behind: no-mistakes --version also prints an "update available" banner when it is behind.

check
➜ no-mistakes --version
! Resets the daemon. no-mistakes update also restarts the no-mistakes daemon — only run it when no validation pipeline is in flight, or you will kill a run in progress.
04

treehouse — release binary

Installed as a binary at /usr/local/bin/treehouse. It is not distributed via npm and has no self-updater — it is release-based, so you compare your version against the repo's latest release tag and reinstall the latest release binary when behind.

check
➜ treehouse --version # installed ➜ git ls-remote --tags --sort=-v:refname https://github.com/kunchenguid/treehouse | head -1 # latest release tag

When behind, install the latest release binary from the repo's releases page (or its install script).

05

gnhf — global npm CLI

Not installed by default. The same npm install -g both installs it fresh and updates an existing copy.

install / update
➜ npm install -g gnhf@latest

Check what's behind: same npm pattern as the axi CLIs.

check
➜ npm view gnhf version # latest ➜ npm ls -g --depth=0 | grep gnhf # installed
06

Safety checklist

Before running any of the above, keep these in mind:

  • firstmate: projects/, data/, config/, state/ are gitignored and must stay untouched — never git clean. Re-read AGENTS.md after updating.
  • lavish-axi: schedule the lavish-axi stop restart for a moment when no review is in progress — it drops live sessions.
  • no-mistakes: run no-mistakes update only when no validation is in flight; it resets the daemon.
  • No sudo: nothing here needs it. If a global install reports a permission error, stop and fix the npm prefix rather than forcing it.
  • Update only what is actually behind, and note the before/after version of anything you touch.

🎯 Key Takeaways

  • Every tool lives at github.com/kunchenguid/<name>; none need sudo.
  • npm CLIs (axi tools, gnhf): npm view <pkg> version to check, npm install -g <pkg>@latest to update.
  • Binaries: no-mistakes update self-updates; treehouse is reinstalled from its latest release tag.
  • firstmate: prefer /updatefirstmate; fall back to an upstream fetch + merge if diverged.
  • Mind the three side effects — the firstmate gitignore protection, the lavish restart timing, and the no-mistakes daemon reset.

Module 01

Introduction to Artificial Intelligence

Let's start from scratch — no buzzwords, just clarity

👶 ELI5

Imagine teaching a child to recognise dogs. You show them 1,000 photos and say "that's a dog." After enough photos they start spotting dogs everywhere — even in shapes they've never seen before. AI does the same thing, with a computer and millions of examples.

🔄 Traditional Programming

You write every rule by hand:

if cpu > 90:
  alert("High CPU")

Rules break when conditions change

🤖 AI Programming

AI learns the rules from data:

model.fit(metrics, labels)
model.predict(new_metrics)

Adapts as patterns change

⏱️ Evolution of AI

1950sRule-Based AI

Explicit if-then rules; expert systems

1980sMachine Learning

Algorithms learn from data

2010sDeep Learning

Neural networks — image recognition, speech

2023+Generative AI & Agents

LLMs, autonomous agents, tool use

🎯

Narrow AI

Specialised for one task. Spam filter, face recognition, chess bots.

Where we are today
🧠

General AI (AGI)

Can do any intellectual task a human can. Still theoretical.

Future goal
📈

Machine Learning

Subset of AI. Finds patterns in data without explicit rules.

Foundation of AI
🌊

Deep Learning

Multi-layer neural networks. Powers vision, language, audio.

Powers LLMs
🔧 DevOps/SRE Analogy

Think of the AI evolution as your monitoring stack evolution:

📋
Manual Runbook

if CPU > 90% → alert (Rule-based AI)

→
📊
ML Anomaly Detection

Learns normal baseline, flags outliers (ML)

→
🤖
AI Agent

Detects, diagnoses, auto-remediates (Agents)

🎯 Key Takeaways

  • AI learns patterns from data instead of following hand-coded rules
  • ML ⊂ AI; Deep Learning ⊂ ML — they are nested subsets
  • We're in the era of Narrow AI → Generative AI → Autonomous Agents
  • For DevOps: AI augments your automation — it doesn't replace your expertise
Module 02

Understanding Large Language Models

The brain behind ChatGPT, Claude, Gemini — and your future AI tools

👶 ELI5

Imagine reading every book, article, StackOverflow post and GitHub repo ever written. After that you'd be incredible at predicting what word comes next in any sentence. That's essentially what an LLM does — trained on massive text, it predicts the next token, over and over, until it produces an answer.

🏗️ How an LLM Processes Your Prompt

⌨️
Your Prompt"Fix the crash"
→
✂️
Tokenise["Fix","the","crash"]
→
🔢
Embed[0.2, 0.8, 0.1…]
→
👁️
AttentionContext understood
→
💬
Generate"Restart the pod…"

🎯 Interactive: Next-Token Prediction

See how LLMs pick the next word — click the most likely token:

The Kubernetes pod is in |
📖

Pre-training

Trained on billions of text documents from the internet, books, code.

Learns language
🎯

Fine-tuning

Specialised on curated datasets for a specific domain or behaviour.

Specialises
⚖️

RLHF

Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback — makes it helpful, honest.

Aligns values
🚀

Inference

The model you use via API. Takes prompt → generates tokens → returns response.

What you call

⚠️ LLM Limitations (And How to Fix Them)

📅Knowledge Cutoff

Doesn't know events after training date

Fix: RAG
🔮Hallucination

Confidently states false facts

Fix: RAG + validation
📦Context Limit

Can only "see" limited text at once

Fix: chunking + memory
🔒No Live Data

Cannot query APIs or databases

Fix: Agents + tools

🎯 Key Takeaways

  • LLMs predict the next token based on patterns learned from massive text data
  • They are powerful text generators but have real limitations
  • RAG (Module 05) fixes the "no live data" and "knowledge cutoff" problems
  • For DevOps: LLMs can read logs, write runbooks, generate Terraform, explain Helm charts
💰 Real WorldCost Awareness

Tokens, Cost & API Reality — What Nobody Tells You

Before you build anything, understand what you're actually paying for.

What is a token?

# ~1 token ≈ 4 characters ≈ ¾ of a word # "CrashLoopBackOff" = 4–5 tokens # 1,000 tokens ≈ 750 words ≈ 1.5 pages # What you send to the API: { "messages": [ {"role":"system", "content":"You are an SRE..."}, {"role":"user", "content":"Why is my pod failing?"} ] } # Tokens counted: system prompt + user msg # + model response # = total billed tokens

Rough cost guide (GPT-4o, Mar 2026)

Use caseTokens/callCost
Simple Q&A~500~$0.001
Log analysis~3,000~$0.006
ReAct agent (5 steps)~8,000~$0.016
RAG with docs~5,000~$0.010
Multi-agent RCA~25,000~$0.050

💡 DevOps tip: 1000 incidents/day × $0.016 = $16/day = $480/month. Cheaper than one on-call engineer's overnight shift. Always add token tracking to your production agents.

# Track tokens in your LangGraph agents response = llm.invoke(messages) print(f"Tokens used: {response.usage_metadata}") # → {'input_tokens': 1240, 'output_tokens': 380, 'total_tokens': 1620} # Context window limits (how much you can send in one call): # GPT-4o: 128,000 tokens (~300 pages of text) # Claude 3.5: 200,000 tokens (~450 pages of text) # Gemini 1.5: 1,000,000 tokens — 1 MILLION tokens # → Paste entire log files, entire codebases directly into the prompt
Module 03

AI Agents — Deep Dive

From simple loops to self-correcting, planning, multi-step reasoning agents

🟢 Beginner → 🔵 Conceptual → 🟠 Practical → 🔴 Architecture
👶 ELI5

A regular LLM is a brilliant person in a sealed room — they can answer questions but can't act. An AI Agent is the same person with a phone, a laptop, and the ability to use tools. They can search the web, run bash commands, call Kubernetes APIs, read log files, and send Slack messages. They accomplish goals, not just answer questions — and they loop until the task is done.

🧱 What Makes an Agent?

🧠LLM

The reasoning engine — decides what to do next

+
🔨Tools

Functions the LLM can call to take real actions

+
💾Memory

Keeps track of previous steps and context

+
🎯Goal

The task to accomplish — drives the loop

=
🤖Agent

Autonomous problem solver

🏗️ Four Major Agent Architectures

Each architecture is suited for different complexity levels and tasks:

ReAct

Reasoning + Acting — The Foundation

ReAct interleaves reasoning traces (thinking out loud) with tool actions. The LLM writes its thought process, picks a tool, gets the result, thinks again. This loop continues until the answer is found. It's the most common agent pattern.

💭Thought

LLM reasons about what to do next

→
⚡Action

Calls a tool with specific input

→
👀Observation

Receives tool output back

↺ repeat
📜 Real ReAct Trace (K8s Incident)
💭 Thought: I need to investigate why the api-server pod is restarting. Let me check its logs first.
⚡ Action: run_kubectl("logs api-server-9xf2k --previous -n production")
👀 Observation: "FATAL: Killed process 1 (java) total-vm:2097152kB, anon-rss:256120kB — OOMKilled"
💭 Thought: The pod was OOMKilled. Memory limit is too low. Let me check what the current limit is.
⚡ Action: run_kubectl("get pod api-server-9xf2k -o jsonpath='{.spec.containers[0].resources}'")
👀 Observation: {"limits": {"memory": "256Mi"}, "requests": {"memory": "128Mi"}}
💭 Thought: Memory limit is 256Mi but process was using 256MB+ before being killed. Need to increase limit to at least 512Mi. I'll patch the deployment.
⚡ Action: patch_deployment("api-server", {"memory_limit": "512Mi"})
👀 Observation: "deployment.apps/api-server patched successfully. Rollout in progress."
💭 Thought: Fix applied. I should verify the pod is now running and create a ticket.
⚡ Action: create_ticket("OOMKilled: api-server memory limit increased 256Mi→512Mi. Root cause: JVM heap growth.")
✅ Final Answer: Root cause identified: OOMKilled due to insufficient memory limit (256Mi). Applied fix: increased to 512Mi. Pod is now Running. Ticket PLAT-2849 created.
Use ReAct when: You have a clear goal, the task needs tool use, and the steps aren't fully predictable in advance. Limitation: Can get stuck in loops or make mistakes without self-correction.
Reflection

Generate → Critique → Revise Loop

A Reflection agent has two roles in one (or two separate LLM calls): a Generator that produces output, and a Critic that evaluates and suggests improvements. The loop runs until the output meets quality standards.

✍️Generate

Produce initial output

→
🔍Critique

Self-evaluate quality

→
✏️Revise

Improve based on critique

↺ until quality met
📜 Reflection Agent: Runbook Writer
✍️ Generate (attempt 1): "If pod crashes, restart it."
🔍 Critique: This runbook is too vague. Missing: how to diagnose the crash reason, specific kubectl commands, escalation path, monitoring steps. Grade: 3/10
✏️ Revise (attempt 2): Added kubectl commands, error type matrix, escalation steps...
🔍 Critique: Better. Still missing: rollback procedure, impact assessment, post-mortem template. Grade: 7/10
✏️ Revise (attempt 3): Full runbook with all sections...
🔍 Critique: Comprehensive. All sections present. Clear commands. Good escalation. Grade: 9/10 — acceptable.
✅ Output accepted after 3 reflection cycles. Quality score: 9/10
Use Reflection when: Output quality matters more than speed. Content generation, code review, documentation writing. DevOps use: Auto-generating runbooks, post-mortems, architecture decision records (ADRs).
Reflexion

Reflection + External Grounding + Memory

Reflexion extends Reflection by adding external evaluation (running tests, checking metrics) and episodic memory — the agent remembers what failed before and avoids repeating mistakes across multiple attempts.

🎭Actor

Generates response using memory of past failures

→
🧪Evaluator

Tests output externally (runs tests, checks accuracy)

→
💡Self-Reflect

Analyses failure, writes lesson to memory

↺ retry with memory
📜 Reflexion Agent: Terraform Fix
🎭 Actor (attempt 1): Writes Terraform to create S3 bucket with wrong region format
🧪 Evaluator: terraform validate → Error: "region" must be string like "us-east-1", not "us-east". Test FAILED.
💡 Self-Reflect: I used wrong region format. Lesson stored in memory: "AWS region must include full name e.g. us-east-1 not us-east"
🎭 Actor (attempt 2, with memory): Rewrites Terraform with correct region — also recalls another memory: "S3 bucket names must be globally unique"
🧪 Evaluator: terraform validate → ✅ Success. terraform plan → ✅ Creates 1 resource.
✅ Passed after 2 attempts. Memory updated with 2 lessons for future use.
Use Reflexion when: Tasks have external feedback (tests, compilers, APIs). Agent needs to learn across attempts. DevOps use: IaC generation with validation, script writing with test suites, config generation.
Plan-and-Execute

Think First, Then Act — More Efficient Than ReAct

Instead of deciding the next action one step at a time (like ReAct), a Plan-and-Execute agent creates a full plan upfront using a Planner LLM, then executes each step. A Replanner re-evaluates if something unexpected happens.

📋Planner

Generate full multi-step plan from goal

→
⚡Executor

Run each step of the plan

→
🔄Replanner

Adapt plan if step failed or new info found

📜 Plan-and-Execute: Cloud Cost Investigation
📋 Planner: Goal is "reduce AWS costs by 20%". My plan: Step 1: Get cost breakdown by service (Cost Explorer API) Step 2: Identify top 3 cost drivers Step 3: For each: analyse usage patterns (CloudWatch) Step 4: Generate specific optimisation recommendations Step 5: Create Terraform PR with changes
⚡ Execute Step 1: cost_explorer.get_cost(last_30_days) → EC2: $12k, RDS: $3k, S3: $900
⚡ Execute Step 2: Top 3: EC2 (72%), RDS (18%), S3 (5%)
⚡ Execute Step 3: CloudWatch CPU avg → 14 instances under 5% utilisation
🔄 Replanner: Step 3 found 14 idle instances (unexpected). Updating plan: Add step 3b — verify instances aren't needed for scheduled jobs before marking for termination.
⚡ Execute Step 3b: Check CloudWatch scheduled tasks → 2 instances needed for nightly backups, 12 can be terminated
✅ Plan complete with one replan event. Savings identified: $4,200/month. Terraform PR created.
Use Plan-and-Execute when: Tasks are long, complex, and have many steps. Better cost efficiency vs ReAct — Planner uses expensive model once, Executor uses cheaper model for each step. DevOps use: Multi-phase incident investigation, complex change management workflows.

🔄 Live ReAct Agent — Animated Demo

Watch the Think → Act → Observe loop execute on a K8s incident:

🧠ThinkReason about next step
→
⚡ActCall a tool
→
👀ObserveProcess result

Click "Run Agent" to watch a DevOps incident agent think through a real problem…

⚖️ When to Use Which Agent Architecture

ArchitectureComplexitySelf-CorrectionBest ForDevOps Example
ReActMedium❌ NoneTool-using tasksIncident investigation
ReflectionMedium✅ Self-critiqueContent qualityRunbook generation
ReflexionHigh✅ With memoryCode/config genTerraform writing
Plan-and-ExecuteHigh✅ ReplannerLong multi-step tasksCost optimisation

🎯 Key Takeaways

  • Agent = LLM + Tools + Memory + Goal — it loops until the task is done
  • ReAct: Most common. Interleaves reasoning with tool calls. Good starting point.
  • Reflection / Reflexion: Self-correcting. Adds critique and revision loops for quality output.
  • Plan-and-Execute: Efficient for complex tasks. Plans upfront, executes cheaply, replans on surprises.
  • For DevOps: Start with ReAct agents, use Plan-and-Execute for multi-phase investigations
🚨 Failure ModesWhat Nobody Shows You

When Agents Go Wrong — And How to Handle It

Real agents fail constantly. Here's every failure mode and the production fix.

Failure ModeWhat HappensProduction Fix
Infinite loop Agent keeps calling the same tool, never reaches a conclusion Set recursion_limit=25 in LangGraph — raises GraphRecursionError after N steps
Tool error kubectl returns non-zero exit, Prometheus returns 500 — agent sees raw error Wrap tools with try/except, return structured error: {"error": "pod not found", "suggestion": "check namespace"} — LLM can reason on it
Hallucination LLM invents a pod name, namespace, or metric that doesn't exist Always validate tool input against real K8s resources before executing. Add a Reflexion critique node.
Wrong tool chosen Agent calls restart_deployment when it should have called get_logs first Add Human-in-the-Loop approval gate before any destructive action. Use LangGraph interrupt().
Context window overflow Long log files + tool history fills the 128K context — API returns 400 error Summarise tool outputs before adding to state. Use trim_messages() to prune old history.
Rate limiting 100 parallel incidents hit OpenAI API limit — 429 Too Many Requests Add exponential backoff + jitter. Use semaphore to cap concurrent agent threads.
# Safe tool wrapper pattern — every tool should look like this @tool def get_pod_logs(pod_name: str, namespace: str = "default") -> str: """Get the last 50 lines of logs from a Kubernetes pod.""" try: result = subprocess.run( ["kubectl", "logs", pod_name, "-n", namespace, "--tail=50"], capture_output=True, text=True, timeout=10 ) if result.returncode != 0: # Return structured error — LLM reasons on this, not a raw exception return f"ERROR: {result.stderr}. Try: kubectl get pods -n {namespace}" return result.stdout[:4000] # trim to avoid context overflow except subprocess.TimeoutExpired: return "ERROR: kubectl timed out after 10s. Cluster may be unreachable."
Module 03.5

Tools & Tool Calling

The hands of an AI agent — how LLMs take real actions in the world

🟢 Beginner → 🔵 Conceptual → 🟠 Practical → 🔴 Architecture
👶 ELI5

A tool is just a Python function the AI is allowed to call. The AI sees the function's name, description, and what parameters it takes — but not the code. When it decides to use a tool, it says "call query_prometheus with argument 'cpu_usage > 90'". Your system runs the function and sends the result back to the AI.

🔩 Anatomy of a Tool — The Schema

This is what the LLM actually sees for each tool (JSON Schema):

PythonDefine a Tool
from langchain_core.tools import tool

@tool
def query_prometheus(query: str) -> str:
    """Query Prometheus metrics API.

    Args:
        query: A valid PromQL expression
    Returns:
        The metric result as a string
    """
    result = prometheus.query(query)
    return str(result)
JSON SchemaWhat the LLM sees
{
  "name": "query_prometheus",
  "description": "Query Prometheus metrics API.
    Args: query: A valid PromQL expression
    Returns: The metric result",
  "parameters": {
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "query": {
        "type": "string",
        "description": "A valid PromQL expression"
      }
    },
    "required": ["query"]
  }
}
💡 The description is critical — it's how the LLM decides when to use this tool. Write clear, specific descriptions with examples of when to use the tool.

🔄 The Tool Calling Flow — Step by Step

1
👤
User Message

"Why is the API slow?"

↓
2
🧠
LLM + Tools

Receives message + JSON schema of all available tools

↓
3
🎯
LLM Decides

Output is a tool_call object, NOT plain text:
{"name": "query_prometheus", "args": {"query": "api_latency_p99"}}

↓
4
⚙️
System Executes

Your code runs the function, gets real result

↓
5
📤
Result → LLM

Tool result appended to message history as ToolMessage

↓
6
💬
LLM Responds

Uses tool result to generate final natural language answer

🔧 Essential DevOps Agent Tools

📊query_prometheus

Run PromQL queries to get metrics, check thresholds, compare time windows

query_prometheus("rate(http_errors[5m])")
☸️run_kubectl

Execute kubectl commands: get pods, describe, logs, exec, patch, rollout

run_kubectl("get pods -n prod -o wide")
🔍search_logs

Query Elasticsearch/Loki for log patterns, error messages, stack traces

search_logs("OOMKilled", "2024-01-01", "prod")
📋create_jira_ticket

Create incident tickets with structured fields, severity, assignee, labels

create_jira("P1: DB connection pool", "OPS")
💬notify_slack

Post messages to Slack channels with formatted incident summaries

notify_slack("#incidents", "🚨 P1: ...")
📚search_runbooks

RAG search over your runbook knowledge base for relevant procedures

search_runbooks("CrashLoopBackOff fix")
🔄trigger_pipeline

Trigger GitHub Actions / Jenkins pipeline with specific parameters

trigger_pipeline("rollback", {"version": "v1.2.3"})
💰get_cost_data

Query AWS Cost Explorer or GCP Billing for spend breakdown and trends

get_cost_data("EC2", "last_30_days")

⚙️ Binding Tools to an LLM (LangChain)

Once you define tools, you bind them to the LLM. The LLM then knows which tools are available:

from langchain_openai import ChatOpenAI
from langchain_core.tools import tool

# 1. Define your DevOps tools
@tool
def query_prometheus(query: str) -> str:
    """Run a PromQL query against the Prometheus metrics server."""
    return prometheus_api.query(query)

@tool
def run_kubectl(command: str) -> str:
    """Execute a kubectl command. Always use --dry-run for destructive operations."""
    return subprocess.run(["kubectl"] + command.split(), capture_output=True).stdout

@tool
def search_runbooks(query: str) -> str:
    """Semantic search over internal runbooks and playbooks."""
    return vector_store.similarity_search(query, k=3)

# 2. Bind tools to LLM — now LLM knows these exist
llm = ChatOpenAI(model="gpt-4o", temperature=0)
llm_with_tools = llm.bind_tools([query_prometheus, run_kubectl, search_runbooks])

# 3. LLM can now decide to call any of these tools
response = llm_with_tools.invoke([
    HumanMessage("The api-server pod is crashing. Diagnose the issue.")
])

# response.tool_calls will contain the tool the LLM chose to invoke
print(response.tool_calls)
# [{"name": "run_kubectl", "args": {"command": "logs api-server --previous"}, "id": "..."}]

🎮 Interactive: Which Tool Would the LLM Pick?

Given a DevOps problem, watch the LLM reason about which tool to call:

Select a Problem:

Available Tools:

🎯 Key Takeaways

  • A tool is a Python function decorated with @tool — the LLM sees its name, description, and parameters
  • The LLM outputs a tool_call object (not text) when it wants to use a tool — your system executes it
  • Description quality = tool selection quality. Be specific about when and how to use each tool.
  • LangGraph's built-in ToolNode automatically handles tool execution in your workflow
  • For DevOps: Build a library of 8–15 tools covering your entire ops stack — the agent picks the right one
Module 04 ⭐

Multi-Agent AI Systems

Specialist agents collaborating — like a hospital, not a single doctor

👶 ELI5

A hospital doesn't have one doctor who does everything. A cardiologist diagnoses your heart, a surgeon operates, a nurse monitors recovery, and an admin handles billing. Each specialises. Multi-Agent AI works the same way — specialised agents collaborate, each doing what they're best at.

🏗️ Multi-Agent Architecture

🎯OrchestratorRoutes tasks, synthesises results
📋Planner AgentCreates step-by-step plan
⚡Executor AgentRuns commands & APIs
🔍Reviewer AgentValidates output quality
📊Reporter AgentWrites summary reports

💬 Live Simulation: Multi-Agent Incident Response

Watch specialised agents collaborate to resolve a K8s incident:

🚨 INCIDENT: api-deployment — CrashLoopBackOff in production

Click "Start Simulation" to watch agents collaborate…

👑

Supervisor

One supervisor agent delegates tasks to specialised worker agents and synthesises results.

🔗

Pipeline

Agents form a chain — each processes and passes results forward in sequence.

🗳️

Debate/Vote

Multiple agents independently analyse and vote or debate to reach consensus.

🌐

Peer-to-Peer

Agents communicate directly; no central coordinator. Like microservices for AI.

🎯 Key Takeaways

  • Multi-agent systems divide complex problems among specialised agents
  • Agents can run in parallel — dramatically faster than one sequential agent
  • LangGraph (next!) is the best framework for building multi-agent systems
  • For DevOps: Planner + Executor + Reviewer for safe automated remediation

💻 What Multi-Agent Code Actually Looks Like

A supervisor routes to specialist agents — each is its own LangGraph create_react_agent

PythonSpecialist agents
from langchain_openai import ChatOpenAI
from langgraph.prebuilt import create_react_agent

llm = ChatOpenAI(model="gpt-4o-mini")

# Each specialist has its own tools
metrics_agent = create_react_agent(
    llm, tools=[query_prometheus, get_cpu_usage]
)
logs_agent = create_react_agent(
    llm, tools=[get_pod_logs, search_log_patterns]
)
remediation_agent = create_react_agent(
    llm, tools=[restart_deployment, scale_replicas],
    # Only this agent can change things
)
PythonSupervisor routes between them
from langgraph.graph import StateGraph, END

def supervisor(state):
    # LLM decides which agent to call next
    decision = llm.invoke([
        SystemMessage("Route to: metrics_agent, "
                      "logs_agent, remediation_agent, or END"),
        HumanMessage(str(state["messages"]))
    ])
    return {"next": decision.content.strip()}

graph = StateGraph(AgentState)
graph.add_node("supervisor", supervisor)
graph.add_node("metrics",    metrics_agent)
graph.add_node("logs",       logs_agent)
graph.add_node("remediation",remediation_agent)

# Supervisor chooses the next node
graph.add_conditional_edges("supervisor", {
    "metrics_agent":     "metrics",
    "logs_agent":        "logs",
    "remediation_agent": "remediation",
    "END": END
})

💡 Each specialist agent runs its own ReAct loop with its own tools. The supervisor only decides who to call next — it never calls tools directly. This separation keeps each agent focused and debuggable.

Module 05 ⭐

RAG — Retrieval Augmented Generation

Giving AI access to your private knowledge base

👶 ELI5

Imagine an open-book exam vs a closed-book exam. A regular LLM is closed-book — limited to what it memorised during training. RAG gives the AI an open book: your runbooks, logs, architecture docs. It searches them before answering. The result is grounded in your real, current data.

🔄 RAG Pipeline — Animated

📥 Indexing Phase (one-time)
📄

→
✂️

→
🔢

→
🗄️
Vector DB

❓
User Query
→
🔢
Embed Query
→
🎯
Similarity Search
→
🤖
LLM + Context
→
💬
Answer

🎮 Interactive RAG Demo: Log Analysis Assistant

Ask a question. Watch RAG retrieve the right runbook chunk and generate an answer:

📚 Knowledge Base (Runbooks)

📄
Runbook: High CPU

If CPU > 90%: check top processes, look for zombie processes, consider horizontal scaling or VPA adjustments…

📄
Runbook: OOMKilled

Out of Memory: check pod resource limits, identify memory leaks with heap dumps, adjust limits.memory or enable VPA…

📄
Runbook: DB Connection

Connection pool exhausted: check pool.size setting, increase max_connections, consider PgBouncer, scale DB pods…

📄
Runbook: CrashLoopBackOff

Pod restarting: check kubectl logs, events, inspect env variables, resource limits, readiness/liveness probes…
