Helm Module 01

What is Helm?

The package manager for Kubernetes — install, upgrade, rollback in one command

👶 ELI5

Helm is the apt/yum for Kubernetes. Instead of running kubectl apply -f deploy.yaml, kubectl apply -f svc.yaml, kubectl apply -f ingress.yaml... (10+ files), you run helm install myapp ./mychart and Helm handles everything — versioned, rollbackable, configurable.

😤 Without Helm
kubectl apply -f deployment.yaml kubectl apply -f service.yaml kubectl apply -f ingress.yaml kubectl apply -f configmap.yaml kubectl apply -f hpa.yaml # 10+ files to manage manually # No version tracking # No easy rollback
⛵ With Helm
helm install myapp ./mychart \ --values prod.yaml # One command # Versioned release # helm rollback myapp 1
🔥 Real ScenarioPain First

The Problem Helm Solves — 3 Environments

Imagine you're a DevOps engineer deploying myapp to dev, staging, and production. You have 5 Kubernetes files:

😩 Without Helm — 15 files to manage
kubectl-manifests/ ├── dev/ │ ├── deployment.yaml ← replicas: 1, image: v1.3.0 │ ├── service.yaml ← port: 8080 │ ├── ingress.yaml ← dev.myapp.internal │ ├── configmap.yaml ← LOG_LEVEL=debug │ └── hpa.yaml ← disabled ├── staging/ │ ├── deployment.yaml ← replicas: 2, image: v1.3.0 │ ├── ... ← copied & tweaked manually │ └── hpa.yaml ← minReplicas: 2 └── production/ ├── deployment.yaml ← replicas: 5, image: v1.2.9 ├── ... └── hpa.yaml ← minReplicas: 5, max: 20 # Problem: image v1.3.0 has a bug. # You must edit 3 deployment.yamls manually. # Miss one → production still runs buggy version.
✅ With Helm — 1 chart + 3 values files
myapp/ ← ONE chart (templates) ├── Chart.yaml ├── templates/ │ ├── deployment.yaml ← image: {{ .Values.image.tag }} │ ├── service.yaml │ ├── ingress.yaml ← {{ if .Values.ingress.enabled }} │ ├── configmap.yaml │ └── hpa.yaml ← {{ if .Values.autoscaling.enabled }} ├── values.yaml ← base defaults ├── values-dev.yaml ← replicas:1, debug logs, no HPA ├── values-staging.yaml ← replicas:2, HPA min 2 └── values-prod.yaml ← replicas:5, HPA min 5, max 20 # Bug in v1.3.0? Fix in ONE place: values-prod.yaml # image: # tag: v1.2.9 ← rollback all 3 envs in seconds
helm install myapp ./myapp
Dev — uses values.yaml defaults
→
helm install myapp ./myapp -f values-staging.yaml
Staging — 2 replicas, HPA enabled
→
helm install myapp ./myapp -f values-prod.yaml
Production — 5 replicas, TLS, alerts

💡 This is why Helm exists. One chart, many environments. Change the image tag in one values file and deploy everywhere. Zero copy-paste drift.

🧩 Core Concepts

📦
Chart
The package — a directory of YAML templates + values
= npm package / apt .deb
🚀
Release
A running instance of a chart installed in a cluster
= running container / installed app
📡
Repository
A server hosting chart packages (.tgz) and an index
= PyPI / npm registry
⚙️
Values
Configuration injected into templates at render time
= environment variables
📝
Templates
Go-templated Kubernetes YAML files with placeholders
= Jinja2 for K8s manifests
🪝
Hooks
Jobs that run at specific lifecycle points (pre/post install)
= CI/CD pipeline stages
🏗️ Architecture
💻
Helm CLI Your local terminal
→
renders templates
📚
Helm Library Go template engine
→
kubectl apply
☸️
K8s API Server Creates resources
→
stores state in
🔒
Secret Release history

💡 No server-side component in Helm 3. Tiller is gone. Helm stores release state as Kubernetes Secrets in the same namespace. Your RBAC is handled by your kubeconfig, not a privileged in-cluster daemon.

🎯 Key Takeaways

  • Helm is a client-only tool in v3 — no Tiller, no cluster-side daemon
  • A Chart is immutable; a Release is a running instance that can be upgraded
  • Release history is stored as K8s Secrets — audit trail built-in
  • DevOps angle: Helm is your deployment artifact. Chart version bumps = deployments
Helm Module 02

Chart Anatomy

Every file in a chart and what it does — click to explore

👶 ELI5

A Helm chart is just a folder with a specific structure. Chart.yaml is the passport (name, version), values.yaml is the settings file, and templates/ is where your Kubernetes YAML lives — but with placeholders instead of hardcoded values.

📂 mychart/ Click a file to explore
📄Chart.yamlmetadata
⚙️values.yamldefaults
🔍values.schema.jsonvalidation
🙈.helmignoreignore
📁charts/deps
📁templates/core
📄deployment.yaml
📄service.yaml
📄ingress.yaml
🔧_helpers.tplpartials
📢NOTES.txt
📁tests/test
🧪test-connection.yaml
Chart.yaml
Chart Metadata — The Passport
Every chart must have a Chart.yaml. It tells Helm the chart name, version, and what app version it deploys. The version field must follow semver — this is what gets bumped on every release.
Example
apiVersion: v2
name: mychart
description: A production-ready app chart
type: application
version: 1.3.0       # Chart version (semver)
appVersion: "2.4.1" # App image tag
maintainers:
  - name: platform-team
    email: platform@company.io
💡 version = chart package version (bump when chart logic changes). appVersion = the app you're deploying (informational only — doesn't affect Helm logic).
Helm Module 02b

⚙️ values.yaml — The Complete Guide

The single most important file in your chart — everything you need to know

👶 ELI5

values.yaml is your chart's configuration knob panel. Every setting that might need to change — image tag, replica count, memory limits, domain name, feature flags — goes here instead of hardcoded in your templates. When you deploy to dev, you leave it as-is. When you deploy to prod, you override just the values that differ. One chart, infinite configurations.

😩 Without values.yaml
# deployment.yaml — hardcoded everything image: myapp:v1.3.0 # ← change for every deploy replicas: 5 # ← different per env memory: "512Mi" # ← different per env host: myapp.prod.com # ← different per env # To deploy dev: manually edit 4+ values # To deploy staging: manually edit again # To update image tag: touch every env's file
✅ With values.yaml
# deployment.yaml — parameterised image: myapp:{{ .Values.image.tag }} replicas: {{ .Values.replicaCount }} memory: {{ .Values.resources.memory }} host: {{ .Values.ingress.host }} # To deploy dev: helm install myapp ./chart # To deploy prod: helm install myapp ./chart \ # -f values-prod.yaml # To update image: --set image.tag=v1.4.0

📄 Anatomy of a Well-Structured values.yaml

A production-grade values file has clear sections, helpful comments, and safe defaults

values.yamlFull annotated example
# ── Replica count ──────────────────────────────────────────────
# Number of pod replicas. Override in values-prod.yaml for scale.
replicaCount: 1

# ── Container image ────────────────────────────────────────────
image:
  repository: myregistry.io/myapp  # full registry path
  tag: "v1.0.0"                     # override via --set image.tag=
  pullPolicy: IfNotPresent          # Always in prod, IfNotPresent in dev

# ── Service ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
service:
  type: ClusterIP   # ClusterIP | NodePort | LoadBalancer
  port: 80
  targetPort: 8080  # port your app actually listens on

# ── Ingress (off by default — enable per environment) ──────────
ingress:
  enabled: false          # set true in values-staging/prod.yaml
  host: ""               # myapp.example.com
  tlsEnabled: false
  annotations: {}        # nginx.ingress.kubernetes.io/... etc

# ── Resource limits (ALWAYS set these — no limits = cluster risk) ─
resources:
  requests:
    cpu: "100m"
    memory: "128Mi"
  limits:
    cpu: "500m"
    memory: "256Mi"

# ── Horizontal Pod Autoscaler (off by default) ─────────────────
autoscaling:
  enabled: false
  minReplicas: 2
  maxReplicas: 10
  targetCPUUtilizationPercentage: 70

# ── Application config (non-secret env vars) ───────────────────
config:
  LOG_LEVEL: "info"        # override to "debug" in dev
  DB_HOST: "postgres.svc"
  CACHE_TTL: "300"

# ── Secrets (reference only — never put real values here!) ─────
# Use: helm install --set secrets.dbPassword=$DB_PASS
# Or:  helm-secrets + SOPS / External Secrets Operator
secrets:
  dbPassword: ""          # injected via CI --set, never committed
  apiKey: ""

🔢 Data Types in values.yaml — Know Your YAML

YAML auto-detects types. This catches beginners off-guard constantly.

TypeExampleGotchaSafe Pattern
String tag: v1.0.0 tag: 1.0 → parsed as float 1.0 Always quote: tag: "v1.0.0"
Integer port: 8080 Fine as-is — YAML parses unquoted numbers as int port: 8080 ✓
Boolean enabled: true enabled: "true" (string) ≠ true (bool) in Go templates No quotes for booleans: enabled: false
Null / empty annotations: {} annotations: (bare) = null, breaks toYaml pipeline Use {} for empty maps, [] for empty lists
Multi-word string name: my app Spaces make it ambiguous — YAML may mis-parse Quote it: name: "my app"
List hosts: [a.com, b.com] Inline list vs block list — both valid but be consistent Block form preferred for readability
# In templates: always pipe strings through | quote for safety name: {{ .Values.config.LOG_LEVEL | quote }} # → "info" # Check if a value is truly set (not empty string, not null) {{- if and .Values.ingress.host (ne .Values.ingress.host "") }} host: {{ .Values.ingress.host }} {{- end }}

🔀 The enabled Pattern — Conditional Resources

The most common and powerful pattern in Helm charts — toggle entire resources on/off per environment

values.yamlDefault off
# Safe defaults — minimal resources
ingress:
  enabled: false  # no ingress in dev

autoscaling:
  enabled: false  # no HPA in dev

monitoring:
  enabled: false  # no ServiceMonitor in dev
values-prod.yamlOverride — enable in prod
# Only override what changes
ingress:
  enabled: true
  host: myapp.example.com

autoscaling:
  enabled: true
  minReplicas: 3

monitoring:
  enabled: true
# In templates/ingress.yaml — entire file is conditional {{- if .Values.ingress.enabled }} apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1 kind: Ingress metadata: name: {{ .Release.Name }} spec: rules: - host: {{ .Values.ingress.host }} {{- end }} # If ingress.enabled=false → this entire file renders as EMPTY # → K8s never sees an Ingress object # → No resource created, no cleanup needed
🔍 Values Validation

values.schema.json — Validate Before Deploying

Add a schema file and Helm will reject invalid values before touching your cluster — like a TypeScript type checker for your config.

values.schema.jsonEnforce rules on input
{
  "$schema": "http://json-schema.org/draft-07/schema",
  "type": "object",
  "required": ["replicaCount", "image"],
  "properties": {
    "replicaCount": {
      "type": "integer",
      "minimum": 1,
      "maximum": 50
    },
    "image": {
      "type": "object",
      "required": ["repository", "tag"],
      "properties": {
        "repository": { "type": "string" },
        "tag":        { "type": "string" },
        "pullPolicy": {
          "type": "string",
          "enum": ["Always", "IfNotPresent", "Never"]
        }
      }
    },
    "service": {
      "type": "object",
      "properties": {
        "port": { "type": "integer", "minimum": 1, "maximum": 65535 }
      }
    }
  }
}
❌ Schema rejects bad input
helm install myapp ./chart \ --set replicaCount=100 Error: values don't meet the specifications of the schema: - replicaCount: Must be ≤ 50 helm install myapp ./chart \ --set image.pullPolicy=latest Error: image.pullPolicy must be one of: Always, IfNotPresent, Never
✅ Why this matters
  • Catches misconfigured values before pods start
  • Required fields enforce contract with users
  • Enum rules prevent typos in critical fields
  • Range checks block runaway replica counts
  • Self-documenting — schema IS the spec

🌐 Global Values — Sharing Config Across Subcharts

When your chart has subcharts (dependencies), use .Values.global to share values without duplicating them in every sub-values file.

parent chart values.yamlDefine globals once
# Global values — accessible to ALL subcharts
global:
  imageRegistry: myregistry.io
  environment:   production
  domain:        example.com
  imagePullSecrets:
    - myregistrysecret

# Per-subchart overrides (optional)
postgresql:       # subchart name
  auth:
    database: myappdb

redis:            # subchart name
  architecture: standalone
subchart templates/deployment.yamlAccess globals
# Subcharts access global via .Values.global
image:
  # Use global registry if set, else local value
  repository: {{- if .Values.global.imageRegistry }}
    {{ .Values.global.imageRegistry }}/myapp
  {{- else }}
    {{ .Values.image.repository }}
  {{- end }}

env:
  - name: ENVIRONMENT
    value: {{ .Values.global.environment | quote }}

imagePullSecrets:
  {{- toYaml .Values.global.imagePullSecrets | nindent 2 }}
💡 global values are merged down into every subchart automatically. You set the registry once at the parent level — all 3 subcharts use it.
🚨 Security

What NEVER Goes in values.yaml (and What to Do Instead)

❌ Never put in values.yamlWhy✅ Use instead
Database passwords, API keys, tokens Git-committed plain text — exposed forever in history --set secrets.dbPass=$DB_PASS in CI, or helm-secrets + SOPS
TLS certificates / private keys Extremely sensitive, large binary data in YAML is fragile cert-manager + K8s TLS secrets
Cloud provider credentials (AWS keys, GCP SA JSON) One accidental commit = full cloud account exposure Workload Identity / IRSA / Pod Identity
SSH private keys As above — immediate security incident if leaked K8s Secrets + External Secrets Operator
# ✅ Safe pattern: placeholder in values.yaml, real value injected in CI # values.yaml: secrets: dbPassword: "" # intentionally empty — never committed with real value # CI pipeline (GitHub Actions / Jenkins / ArgoCD): helm upgrade myapp ./chart \ --set secrets.dbPassword=${{ secrets.DB_PASSWORD }} # The real password lives only in your CI secrets vault, never in git

🔍 Inspecting Active Values at Runtime

Always verify what values Helm is actually using — especially after complex overrides

# See what values are active for a running release helm get values myapp # user-supplied values only helm get values myapp --all # ALL values including defaults helm get values myapp --revision 3 # what was active at revision 3 helm get values myapp -n production # specify namespace # Preview what values would be used BEFORE deploying helm install myapp ./chart \ -f values-prod.yaml \ --set image.tag=v2.0.0 \ --dry-run --debug \ 2>&1 | grep -A 50 "COMPUTED VALUES" # see merged result # Render templates with specific values (no cluster needed) helm template myapp ./chart -f values-prod.yaml

🎯 Key Takeaways

  • values.yaml = safe dev defaults. Every env that needs different config gets its own values-{env}.yaml
  • Use the enabled: false pattern to conditionally include whole resources (ingress, HPA, monitoring)
  • Always quote string values that look like numbers or booleans. Use {} for empty maps, [] for empty lists
  • Add values.schema.json to validate input — catches misconfiguration before a single pod starts
  • Never commit secrets to values.yaml — use --set injection from CI secrets or External Secrets Operator
  • Use .Values.global in umbrella charts so all subcharts share registry, environment, and pull secrets from one place
Helm Module 03

Templates & Values

Go templating in Kubernetes YAML — how values flow into manifests

🎛️ Live Template Renderer

Edit values on the left — see the rendered YAML update in real time →

⚙️ values.yaml
📝 deployment.yaml (template)
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: {{ .Release.Name }}
spec:
  replicas: {{ .Values.replicaCount }}
  template:
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: app
        image: myapp:{{ .Values.image.tag }}
        ports:
        - containerPort: {{ .Values.service.port }}
        resources:
          limits:
            memory: {{ .Values.resources.memory }}
{{- if .Values.ingress.enabled }}
---
kind: Ingress # rendered only when enabled
{{- end }}
✅ Rendered Output

        

      

    


    
    

      
📖 Template Syntax Cheatsheet

      

        
{{ .Values.xxx }}
Value substitution from values.yaml

        
{{ .Release.Name }}
Built-in release name (set by helm install)

        
{{ include "chart.name" . }}
Call a named template from _helpers.tpl

        
{{- if .Values.x }} ... {{- end }}
Conditional block (- trims whitespace)

        
{{- range .Values.list }} ... {{- end }}
Loop over a list or map

        
{{ toYaml . | nindent 8 }}
Dump a map as YAML with 8-space indent

        
{{ .Chart.Name }}-{{ .Chart.Version }}
Access Chart.yaml fields

        
{{ default "80" .Values.port }}
Provide a fallback default value

      

    


    
    

      
🔑 Built-in Objects

      

        

          
ObjectKey FieldsDevOps Analogy

          
            
.ReleaseName, Namespace, Revision, IsInstall, IsUpgradeThe deployment metadata — like a CI run context

            
.ChartName, Version, AppVersion, DescriptionThe artifact manifest — like an OCI image label

            
.ValuesWhatever's in values.yamlConfigMap / environment variables

            
.Files.Get, .Glob, .AsConfig, .AsSecretsEmbed static files as ConfigMaps

            
.Capabilities.KubeVersion, .APIVersionsFeature detection — like checking K8s version

          
        

      

    


    
    

      
🔧 Named Templates in _helpers.tpl

      
Define reusable snippets once, include anywhere — the DRY principle for Helm

      

        

          
_helpers.tplDefine

          
{{/*
  Generate standard labels for all resources
*/}}
{{- define "mychart.labels" -}}
app.kubernetes.io/name: {{ .Chart.Name }}
app.kubernetes.io/instance: {{ .Release.Name }}
app.kubernetes.io/version: {{ .Chart.AppVersion }}
{{- end }}

        

        

          
deployment.yamlInclude

          
metadata:
  labels:
    {{- include "mychart.labels" . | nindent 4 }}
# Rendered output:
#   app.kubernetes.io/name: mychart
#   app.kubernetes.io/instance: myapp
#   app.kubernetes.io/version: "2.4.1"

        

      

    


    
    

      
🔑 Critical Concept

      
Values Override Hierarchy — How Helm Merges Values

      
Helm merges values from multiple sources. The order of precedence (highest wins):


      

        

          
1 (highest)

          

            --set image.tag=v2.0.0
            CLI flag — overrides everything. Use for one-off tweaks or CI pipeline injection
          

        

        
▼ overrides

        

          
2

          

            -f values-prod.yaml
            Environment-specific values file passed with -f. Overrides defaults
          

        

        
▼ overrides

        

          
3 (lowest)

          

            values.yaml
            Chart defaults — always present, lowest priority. Everything starts here
          

        

      


      

        

          
values.yamlBase defaults

          
replicaCount: 1
image:
  tag: "latest"
resources:
  memory: "128Mi"
autoscaling:
  enabled: false

        

        

          
values-prod.yamlEnv override

          
# Only override what differs from base
replicaCount: 5
resources:
  memory: "512Mi"
autoscaling:
  enabled: true
  maxReplicas: 20

        

      


      
# Prod deploy: base values + prod override + CLI tag injection
helm upgrade --install myapp ./myapp \
  -f values-prod.yaml \
  --set image.tag=v2.1.0 \
  --namespace production

# Result — what Helm actually sees:
# replicaCount: 5         (from values-prod.yaml)
# image.tag: "v2.1.0"     (from --set, overrides prod file)
# resources.memory: 512Mi (from values-prod.yaml)
# autoscaling.enabled: true (from values-prod.yaml)


      
💡 Best practice: Keep values.yaml as safe dev defaults. Create values-staging.yaml and values-prod.yaml with only the differences. Use --set in CI for dynamic values like image tags or build IDs.

    


    
    

      
⚙️ How Templates Become YAML — The Rendering Pipeline

      
Understanding what happens between helm install and pods starting

      

        

          
📂

          
Chart Source

          
templates/deployment.yaml with {{ .Values.xxx }} placeholders

        

        
+

        

          
⚙️

          
Merged Values

          
values.yaml + -f file + --set flags merged into one object

        

        
→

        

          
🔧

          
Go Template Engine

          
Helm renders all {{ }} expressions. Conditionals, loops, includes evaluated

        

        
→

        

          
📋

          
Pure Kubernetes YAML

          
Plain manifests — identical to what you'd write by hand, but generated

        

        
→

        

          
☸️

          
kubectl apply

          
Helm sends manifests to the K8s API — resources are created/updated

        

      

      
💡 Run helm template myapp ./myapp -f values-prod.yaml to see the rendered YAML without touching the cluster — great for debugging.

    


    

      
⚠️ Common Gotchas

      
    
        
  • → Use nindent not indent — nindent adds a leading newline, indent doesn't
    • 
        
  • → Always quote string values: {{ .Values.name | quote }} — bare strings in YAML can be parsed as booleans/numbers
    • 
        
  • → Whitespace control: {{- trims leading whitespace, -}} trims trailing — extra blank lines break manifest indentation
    • 
        
  • → {{ if .Values.x }} vs {{ if .Values.x | ne "" }} — an empty string is falsy in Go templates
    • 
      

    

  


  
  

    

      
Helm Module 04

      
Release Lifecycle

      
Install → Upgrade → Rollback → Uninstall — and every state in between

    


    
    

      
🔄 Release State Machine

      

        
pending-install

        
→

        
deployed

        
→

        
pending-upgrade

        
→

        
deployed (v2)

        

          
failed

          
→

          
deployed (rollback)

          
→

          
uninstalled

        

      

    


    
    

      

        
📥
Install

        
helm install <name> <chart>
helm install --dry-run --debug

        
First deployment. Creates revision 1.

      

      

        
⬆️
Upgrade

        
helm upgrade <name> <chart>
helm upgrade --install  # upsert

        
Bump chart or values. Revision ++.

      

      

        
⏪
Rollback

        
helm rollback <name> <rev>
helm history <name>

        
Restore any previous revision.

      

      

        
🗑️
Uninstall

        
helm uninstall <name>
helm uninstall --keep-history

        
Remove all chart resources.

      

    


    
    

      
📋 Release History — helm history myapp

      

        

          
REVISIONSTATUSCHARTAPP VERSIONDESCRIPTION

          
            
1deployedmychart-1.0.02.3.0Install complete

            
2deployedmychart-1.1.02.4.0Upgrade complete

            
3failedmychart-1.2.02.5.0-rc1Upgrade failed: CrashLoopBackOff

            
4deployedmychart-1.1.02.4.0Rollback to revision 2

          
        

      

      
💡 --keep-history on uninstall preserves this table so you can audit past deployments — like keeping CloudTrail logs.

    


    
    

      
🔑 Zero-Downtime Secret

      
What Happens to Your Pods During helm upgrade?

      
This is the question every beginner has. Here's exactly what happens — step by step.


      

        

          
1

          

            You run helm upgrade myapp ./myapp --set image.tag=v2.0.0
            
Helm renders new templates, compares to current cluster state, applies the diff.

          

        

        

          
2

          

            K8s starts a Rolling Update (default Deployment strategy)
            
New pods with the new image are created first. Old pods are NOT immediately killed.

            
# Watch what happens:
kubectl get pods -w
myapp-v1-abc123   Running   ← still serving traffic
myapp-v2-def456   Pending   ← new pod starting up
myapp-v2-def456   Running   ← health checks pass
myapp-v1-abc123   Terminating ← NOW old pod is killed

          

        

        

          
3

          

            Readiness gates ensure zero downtime
            
K8s won't kill the old pod until the new one is Ready (passes readinessProbe). Traffic continues flowing to v1 while v2 starts. No requests are dropped.

          

        

        

          
4

          

            If the new pod fails health checks → Helm detects failure
            
With --atomic flag: Helm auto-rollbacks. Without it: release state = failed, old pods keep running until you fix it manually.

          

        

      


      

        

          
✅ Recommended: --atomic

          
helm upgrade myapp ./myapp \
  --set image.tag=v2.0.0 \
  --atomic \
  --timeout 5m

# If v2 pods don't become Ready in 5 min
# → Helm auto-rollbacks to v1
# → No manual intervention needed
# → Great for CI/CD pipelines

        

        

          
⚠️ Without --atomic

          
helm upgrade myapp ./myapp \
  --set image.tag=v2.0.0

# If upgrade fails:
# Release state = "failed"
# Your old pods are still running (good)
# But next helm upgrade will fail:
# Error: UPGRADE FAILED: another operation
# (install/upgrade/rollback) is in progress
# Fix: helm rollback myapp 2

        

      

    


    
    

      
🖥️ What Real helm install Output Looks Like

      
First-timers are often confused by what to expect. Here's a real terminal session:

      

        

          
terminalSuccessful install

          
$ helm install myapp ./myapp \
    -f values-prod.yaml \
    --namespace production \
    --create-namespace

NAME: myapp
LAST DEPLOYED: Mon Mar 17 10:42:15 2026
NAMESPACE: production
STATUS: deployed
REVISION: 1
NOTES:
  Application myapp has been deployed!
  Access it at: https://myapp.example.com

  Verify pods are running:
  kubectl get pods -n production

        

        

          
terminalFailed install (and what to do)

          
$ helm install myapp ./myapp \
    -f values-prod.yaml

Error: INSTALLATION FAILED:
  timed out waiting for the condition

# Pods stuck? Check what's wrong:
$ kubectl get pods -n production
NAME            STATUS             AGE
myapp-abc123    CrashLoopBackOff   90s

$ kubectl logs myapp-abc123
Error: DB_HOST env var not set

# Fix: add DB_HOST to values-prod.yaml
# then: helm upgrade myapp ./myapp
#        -f values-prod.yaml --atomic

        

      

    


    

      
🎯 Key Takeaways

      
    
        
  • helm upgrade --install is the idempotent upsert — use this in CI/CD pipelines
    • 
        
  • Every upgrade creates a new revision — rollback is always one command away
    • 
        
  • failed state blocks future upgrades until you rollback or fix
    • 
        
  • Use --atomic flag to auto-rollback on upgrade failure
    • 
        
  • Pods are replaced via rolling update — zero downtime when readinessProbe is configured
    • 
      

    

  


  
  

    

      
Helm Guide

      
🕰️ Version Control & Rollbacks

      
Every release is versioned — list history, inspect any revision, roll back in one command

    


    

      
👶 ELI5

      
Helm automatically keeps a full history of every deploy — like git log for your Kubernetes deployments. Each helm install or helm upgrade creates a new revision number. If something breaks, helm rollback myapp 2 instantly restores the exact state of revision 2 — the same templates, the same values, everything.

    


    
    

      
📦 How Helm Stores Release History

      
No external database. Helm stores each revision as a Kubernetes Secret in the same namespace.

      

        

          
# See the raw secrets Helm creates:
kubectl get secrets -n production \
  -l owner=helm,name=myapp

NAME                          TYPE
sh.helm.release.v1.myapp.v1   helm.sh/release.v1
sh.helm.release.v1.myapp.v2   helm.sh/release.v1
sh.helm.release.v1.myapp.v3   helm.sh/release.v1
sh.helm.release.v1.myapp.v4   helm.sh/release.v1

        

        

          
🏗️ What each secret contains

          
    
            
  • Rendered manifests — the exact YAML that was applied to the cluster at that revision
    • 
            
  • Computed values — all merged values (base + overrides + --set flags)
    • 
            
  • Chart metadata — chart name, version, app version
    • 
            
  • Release status — deployed / failed / superseded
    • 
            
  • Timestamps & description — when deployed and any description you set
    • 
          

          
💡 This is why helm rollback is so reliable — it replays the exact stored state, not a guess.

        

      

    


    
    

      
🔬 Release History Inspector — helm history myapp

      
Click any revision to inspect it and see the rollback command


      

        
        

          

            
Rev 1

            

              deployed
              mychart-1.0.0
            

            
Install complete

            
Mar 10, 09:12

          

          

          

            
Rev 2

            

              deployed
              mychart-1.1.0
            

            
Upgrade: image v1.2.0 → v1.3.0

            
Mar 12, 14:30

          

          

          

            
Rev 3

            

              failed
              mychart-1.2.0
            

            
Upgrade failed: CrashLoopBackOff

            
Mar 14, 10:05

          

          

          

            
Rev 4

            

              deployed
              mychart-1.1.0
            

            
Rollback to revision 2

            
Mar 14, 10:08

          

          

          

            
Rev 5 CURRENT

            

              deployed
              mychart-1.2.1
            

            
Upgrade: hotfix v1.4.0 with DB fix

            
Mar 15, 16:44

          

        


        
        

          
        

      

    


    
    

      
📋 All Version Control Commands

      

        

          
📜

          

            
List all revisions

            
helm history myapp
helm history myapp -n production
helm history myapp --max 20   # limit output

            
Shows: revision, date, status, chart version, app version, description

          

        

        

          
🔍

          

            
Inspect a specific revision

            
# What values were used?
helm get values myapp --revision 2
helm get values myapp --revision 2 --all

# What manifests were applied?
helm get manifest myapp --revision 2

# Notes shown to user at that revision
helm get notes myapp --revision 2

            
Use this before rolling back — confirm the revision has the config you expect

          

        

        

          
⏪

          

            
Roll back to a revision

            
# Rollback to specific revision
helm rollback myapp 2
helm rollback myapp 2 -n production

# Rollback to the previous revision
helm rollback myapp

# Rollback creates a NEW revision
# (rev 2 restored = becomes rev 5)

# Dry-run first (see what changes)
helm rollback myapp 2 --dry-run

            
⚠️ Rollback creates a new revision — it does NOT delete the failed one. History is always preserved.

          

        

        

          
🗑️

          

            
Keep or delete history on uninstall

            
# Default: wipes ALL history secrets
helm uninstall myapp

# Keep history even after uninstall
helm uninstall myapp --keep-history

# With --keep-history you can still see:
helm history myapp
# STATUS = uninstalled (but history intact)

# And even re-install later with history
helm rollback myapp 3   # restores rev 3

            
Use --keep-history in production — audit trail is essential for incident review

          

        

      

    


    
    

      
🚨 Real IncidentProd Down — 30 Second Recovery

      
Scenario: You upgraded prod at 10:05 AM. At 10:07 AM — alerts fire.

      

        

          
1

          

            Check what's running and what broke
            
# Is the release in failed state?
helm status myapp -n production
# STATUS: failed

# See full history — find the last good revision
helm history myapp -n production
# REVISION  STATUS    CHART          DESCRIPTION
# 1         deployed  mychart-1.0.0  Install complete
# 2         deployed  mychart-1.1.0  Upgrade: v1.3.0   ← last good ✓
# 3         failed    mychart-1.2.0  CrashLoopBackOff  ← this broke it

          

        

        

          
2

          

            Verify revision 2 has what you expect (don't just blindly roll back)
            
helm get values myapp --revision 2 -n production
# image:
#   tag: v1.3.0       ← confirmed, this was stable
# replicaCount: 5
# autoscaling:
#   enabled: true

          

        

        

          
3

          

            Roll back — one command, prod is restored
            
helm rollback myapp 2 -n production
# Rollback was a success.

# Helm has now:
# → Re-applied revision 2's templates to the cluster
# → Started a rolling update back to v1.3.0
# → Created revision 4 = "Rollback to revision 2"
# → Pods are healthy in ~30 seconds

# Confirm recovery
helm status myapp -n production
helm history myapp -n production

          

        

      

      
⏱️ Total time from alert to recovery: ~30–60 seconds. This is the power of Helm version control — you never need to scramble to find the "old" YAML files.

    


    
    

      
🔭 Pro Tip: Preview Changes Before Upgrading — helm diff

      
The helm-diff plugin shows exactly what will change before you run helm upgrade — like git diff before committing.

      

        

          
# Install the plugin (one-time)
helm plugin install \
  https://github.com/databus23/helm-diff

# Preview an upgrade before applying it
helm diff upgrade myapp ./myapp \
  -f values-prod.yaml \
  --set image.tag=v2.0.0

# Preview a rollback before committing
helm diff rollback myapp 2

        

        

          
# Example diff output:
- image: myapp:v1.3.0
+ image: myapp:v2.0.0

- replicas: 3
+ replicas: 5

# No surprise changes — you see every
# field that will be modified before
# a single pod is touched.

        

      

      
💡 SRE best practice: Make helm diff upgrade a required step in your CI/CD pipeline before helm upgrade. Post the diff as a PR comment — your team reviews what's actually changing, not just the PR description.

    


    

      
🎯 Key Takeaways

      
    
        
  • Every helm install / upgrade / rollback creates a new revision — full audit trail
    • 
        
  • Use helm get values --revision N to inspect the exact config that was active at any revision
    • 
        
  • Rollback creates a new revision — it never deletes history
    • 
        
  • Always helm uninstall --keep-history in production so audit logs survive
    • 
        
  • Use helm diff plugin to preview changes before every upgrade — no surprises
    • 
      

    

  


  
  

    

      
Helm Module 05

      
Repositories

      
Where charts live, how to find them, and how to host your own

    


    

      

        
📦 Traditional Repo

        
An HTTP server with an index.yaml and chart .tgz files. Helm fetches the index, resolves the chart, downloads the tarball.

        
helm repo add bitnami \
  https://charts.bitnami.com/bitnami
helm repo update
helm search repo bitnami/postgresql

      

      

        
🐳 OCI Registry

        
Helm 3.8+ supports storing charts in any OCI-compliant registry (ECR, GCR, ACR, Harbor). No separate index file needed.

        
helm push mychart-1.0.0.tgz \
  oci://registry.example.com/charts
helm install myapp \
  oci://registry.example.com/charts/mychart

      

    


    
    

      
🌟 Popular Public Repositories

      

        

          
🔍

          ArtifactHub
          hub.helm.sh — search all public charts in one place
          artifacthub.io
        

        

          
🧪

          Bitnami
          Production-hardened charts (PostgreSQL, Redis, Kafka…)
          charts.bitnami.com
        

        

          
📊

          Prometheus
          prometheus-community/kube-prometheus-stack
          prometheus-community
        

        

          
🌐

          Ingress-NGINX
          The standard ingress controller chart for K8s
          kubernetes/ingress-nginx
        

      

    


    
    

      
🔗 Chart Dependencies

      
Declare sub-chart dependencies in Chart.yaml — Helm resolves and downloads them automatically

      

        

          
Chart.yamlDeclare deps

          
dependencies:
  - name: postgresql
    version: "~13.0"     # semver range
    repository: "https://charts.bitnami.com/bitnami"
    condition: postgresql.enabled
  - name: redis
    version: "~18.0"
    repository: "oci://registry-1.docker.io/bitnamicharts"

        

        

          
ShellDownload deps

          
# Downloads charts/ to lock file
helm dependency update ./mychart

# Check what's locked
cat Chart.lock

# Enable/disable via values
helm install myapp ./mychart \
  --set postgresql.enabled=true \
  --set redis.enabled=false

        

      

    

  


  
  

    

      
Helm Module 06

      
Hooks

      
Run jobs at specific lifecycle points — database migrations, smoke tests, notifications

    


    

      
👶 ELI5

      
Hooks are like CI/CD pipeline stages. A pre-install hook runs before Helm creates any resources — perfect for a DB migration job. A post-install hook runs after everything is up — perfect for a smoke test. Helm waits for each hook to complete before proceeding.

    


    

      
📋 Hook Reference

      

        

          
HookWhen it runsCommon use case

          
            
pre-installBefore any templates are appliedDatabase migration Job

            
post-installAfter all templates are runningSmoke test, send Slack notification

            
pre-upgradeBefore upgrade beginsBackup current state

            
post-upgradeAfter upgrade completesCache warm-up, verify health

            
pre-rollbackBefore rollback beginsNotify on-call, capture state

            
pre-deleteBefore uninstallData export, drain connections

            
testOn helm test commandConnection test, API health check

          
        

      

    


    

      
📝 Pre-install DB Migration Hook

      
YAMLtemplates/pre-install-migration.yaml

      
apiVersion: batch/v1
kind: Job
metadata:
  name: {{ .Release.Name }}-db-migrate
  annotations:
    "helm.sh/hook": pre-install,pre-upgrade  # ← This is what makes it a hook
    "helm.sh/hook-weight": "-5"              # ← Lower = runs first
    "helm.sh/hook-delete-policy": hook-succeeded # ← Auto-cleanup
spec:
  template:
    spec:
      restartPolicy: Never
      containers:
      - name: migrate
        image: myapp:{{ .Values.image.tag }}
        command: ["python", "manage.py", "migrate"]

      
💡 Hook weight: multiple hooks of the same type run in ascending weight order (-10 before -5 before 0 before 5). Use negative weights for hooks that must run earliest.

    


    

      
🗑️ Hook Deletion Policies

      

        
hook-succeeded
Delete the hook resource after it succeeds. Good for migrations — keeps the cluster clean.

        
hook-failed
Delete even if it fails. Use only when you have external logging — you'll lose the failure logs.

        
before-hook-creation
Default. Delete the old hook resource before running the new one. Allows retries on re-install.

        
(none)
Keep the resource forever. Useful for audit trail — but your cluster accumulates old Job objects.

      

    

  


  
  

    

      
Helm Module 07

      
Testing Charts

      
Lint, dry-run, unit test, and CI validation — before anything touches the cluster

    


    

      

        
🔍
helm lint

        
Syntax check. Catches YAML errors, missing required values, and malformed templates.

        
helm lint ./mychart \
  -f values-dev.yaml

      

      

        
📄
helm template

        
Render templates locally without connecting to a cluster. Review the output YAML.

        
helm template myapp ./mychart \
  --values prod.yaml

      

      

        
🧪
--dry-run

        
Full render + server-side validation. Connects to K8s API but creates nothing.

        
helm install myapp ./mychart \
  --dry-run --debug

      

      

        
✅
helm test

        
Runs test hook pods in the cluster. Verifies the release works post-install.

        
helm test myapp \
  --logs

      

    


    

      
🧬 helm-unittest — Unit Testing Charts

      
Test your templates without a cluster — assert on rendered YAML output

      

        

          
YAMLtests/deployment_test.yaml

          
suite: test deployment
tests:
  - it: should set replica count from values
    set:
      replicaCount: 3
    asserts:
      - equal:
          path: spec.replicas
          value: 3
      - isKind:
          of: Deployment
  - it: should disable ingress when false
    set:
      ingress.enabled: false
    asserts:
      - hasDocuments:
          count: 0
        template: templates/ingress.yaml

        

        

          
ShellCI pipeline

          
# Install plugin
helm plugin install \
  https://github.com/helm-unittest/helm-unittest

# Run all tests
helm unittest ./mychart

# CI-friendly output
helm unittest ./mychart \
  --output-type JUnit \
  --output-file test-results.xml

# Chart-testing (ct) for CI
ct lint --charts ./charts/mychart
ct install --charts ./charts/mychart

          
💡 CI order: helm lint → helm unittest → helm template | kubeval → ct install. Each stage catches different issues before cluster impact.

        

      

    

  


  
  

    

      
Helm Module 08

      
Secrets & Security

      
The values.yaml problem, encryption strategies, and Helm 3 RBAC model

    


    

      
⚠️ The Problem

      
values.yaml is plaintext. If you put a database password in values.yaml and commit it, it's in your git history forever. Even worse — Helm stores release state as K8s Secrets, but those are only base64-encoded, not encrypted. Anyone with cluster access can read them.

    


    

      
🔐 Secrets Management Approaches

      

        

          
ApproachToolProsCons

          
            
Encrypted valueshelm-secrets + SOPSGit-safe, portableExtra tooling, key management

            
External secretsESO + Vault / AWS SMNo secrets in chartOperator required in cluster

            
Sealed SecretsBitnami Sealed SecretsK8s native, GitOps-friendlyCluster-specific (can't move certs)

            
CI env injection--set in pipelineSimple, no extra toolingSecrets appear in CI logs, no audit

          
        

      

    


    

      

        
🏆 Helm 3 Security Win

        
Tiller is Gone

        
Helm 2 had Tiller — a cluster-side daemon with cluster-admin privileges. It was a massive security hole. Helm 3 is client-only: it uses your kubeconfig credentials directly, so RBAC applies normally.

        
# Your CI ServiceAccount needs only:
- get/list/watch on Secrets
- create/update/delete on
  Deployments, Services, etc.
# NOT cluster-admin!

      

      

        
✅ Best Practices Checklist

        
    
          
  • ☑ Never commit real secrets to values.yaml
    • 
          
  • ☑ Use values.schema.json to validate all required fields
    • 
          
  • ☑ Pin dependency versions (avoid * or >=)
    • 
          
  • ☑ Use --atomic in CI to auto-rollback on failure
    • 
          
  • ☑ Verify chart integrity with helm verify
    • 
          
  • ☑ Least-privilege ServiceAccount for CI runners
    •

Helm Module 10

      
Helm Project Ideas

      
Real projects to build, ship, and add to your DevOps portfolio

    


    

      

        

          🪆
          

            
Microservices Umbrella Chart

            Intermediate
          

        

        
Build an umbrella (parent) chart that wraps 3 microservice sub-charts — api, worker, scheduler. Share global config (database URL, image registry) via .Values.global. Deploy the whole stack with one helm install.

        

          Umbrella ChartSub-chartsglobals
        

        

          Skills: Chart dependencies, global values, per-chart overrides, Chart.yaml conditions
        

      


      

        

          🔁
          

            
GitOps Chart Pipeline

            Intermediate
          

        

        
Connect ArgoCD to a Helm chart repo. When you bump the chart version field, ArgoCD auto-detects the change and triggers a sync. Add Slack notifications on deploy success/failure via ArgoCD hooks.

        

          ArgoCDGitOpsOCI Registry
        

        

          Skills: OCI chart registry, ArgoCD Application, Helm parameters, sync policies
        

      


      

        

          🤖
          

            
Helm Operator in Python

            Advanced
          

        

        
Write a Python Kubernetes operator using kopf that watches a custom CRD (HelmRelease). When the spec changes, the operator calls helm upgrade --install. Think of it as a lightweight Flux/ArgoCD for learning.

        

          kopfCRDPythonsubprocess
        

        

          Skills: K8s operators, CRDs, kopf framework, subprocess helm calls, RBAC
        

      


      

        

          🔬
          

            
Chart Linting CI Pipeline

            Beginner
          

        

        
GitHub Actions pipeline that runs on every PR: helm lint → helm unittest → helm template | kubeval → ct install against a kind cluster. Zero manual review needed for chart correctness.

        

          GitHub Actionsctkindkubeval
        

        

          Skills: chart-testing, kind cluster, GitHub Actions matrix, test artifacts
        

      

    

  



  
  

    

      
Helm Guide

      
🔧 Troubleshooting Helm

      
Common errors every Helm user hits — and exactly how to fix them

    


    

      
👶 ELI5

      
Helm errors look scary but they follow patterns. Almost every error falls into one of 4 categories: template syntax (typo in {{ }}), values mismatch (wrong key names), cluster state (resource already exists), or app crash (your code broke, not Helm). Learn these patterns and you'll debug any issue in minutes.

    


    
    

      
⚡ Error Quick Reference

      

        

          
ErrorCategoryImmediate Fix

          
            

              cannot re-use a name that is still in use
              state
              Use helm upgrade instead of helm install, or helm uninstall first
            

            

              function "xxx" not defined
              template
              Typo in template function — run helm lint ./chart for line number
            

            

              wrong type for value; expected string
              values
              Quote the value in values.yaml: tag: "1.0" not tag: 1.0
            

            

              rendered manifests contain a resource that already exists
              state
              Resource was created outside Helm — use --force or delete the resource first
            

            

              UPGRADE FAILED: another operation (install/upgrade/rollback) is in progress
              state
              Previous op stuck in pending — helm rollback myapp 0 or delete the pending secret
            

            

              ImagePullBackOff / ErrImagePull
              app
              Wrong image tag or registry auth — check .Values.image.tag and imagePullSecrets
            

            

              CrashLoopBackOff
              app
              App crashed at startup — kubectl logs <pod> to see the error
            

            

              OOMKilled
              app
              Pod exceeded memory limit — increase resources.limits.memory in values.yaml
            

            

              Pending — no nodes available to schedule pods
              cluster
              Cluster out of capacity — reduce resources.requests or scale cluster nodes
            

          
        

      

    


    
    

      
🗺️ The Universal Helm Debug Flowchart

      

        

          helm install / upgrade failed
        

        
↓

        

          
Is the error during rendering?
(Error before any K8s API call)

          

            

              
YES

              

                Template / Values issue
                
helm lint ./chart
helm template myapp ./chart \
  -f values.yaml
# Find the {{ }} line with the error

              

            

            

              
NO

              

                Cluster / App issue — check pods
                
kubectl get pods -n <ns>
kubectl describe pod <name> -n <ns>
kubectl logs <name> -n <ns>

              

            

          

        

      

    


    
    

      
🛠️ Essential Debug Commands — Bookmark These

      

        

          
helmInspect release

          
# Release overview
helm status myapp -n production

# What values are active?
helm get values myapp -n production --all

# What manifests did Helm apply?
helm get manifest myapp -n production

# Full release history
helm history myapp -n production

# All releases across namespaces
helm list -A

        

        

          
kubectlInspect pods/resources

          
# All pods for this release
kubectl get pods -n production \
  -l app.kubernetes.io/instance=myapp

# Why is the pod not starting?
kubectl describe pod <name> -n production

# App logs (add -f to follow)
kubectl logs <name> -n production
kubectl logs <name> -n production --previous

# Events (great for pull errors)
kubectl get events -n production \
  --sort-by='.lastTimestamp'

        

      

    


    
    

      
🆘 Recovery Commands

      
# Rollback to specific revision
helm rollback myapp 2 -n production      # roll back to revision 2
helm rollback myapp -n production        # roll back to previous revision

# Force uninstall a stuck release
helm uninstall myapp -n production --no-hooks   # skip hooks if they're stuck

# Clear a "pending" state manually (use with caution)
kubectl delete secret -n production \
  -l owner=helm,name=myapp,status=pending-install

# Re-install from scratch
helm uninstall myapp -n production
helm install myapp ./myapp -f values-prod.yaml -n production --create-namespace

    


    

      
🎯 Key Takeaways

      
    
        
  • Always run helm lint + helm template --dry-run before touching a cluster
    • 
        
  • If Helm errors before applying: it's a template/values problem
    • 
        
  • If pods crash after applying: it's an app/config problem — use kubectl logs
    • 
        
  • helm get values --all shows the merged values Helm actually used — great for debugging overrides
    • 
        
  • helm history + helm rollback is your safety net — you can always go back
    • 
      

    

  


  
  

    

      
Helm Lab

      
Project: kubectl → Helm

      
Take 5 raw kubectl files and convert them into a production-grade Helm chart — step by step

    


    
    

      
😩 The Problem You're Solving

      
You have 5 files that you deploy with 5 separate commands. Every environment needs different values (image tag, replicas, domain). You can't version it. You can't roll it back. You can't share it with another team.

      
kubectl apply -f deployment.yaml   # hardcoded image tag
kubectl apply -f service.yaml      # hardcoded port
kubectl apply -f ingress.yaml      # hardcoded hostname
kubectl apply -f configmap.yaml    # hardcoded env values
kubectl apply -f hpa.yaml          # hardcoded min/max replicas

      
After this lab: one command deploys everything, anywhere, with any config.

    


    
    

      
      
      
      
      
      
      
      
      
      
    


    
    

      
📂 Where Do Your Files Live?

      
Every kubectl YAML goes into templates/. Nothing else changes about the YAML — yet. Here's the full chart layout:

      

        

          
📁 myapp/ new chart

          

            
📄 Chart.yaml chart name, version, description

            
⚙️ values.yaml all your variables in one place

            
🔍 values.schema.json validate required fields

            
🙈 .helmignore like .gitignore

            
📁 templates/

            

              
📄 deployment.yaml ← your existing file

              
📄 service.yaml ← your existing file

              
📄 ingress.yaml ← your existing file

              
📄 configmap.yaml ← your existing file

              
📄 hpa.yaml ← your existing file

              
🔧 _helpers.tpl common labels (new)

              
📢 NOTES.txt post-install message (new)

            

          

        

        

          

            
✅

            

              Your YAML files go in templates/
              
Copy your 5 files directly into myapp/templates/. They'll work as-is. You can then gradually replace hardcoded values with {{ .Values.xxx }} placeholders.

            

          

          

            
✅

            

              One chart = one application
              
All 5 resources belong to the same app — they go in the same chart. Helm manages them as a single unit: install all 5, upgrade all 5, rollback all 5 together.

            

          

          

            
❌

            

              Don't put secrets in templates/ or values.yaml
              
Passwords, API keys, tokens — never in the chart. Use helm-secrets + SOPS, or External Secrets Operator to pull from Vault / AWS Secrets Manager.

            

          

          

            
💡

            

              Initialise the chart structure with one command
              
helm create myapp
# Then replace the generated templates
# with your own YAML files

            

          

        

      

    


    
    

      
🚀 deployment.yaml — Before vs After

      
Replace hardcoded values with {{ .Values.xxx }} placeholders. Values come from values.yaml or --set on the command line.

      

        

          
❌ Before (kubectl)

          
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: myapp
  namespace: production
  labels:
    app: myapp
    version: v2.1.0
spec:
  replicas: 3
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: myapp
  template:
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: myapp
        image: registry.io/myapp:v2.1.0
        ports:
        - containerPort: 8080
        resources:
          requests:
            memory: "256Mi"
            cpu: "100m"
          limits:
            memory: "512Mi"
            cpu: "500m"

        

        
→

        

          
✅ After (Helm template)

          
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: {{ .Release.Name }}
  namespace: {{ .Release.Namespace }}
  labels:
    {{- include "myapp.labels" . | nindent 4 }}
spec:
  replicas: {{ .Values.replicaCount }}
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: {{ .Release.Name }}
  template:
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: {{ .Chart.Name }}
        image: "{{ .Values.image.repository }}:{{ .Values.image.tag }}"
        ports:
        - containerPort: {{ .Values.service.targetPort }}
        resources:
          {{- toYaml .Values.resources | nindent 10 }}

        

      

      
💡 {{ .Release.Name }} automatically uses whatever name you give in helm install <name> — so you can deploy multiple instances in different namespaces without editing the YAML.

    


    
    

      
🌐 service.yaml — Before vs After

      
Port and service type are the main variables here. Type can be ClusterIP in staging, LoadBalancer in prod — controlled by values.

      

        

          
❌ Before

          
apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: myapp-svc
spec:
  type: ClusterIP
  selector:
    app: myapp
  ports:
  - port: 80
    targetPort: 8080
    protocol: TCP

        

        
→

        

          
✅ After

          
apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: {{ .Release.Name }}
  labels:
    {{- include "myapp.labels" . | nindent 4 }}
spec:
  type: {{ .Values.service.type }}
  selector:
    app: {{ .Release.Name }}
  ports:
  - port: {{ .Values.service.port }}
    targetPort: {{ .Values.service.targetPort }}
    protocol: TCP

        

      

      
💡 Naming consistency: Use {{ .Release.Name }} for the Service name — the Deployment's selector also uses it, so they always match regardless of what name you pass to helm install.

    


    
    

      
🔀 ingress.yaml — Conditional + Parameterised

      
Ingress is optional — not every environment needs it. Wrap the whole file in {{- if .Values.ingress.enabled }} so it's only created when explicitly enabled.

      

        

          
❌ Before

          
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: Ingress
metadata:
  name: myapp-ingress
  annotations:
    nginx.ingress.../rewrite-target: /
spec:
  rules:
  - host: myapp.company.com
    http:
      paths:
      - path: /
        pathType: Prefix
        backend:
          service:
            name: myapp-svc
            port:
              number: 80

        

        
→

        

          
✅ After

          
{{- if .Values.ingress.enabled -}}
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: Ingress
metadata:
  name: {{ .Release.Name }}
  {{- with .Values.ingress.annotations }}
  annotations:
    {{- toYaml . | nindent 4 }}
  {{- end }}
spec:
  rules:
  - host: {{ .Values.ingress.host }}
    http:
      paths:
      - path: {{ .Values.ingress.path | default "/" }}
        pathType: Prefix
        backend:
          service:
            name: {{ .Release.Name }}
            port:
              number: {{ .Values.service.port }}
{{- end }}

        

      

      
💡 Default disabled: Set ingress.enabled: false in values.yaml as the default. Prod overrides enable it. This way staging never accidentally exposes the app publicly.

    


    
    

      
⚙️ configmap.yaml — Environment Config

      
ConfigMap data is just key-value config. Dump the entire data: block from .Values.config using toYaml — one line replaces many hardcoded entries.

      

        

          
❌ Before

          
apiVersion: v1
kind: ConfigMap
metadata:
  name: myapp-config
data:
  APP_ENV: "production"
  LOG_LEVEL: "info"
  DB_HOST: "postgres.internal"
  DB_PORT: "5432"
  CACHE_TTL: "300"
  FEATURE_X: "true"

        

        
→

        

          
✅ After

          
apiVersion: v1
kind: ConfigMap
metadata:
  name: {{ .Release.Name }}-config
  labels:
    {{- include "myapp.labels" . | nindent 4 }}
data:
  {{- toYaml .Values.config | nindent 2 }}

# In values.yaml:
# config:
#   APP_ENV: "production"
#   LOG_LEVEL: "info"
#   DB_HOST: "postgres.internal"
#   DB_PORT: "5432"
#   CACHE_TTL: "300"
#   FEATURE_X: "true"

        

      

      
💡 toYaml . | nindent 2 dumps the entire config map as YAML with 2-space indent. Add or remove config keys purely in values.yaml — the template never needs to change.

    


    
    

      
📈 hpa.yaml — Auto-scaling Config

      
HPA min/max replicas and CPU threshold differ per environment. Dev might be 1–2, prod 3–20. Wrap in a conditional — not every deployment needs autoscaling.

      

        

          
❌ Before

          
apiVersion: autoscaling/v2
kind: HorizontalPodAutoscaler
metadata:
  name: myapp-hpa
spec:
  scaleTargetRef:
    apiVersion: apps/v1
    kind: Deployment
    name: myapp
  minReplicas: 3
  maxReplicas: 20
  metrics:
  - type: Resource
    resource:
      name: cpu
      target:
        type: Utilization
        averageUtilization: 70

        

        
→

        

          
✅ After

          
{{- if .Values.autoscaling.enabled -}}
apiVersion: autoscaling/v2
kind: HorizontalPodAutoscaler
metadata:
  name: {{ .Release.Name }}-hpa
  labels:
    {{- include "myapp.labels" . | nindent 4 }}
spec:
  scaleTargetRef:
    apiVersion: apps/v1
    kind: Deployment
    name: {{ .Release.Name }}
  minReplicas: {{ .Values.autoscaling.minReplicas }}
  maxReplicas: {{ .Values.autoscaling.maxReplicas }}
  metrics:
  - type: Resource
    resource:
      name: cpu
      target:
        type: Utilization
        averageUtilization: {{ .Values.autoscaling.targetCPU }}
{{- end }}

        

      

      
💡 HPA + replicaCount conflict: When HPA is enabled, set replicaCount: 1 in values.yaml. The HPA takes over scaling — having a fixed replicaCount and HPA fight each other causes flapping.

    


    
    

      
⚙️ The Complete values.yaml

      
All hardcoded values from your 5 files, now in one file. Override per environment with -f values-prod.yaml.

      

        

          
YAMLvalues.yaml (defaults / dev)

          
# ── Image ──────────────────────────
image:
  repository: registry.io/myapp
  tag: "latest"        # override in CI
  pullPolicy: IfNotPresent

# ── Replicas ───────────────────────
replicaCount: 1

# ── Service ────────────────────────
service:
  type: ClusterIP
  port: 80
  targetPort: 8080

# ── Ingress ────────────────────────
ingress:
  enabled: false     # off by default
  host: ""
  path: "/"
  annotations: {}

# ── Config (→ ConfigMap) ───────────
config:
  APP_ENV: "development"
  LOG_LEVEL: "debug"
  DB_HOST: "localhost"
  CACHE_TTL: "60"
  FEATURE_X: "false"

# ── Resources ──────────────────────
resources:
  requests:
    memory: "128Mi"
    cpu: "50m"
  limits:
    memory: "256Mi"
    cpu: "200m"

# ── Autoscaling (→ HPA) ────────────
autoscaling:
  enabled: false
  minReplicas: 1
  maxReplicas: 5
  targetCPU: 70

        

        

          
YAMLvalues-prod.yaml (prod overrides only)

          
# Only override what's different in prod
# Helm merges this on top of values.yaml

image:
  tag: "v2.1.0"         # pinned version

replicaCount: 3

service:
  type: LoadBalancer

ingress:
  enabled: true
  host: "myapp.company.com"
  annotations:
    nginx.ingress.kubernetes.io/rewrite-target: "/"

config:
  APP_ENV: "production"
  LOG_LEVEL: "info"
  DB_HOST: "postgres.prod.internal"
  CACHE_TTL: "300"
  FEATURE_X: "true"

resources:
  requests:
    memory: "256Mi"
    cpu: "100m"
  limits:
    memory: "512Mi"
    cpu: "500m"

autoscaling:
  enabled: true
  minReplicas: 3
  maxReplicas: 20
  targetCPU: 70

        

      

    


    
    

      
🧩 Other Files You Need

      

        

          
📋

{{- end }}

{{- else }}

  svc/{{ .Release.Name }} \
  8080:{{ .Values.service.port }}
{{- end }}

🔒 What About Secrets?

        
Your configmap.yaml likely had a DB password or API key. That cannot go in values.yaml. Here are your options:

        

          

            1. helm-secrets + SOPS
            
Encrypt a secrets.yaml file. Git-safe, decrypted at deploy time.

            
helm secrets install myapp ./myapp \
  -f values-prod.yaml \
  -f secrets.enc.yaml

          

          

            2. --set in CI pipeline
            
Inject at deploy time from your CI secrets store. Simple but appears in CI logs.

            
helm upgrade --install myapp ./myapp \
  --set config.DB_PASSWORD=$DB_PASS

          

          

            3. External Secrets Operator
            
K8s operator that pulls secrets from Vault/AWS SM and creates K8s Secrets automatically.

            
# Chart just has an ExternalSecret CR
# ESO syncs the actual values

          

        

      

    


    
    

      
🚀 Deploy From Anywhere

      
Package your chart, push it to a registry, install from anywhere with a single command.


      

        

          
01

          

            Render & verify locally (no cluster needed)
            
helm template myapp ./myapp \
  -f values-prod.yaml

helm lint ./myapp -f values-prod.yaml

          

        

        

          
02

          

            Install to a cluster
            
# Dev (all defaults, no ingress, no HPA)
helm install myapp ./myapp

# Prod (override with prod values)
helm install myapp ./myapp \
  -f values-prod.yaml \
  --namespace production \
  --create-namespace \
  --atomic             # auto-rollback on failure

          

        

        

          
03

          

            Package the chart into a .tgz
            
helm package ./myapp
# Creates: myapp-1.0.0.tgz

          

        

        

          
04

          

            Push to an OCI registry (install from anywhere)
            
# Push to AWS ECR / GitHub / GCR / Harbor
helm push myapp-1.0.0.tgz \
  oci://registry.example.com/helm-charts

# Install from anywhere — no local files needed
helm install myapp \
  oci://registry.example.com/helm-charts/myapp \
  --version 1.0.0 \
  -f values-prod.yaml

          

        

        

          
05

          

            Upgrade (bump image tag, zero-downtime)
            
helm upgrade myapp \
  oci://registry.example.com/helm-charts/myapp \
  --version 1.1.0 \
  -f values-prod.yaml \
  --set image.tag=v2.2.0 \
  --atomic

# Something went wrong?
helm rollback myapp 1   # back to revision 1
helm history myapp      # see all revisions

          

        

      


      
      

        
📊 Before vs After — Full Comparison

        

          

            
Capabilitykubectl applyhelm install

            
              
Deploy all 5 resources5 separate commands1 command

              
Different config per envEdit files manually-f values-prod.yaml

              
RollbackRe-apply old files (manual)helm rollback myapp 1

              
Release historyNone (check git blame)helm history myapp

              
Share with another teamSend YAML files over SlackPush to OCI registry, share URL

              
Dry run--dry-run (no render)helm template + --dry-run

              
Conditional resources (HPA)Delete/apply manuallyautoscaling.enabled: true/false

            
          

        

      

    


    
    

      
🔍 Debug & Verify — When Things Go Wrong

      
Every beginner hits errors. Here's your debugging playbook for this exact chart.


      

        
🚨 The Most Common Errors

        

          

            
Error MessageCauseFix

            
              

                Error: INSTALLATION FAILED: cannot re-use a name that is still in use
                Release name already exists
                helm upgrade myapp ./myapp or helm uninstall myapp first
              

              

                Error: template: mychart/templates/...: function not defined
                Typo in template function name
                Run helm lint ./myapp — it will show the exact line
              

              

                CrashLoopBackOff after install
                App crashed — bad config or missing env var
                kubectl logs <pod-name> -n <namespace>
              

              

                ImagePullBackOff
                Wrong image tag in values.yaml
                Check image.tag value — fix and helm upgrade
              

              

                Error: UPGRADE FAILED: another operation is in progress
                Previous install/upgrade got stuck in pending state
                helm rollback myapp 0 to clear the pending state
              

              

                Error: rendered manifests contain a resource that already exists
                A resource was created outside Helm (raw kubectl apply)
                --force flag or delete the resource and reinstall
              

            
          

        

      


      
🔬 Debug Workflow — Step by Step

      

        

          
D1

          

            Before installing — validate your chart and values
            
# Lint checks YAML syntax and chart structure
helm lint ./myapp
helm lint ./myapp -f values-prod.yaml   # test with prod values

# Dry-run renders templates WITHOUT connecting to cluster
helm template myapp ./myapp -f values-prod.yaml

# Full dry-run: renders AND validates against cluster API
helm install myapp ./myapp \
  -f values-prod.yaml \
  --dry-run --debug \
  --namespace production

          

        

        

          
D2

          

            After installing — verify resources were created
            
# Check release status
helm status myapp -n production

# See all K8s resources this release owns
helm get manifest myapp -n production

# List all releases across namespaces
helm list -A

# Check pods are running
kubectl get pods -n production -l app.kubernetes.io/instance=myapp

          

        

        

          
D3

          

            Pod not starting? Investigate the logs
            
# Get pod name
kubectl get pods -n production
# → myapp-7d8f9-abc12  0/1  CrashLoopBackOff

# Read the crash logs
kubectl logs myapp-7d8f9-abc12 -n production
kubectl logs myapp-7d8f9-abc12 -n production --previous  # last crash

# Describe pod to see events (pull errors, OOM, etc.)
kubectl describe pod myapp-7d8f9-abc12 -n production

          

        

        

          
D4

          

            Check exactly what values Helm used for this release
            
# See computed values (after merging all overrides)
helm get values myapp -n production
helm get values myapp -n production --all   # include defaults

# See the rendered manifests Helm actually applied
helm get manifest myapp -n production

          

        

        

          
D5

          

            Fix → upgrade → verify loop
            
# Fix values-prod.yaml or template, then:
helm upgrade myapp ./myapp \
  -f values-prod.yaml \
  --atomic \
  --timeout 3m \
  --namespace production

# Check revision history
helm history myapp -n production

# If upgrade failed, rollback to last working revision
helm rollback myapp -n production  # rolls back to previous rev

          

        

      


      

        
🎖️ SRE Debugging Golden Rule

        
Always debug in this order: 1) helm lint → 2) helm template --dry-run → 3) helm install --dry-run --debug → 4) kubectl logs → 5) kubectl describe. 90% of all Helm failures are caught in the first 3 steps before a single pod is created.

      

    

  






  

    
🔄 ArgoCD Deep Dive

    
Master ArgoCD & GitOps

    
From git commit to deployed pod — the declarative, self-healing way to run Kubernetes in production. No more kubectl apply from CI pipelines.

    

      Start Learning →
      Try Simulations

1Source of Truth

    

  

  

    
Your ArgoCD Journey
