Module 01

  
What is AWS ADOT?

  
From "something is broken" to "here's exactly why" — ADOT is the observability layer every production system needs.


  

    
🧠 ELI5 — The Black Box Problem

    
Imagine your production system is a black box. Users say it's slow. Your boss says fix it. But you have no idea WHAT is slow, WHERE the bottleneck is, or WHY it started happening after last night's deployment. ADOT installs sensors everywhere inside that box — on every function call, every database query, every HTTP request. Now you can see exactly which database query took 258ms, which user's request triggered it, and that it started happening at exactly 14:32 — right after your migration ran.

  


  

    
🌳 Two Projects, One Tool

    

      

        OpenTelemetry (OTel)

        The open standard — a CNCF project (same home as Kubernetes, Prometheus). Vendor-neutral. Defines how observability data is collected and transmitted. Any vendor can implement it.
        
✅ Free, open source, community-driven

      

      

        AWS Distro for OTel (ADOT)

        AWS's hardened, enterprise-supported distribution of OpenTelemetry. Pre-configured for AWS services (X-Ray, CloudWatch, AMP). Tested, signed, and supported by AWS. Like Amazon Linux is to Linux.
        
✅ AWS-integrated, enterprise supported, security patched

      

    

    

      💡 DevOps Analogy: OpenTelemetry is like Kubernetes — the open standard. ADOT is like EKS — AWS's managed, supported version. You get all the features plus AWS integrations and enterprise support.
    

  


  

    
⚡ Before ADOT / After ADOT

    

      

        
❌ Before: Tool Sprawl

        
Separate tools for each signal. All with different configs, different agents, different dashboards. Every new service needs to be wired up 3 separate times.

        

          Prometheus Agent → metrics

          Jaeger Agent → traces

          Fluent Bit → logs

          StatsD → custom metrics

          AWS X-Ray SDK → AWS traces

          5 different configs per service
        

      

      

        
✅ After: One Collector

        
One ADOT Collector handles all three signals. One config file. One deployment. Automatically routes to the right AWS backend.

        

          ADOT Collector ─→ X-Ray (traces)

          ADOT Collector ─→ CloudWatch (metrics)

          ADOT Collector ─→ AMP (Prometheus)

          ADOT Collector ─→ OpenSearch (logs)

          1 config for all signals
        

      

    

  


  

    
📅 Timeline — How We Got Here

    

      
2016–2018
Observability chaos: every vendor had their own agent. OpenCensus (Google) and OpenTracing (community) were two competing standards. Teams had to pick sides.

      
2019
OpenCensus + OpenTracing merge to create OpenTelemetry. CNCF adopts it. One standard to rule them all — traces, metrics, and eventually logs.

      
2020
AWS announces AWS Distro for OpenTelemetry (ADOT) in preview. AWS starts contributing heavily to the OpenTelemetry project.

      
2021
ADOT goes GA. AWS adds native integrations with X-Ray, CloudWatch, and Amazon Managed Service for Prometheus. EKS add-on support.

      
2023+
ADOT becomes the recommended observability approach for AWS workloads. Full support for all three signals (metrics, traces, logs) via OTel 1.0 stable spec.

    

  


  

    
✅ Six Reasons ADOT Wins

    

      
🏗️ Unified Pipeline
One collector, one config, all three signals. No more Frankenstein stacks.

      
☁️ AWS-Native
Pre-wired to X-Ray, CloudWatch, AMP. No custom exporter code needed.

      
🔒 Enterprise Ready
AWS-tested, security-patched, and SLA-backed. No community-only support.

      
🌍 Vendor-Neutral
Data in OpenTelemetry format. Switch backends (Datadog → Grafana) without re-instrumenting.

      
⚡ Auto-Instrumentation
Zero code changes for common frameworks (FastAPI, Django, Redis, SQLAlchemy, gRPC).

      
🧩 Extensible
Custom receivers, processors, exporters. Write your own pipeline logic when needed.

    

  







  
Module 02

  
Core Concepts — The Three Signals

  
Everything in observability boils down to three types of data. Master these and you can understand any monitoring system.


  

    
🚗 ELI5 — Your Car's Instrument Cluster

    
Metrics are like your car's gauges — speed (requests/sec), fuel (memory %), temperature (CPU %). Numbers that change over time. Traces are like GPS history — the exact path your package took from warehouse → truck → your door, with timestamps. Logs are like the driver's logbook — "14:32: left warehouse. 15:10: traffic jam on I-95. 15:45: delivered." Together they give you the full picture of what your system is doing.

  


  
  

    
📊 The Three Signals

    

      

        
NUMBERS OVER TIME

        
📈 Metrics

        
What: Numerical measurements sampled at intervals. Tells you how much and how fast.

        
DevOps examples:

        
    
          
  • CPU: 78%, Memory: 4.2GB
    • 
          
  • Requests/sec: 1,250 r/s
    • 
          
  • P99 latency: 45ms
    • 
          
  • Error rate: 0.3%
    • 
          
  • Active DB connections: 42
    • 
        

        
⚡ Answer: "How bad is it right now?"

      

      

        
REQUEST JOURNEY

        
🔍 Traces

        
What: The complete journey of one request through all services. Tells you where time was spent.

        
DevOps examples:

        
    
          
  • User clicked "Diagnose" → API Gateway → FastAPI → LangGraph → Redis → Postgres → Response
    • 
          
  • Total: 342ms. Postgres: 258ms (slow!)
    • 
          
  • Shows parent-child span relationships
    • 
        

        
⚡ Answer: "Which step is causing the slowness?"

      

      

        
TIMESTAMPED EVENTS

        
📝 Logs

        
What: Timestamped text records of discrete events. Tells you what happened.

        
DevOps examples:

        
    
          
  • 14:32 ERROR DB connection timeout
    • 
          
  • 14:32 WARN Retrying query (attempt 2/3)
    • 
          
  • 14:33 INFO Deployment abc1234 started
    • 
        

        
⚡ Answer: "What exactly happened at that moment?"

      

    

  


  

    
🎯 Traces Deep Dive — Spans Explained

    

      ELI5: A Trace is a relay race. The full race = the trace. Each runner's leg = a span. Every span knows who passed it the baton (parent span) and how long it ran. The trace ID is the race number that stays the same across all runners.
    

    
Trace ID: abc-123-xyz-456   (one unique ID for the entire request)
│
├── Span: GET /api/sre/diagnose  [0ms → 342ms]  ← root span
│     service: api-gateway
│     trace_id: abc-123-xyz-456
│     span_id: span-001
│
├── Span: sre_diagnose handler   [8ms → 333ms]  ← child of root
│     service: fastapi-app
│     parent_span_id: span-001
│
│   ├── Span: redis.get          [30ms → 34ms]  ← cache miss
│   │     db.system: redis
│   │     db.statement: GET cache:diag:alert-42
│   │     cache.hit: false
│   │
│   ├── Span: db.query           [36ms → 294ms] ← SLOW: 258ms!
│   │     db.system: postgresql
│   │     db.statement: SELECT * FROM events WHERE team_id=?
│   │     db.rows_returned: 50000
│   │     ↑ Full table scan — missing index detected here
│   │
│   └── Span: langgraph.reason   [297ms → 325ms]
│         agent.steps: 4
│         agent.model: claude-sonnet-4-6

  


  

    
🌐 Context Propagation — How Traces Cross Service Boundaries

    

      

        
When FastAPI calls Redis or Postgres, how does the trace know it's still the same request? The answer is Context Propagation — the trace ID is passed as an HTTP header (W3C TraceContext standard).

        
# Header automatically injected by ADOT SDK:
traceparent: 00-abc123xyz456-span001-01
#             ^^ trace-id       ^^ span-id

# Receiving service reads the header:
# "I'm part of trace abc123xyz456"
# → creates a child span with parent=span001

      

      

        DevOps Analogy: VIP Wristband 🎫

        At a festival, your VIP wristband stays with you all day. Security at each stage scans it to know you belong there and logs your visit. The wristband is your trace ID — it travels with every request across every service.


        What gets propagated automatically:
        
    
          
  • Trace ID (unique per user request)
    • 
          
  • Span ID (the "current" step)
    • 
          
  • Sampling decision (don't re-decide mid-trace)
    • 
          
  • Baggage (custom key-value pairs)
    • 
        

      

    

  


  

    
🏷️ Attributes — Metadata that Makes Traces Searchable

    
Attributes are key-value pairs attached to spans. They make your traces filterable and searchable in X-Ray and other backends.

    

      

        
Standard Semantic Conventions (auto-added by SDK)

        
http.method: "POST"
http.url: "/api/sre/diagnose"
http.status_code: 200
db.system: "postgresql"
db.name: "sre_autopilot"
db.statement: "SELECT * FROM events..."
service.name: "sre-autopilot"
service.version: "1.2.3"
k8s.pod.name: "fastapi-app-7d9b-xk2p"
k8s.namespace.name: "production"

      

      

        
Custom Business Attributes (you add these)

        
alert.type: "cpu_spike"
alert.severity: "critical"
team.name: "platform-sre"
diagnosis.found: true
diagnosis.root_cause: "missing_index"
langgraph.steps: 4
langgraph.model: "claude-sonnet-4-6"
customer.tier: "enterprise"

      

    

  







  
Module 03

  
ADOT Architecture — The Collector Pipeline

  
The ADOT Collector is the central hub. Everything flows through it — receive data, process it, export to AWS backends.


  

    
🏭 ELI5 — The Logistics Hub

    
The ADOT Collector is like an Amazon fulfillment center. Packages arrive from many carriers (receivers). Inside, they're sorted, labeled, consolidated, and some are inspected (processors). Then they're shipped to specific destinations — X-Ray gets traces, CloudWatch gets metrics, S3 gets logs (exporters). The warehouse doesn't care what's in the packages — it just routes them.

  


  

    
🏗️ Full Architecture Diagram

    
YOUR APPLICATION SERVICES
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│  FastAPI App     Redis Client     Postgres ORM           │
│  (auto-instrumented via ADOT Python SDK)                 │
│  ↓ OTLP gRPC (port 4317)  ↓ OTLP HTTP (port 4318)      │
└────────────────────┬────────────────────────────────────┘
                     │
        ┌────────────▼──────────────────────────┐
        │       ADOT COLLECTOR                  │
        │  ┌──────────────────────────────────┐ │
        │  │  RECEIVERS                       │ │
        │  │  • OTLP (gRPC + HTTP)            │ │
        │  │  • Prometheus scrape             │ │
        │  │  • AWS Container Insights        │ │
        │  │  • Fluent Bit (logs)             │ │
        │  └──────────────┬───────────────────┘ │
        │                 │                      │
        │  ┌──────────────▼───────────────────┐ │
        │  │  PROCESSORS (in pipeline order)  │ │
        │  │  • Batch (buffer 1024 spans)     │ │
        │  │  • Probabilistic Sampler (10%)   │ │
        │  │  • Resource (add k8s metadata)   │ │
        │  │  • Filter (drop health checks)   │ │
        │  └──────────────┬───────────────────┘ │
        │                 │                      │
        │  ┌──────────────▼───────────────────┐ │
        │  │  EXPORTERS                       │ │
        │  │  • AWS X-Ray   → traces          │ │
        │  │  • AWS EMF     → CloudWatch      │ │
        │  │  • Prometheus  → AMP (Grafana)   │ │
        │  │  • OTLP        → any backend     │ │
        │  └──────────────────────────────────┘ │
        └───────────────────────────────────────┘

  


  

    
🧩 Receivers, Processors, Exporters — The Three Component Types

    

      

        
📥 Receivers
Data comes IN

        
    
          
  • otlp — standard OpenTelemetry (gRPC/HTTP)
    • 
          
  • prometheus — scrapes /metrics endpoints
    • 
          
  • awsecscontainermetrics — auto-collects ECS task/container stats
    • 
          
  • awscontainerinsightreceiver — EKS node/pod metrics
    • 
          
  • filelog — tail log files
    • 
          
  • kafka — consume from Kafka topics
    • 
        

      

      

        
⚙️ Processors
Data gets TRANSFORMED

        
    
          
  • batch — buffer data before exporting (efficiency)
    • 
          
  • probabilistic_sampler — keep X% of traces (cost control)
    • 
          
  • filter — drop health-check spans, noisy metrics
    • 
          
  • resource — add/change attributes (env, team, region)
    • 
          
  • attributes — rename or delete span attributes
    • 
          
  • memory_limiter — prevent OOM in the collector
    • 
        

      

      

        
📤 Exporters
Data goes OUT

        
    
          
  • awsxray — sends traces to AWS X-Ray
    • 
          
  • awsemf — CloudWatch metrics (EMF format)
    • 
          
  • prometheusremotewrite — sends to AMP
    • 
          
  • awss3 — archive telemetry to S3
    • 
          
  • otlp — forward to any OTLP backend
    • 
          
  • logging — debug: print to stdout
    • 
        

      

    

  


  

    
📄 Complete Collector Config for SRE Auto-Pilot

    
# adot-collector-config.yaml
receivers:
  otlp:
    protocols:
      grpc: { endpoint: 0.0.0.0:4317 }  # Used by apps
      http: { endpoint: 0.0.0.0:4318 }  # Used by Lambda
  awscontainerinsightreceiver:           # Auto: EKS node+pod metrics
    collection_interval: 30s
  prometheus:                            # Scrape FastAPI /metrics
    config:
      scrape_configs:
        - job_name: fastapi-sre
          static_configs:
            - targets: ['localhost:8000']

processors:
  batch:
    timeout: 5s
    send_batch_size: 1024     # Buffer before exporting (reduces API calls)
  probabilistic_sampler:
    hash_seed: 42
    sampling_percentage: 10   # Keep 10% of traces in high-traffic (save cost)
  resource:
    attributes:               # Add metadata to ALL telemetry
      - key: environment  value: "production"         action: upsert
      - key: service.team value: "platform-sre"      action: upsert
  filter/drop-health:         # Remove noisy health-check traces
    traces:
      span:
        - 'attributes["http.target"] == "/health"'

exporters:
  awsxray:
    region: us-east-1         # Traces → AWS X-Ray
  awsemf:
    namespace: SREAutopilot
    region: us-east-1         # Metrics → CloudWatch in EMF format
    log_group_name: /aws/sre-autopilot/metrics
  prometheusremotewrite:
    endpoint: "https://aps-workspaces.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/workspaces/ws-xxx/api/v1/remote_write"
    auth: { authenticator: sigv4auth }  # Metrics → Amazon Managed Prometheus

service:
  pipelines:
    traces:
      receivers: [otlp]
      processors: [batch, probabilistic_sampler, resource, filter/drop-health]
      exporters: [awsxray]
    metrics:
      receivers: [otlp, awscontainerinsightreceiver, prometheus]
      processors: [batch, resource]
      exporters: [awsemf, prometheusremotewrite]

  







  
Module 04

  
Installation — EKS, ECS, Lambda, EC2

  
Deploy the ADOT Collector as a DaemonSet on EKS, a sidecar on ECS, a Layer on Lambda, or a systemd service on EC2.


  

    
🚀 Choose Your Platform

    

      
      
      
      
    


    

      
EKS DaemonSet — runs one Collector pod per node. Every application pod sends telemetry to the local node's Collector over localhost. Best for: all pods on EKS.

      
# Option 1: EKS Add-on (recommended — AWS manages the version)
aws eks create-addon \
  --cluster-name sre-autopilot-cluster \
  --addon-name adot \
  --addon-version v0.94.1-eksbuild.1

# Option 2: Helm chart (more control)
helm repo add open-telemetry https://open-telemetry.github.io/opentelemetry-helm-charts
helm repo update

helm upgrade --install adot-collector open-telemetry/opentelemetry-collector \
  --namespace adot \
  --create-namespace \
  --set mode=daemonset \
  --set config.exporters.awsxray.region=us-east-1 \
  --values adot-collector-config.yaml

# Verify collector is running on every node
kubectl get pods -n adot -o wide
# NAME                    READY   STATUS    NODE
# adot-collector-7xk2p   1/1     Running   ip-10-0-1-42.ec2.internal
# adot-collector-9nmqr   1/1     Running   ip-10-0-2-18.ec2.internal

      
Your app sends to http://localhost:4317 (same node) — zero network hop, highest performance.

    


    

      
ECS Sidecar — ADOT Collector runs as a sidecar container in the same task as your app. App sends to localhost:4317.

      
# task-definition.json (add this container alongside your app)
{
  "family": "sre-autopilot",
  "containerDefinitions": [
    {
      "name": "fastapi-app",
      "image": "ghcr.io/myorg/sre-autopilot:latest",
      "environment": [
        {"name": "OTEL_EXPORTER_OTLP_ENDPOINT", "value": "http://localhost:4317"}
      ]
    },
    {
      "name": "adot-collector",
      "image": "public.ecr.aws/aws-observability/aws-otel-collector:latest",
      "command": ["--config=/etc/ecs/ecs-default-config.yaml"],
      "logConfiguration": {
        "logDriver": "awslogs",
        "options": {
          "awslogs-group": "/ecs/adot-collector",
          "awslogs-region": "us-east-1"
        }
      }
    }
  ],
  "taskRoleArn": "arn:aws:iam::123456789:role/adot-task-role"
}

    


    

      
Lambda Layer — ADOT runs as a Lambda Layer. Zero sidecar needed — the layer instruments your function before the handler runs.

      
# 1. Add the ADOT Lambda Layer (ARN varies by region + runtime)
aws lambda update-function-configuration \
  --function-name sre-alert-processor \
  --layers \
    arn:aws:lambda:us-east-1:901920570463:layer:aws-otel-python-amd64-ver-1-25-0:1 \
  --environment Variables='{
    "AWS_LAMBDA_EXEC_WRAPPER": "/opt/otel-instrument",
    "OPENTELEMETRY_COLLECTOR_CONFIG_URI": "/var/task/collector.yaml"
  }'

# 2. Add collector.yaml to your Lambda deployment package
# (same format as EKS config, but use otlp exporter to X-Ray endpoint)

# 3. Python handler — zero code changes needed!
def handler(event, context):
    # ADOT auto-instruments boto3, requests, etc.
    result = diagnose_alert(event)  # spans created automatically
    return result

    


    

      
EC2 systemd service — run ADOT as a system daemon on bare EC2 instances.

      
# Install the ADOT Collector
wget https://aws-otel-collector.s3.amazonaws.com/amazon_linux/amd64/latest/aws-otel-collector.rpm
sudo rpm -Uvh aws-otel-collector.rpm

# Configure it
sudo cat > /opt/aws/aws-otel-collector/etc/config.yaml << 'EOF'
# paste your collector config here
EOF

# Enable and start
sudo systemctl enable aws-otel-collector
sudo systemctl start aws-otel-collector

# Verify
sudo systemctl status aws-otel-collector
sudo tail -f /var/log/aws-otel-collector.log

    

  


  

    
🐍 Instrument Your FastAPI App — Zero-Change Auto-Instrumentation

    
# requirements.txt
opentelemetry-distro[otlp]==0.46b0     # Core OTel + OTLP exporter
opentelemetry-instrumentation-fastapi  # Auto: all routes become spans
opentelemetry-instrumentation-redis    # Auto: all Redis calls become spans
opentelemetry-instrumentation-sqlalchemy  # Auto: all SQL queries become spans
opentelemetry-instrumentation-httpx    # Auto: all outgoing HTTP calls

# Install and auto-configure instruments
opentelemetry-bootstrap --action=install

    
# main.py — add BEFORE your FastAPI app code
from opentelemetry import trace
from opentelemetry.instrumentation.fastapi import FastAPIInstrumentor
from opentelemetry.instrumentation.redis import RedisInstrumentor
from opentelemetry.instrumentation.sqlalchemy import SQLAlchemyInstrumentor
from opentelemetry.exporter.otlp.proto.grpc.trace_exporter import OTLPSpanExporter
from opentelemetry.sdk.trace import TracerProvider
from opentelemetry.sdk.trace.export import BatchSpanProcessor
from opentelemetry.sdk.resources import Resource

# Step 1: Describe your service (appears in X-Ray and CloudWatch)
resource = Resource.create({
    "service.name":         "sre-autopilot",
    "service.version":      os.getenv("IMAGE_TAG", "unknown"),
    "deployment.environment": "production",
})

# Step 2: Create the tracer pipeline (sends to ADOT Collector on localhost)
provider = TracerProvider(resource=resource)
provider.add_span_processor(
    BatchSpanProcessor(OTLPSpanExporter(
        endpoint="http://localhost:4317",   # ADOT Collector
        insecure=True
    ))
)
trace.set_tracer_provider(provider)

# Step 3: Auto-instrument all libraries (one line each!)
FastAPIInstrumentor().instrument()   # Every route = span automatically
RedisInstrumentor().instrument()     # Every Redis call = span
SQLAlchemyInstrumentor().instrument() # Every SQL query = span

# Step 4: Custom spans for your business logic
tracer = trace.get_tracer("sre-autopilot")

async def diagnose_incident(alert: dict):
    with tracer.start_as_current_span("langgraph.diagnose") as span:
        span.set_attribute("alert.type",     alert["type"])
        span.set_attribute("alert.severity", alert["severity"])
        result = await langgraph_agent.ainvoke(alert)
        span.set_attribute("diagnosis.found",      bool(result["root_cause"]))
        span.set_attribute("diagnosis.root_cause", result.get("root_cause", ""))
        return result

    

      💡 SRE Tip: Start with just FastAPIInstrumentor in dev. You'll immediately see every route, its latency, and its HTTP status in X-Ray — zero other changes needed.
    

  


  

    
✅ Verify Your Setup in 60 Seconds

    
# 1. Send a test trace to the local collector
curl -X POST http://localhost:4318/v1/traces \
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
  -d '{"resourceSpans": [{"resource": {"attributes": [{"key":"service.name","value":{"stringValue":"test"}}]}}]}'

# 2. Check collector is receiving data
kubectl logs -n adot daemonset/adot-collector | grep "Everything is ready"

# 3. Check AWS X-Ray console — traces should appear within 30 seconds
aws xray get-trace-summaries \
  --start-time $(date -d '5 minutes ago' +%s) \
  --end-time $(date +%s) \
  --query 'TraceSummaries[*].Id'

# 4. Check CloudWatch for metrics
aws cloudwatch list-metrics --namespace SREAutopilot

  







  
Module 05

  
What to Monitor — The Complete Checklist

  
From the RED method to custom business metrics — here's exactly what to instrument and why.


  

    
🔴 The RED Method — For Every Service

    
Coined by Tom Wilkie at Weave. These three metrics tell you if a service is healthy. If any of these is wrong, users are unhappy.

    

      

        
🔴 R — Rate

        How many requests per second?
        
    
          
  • HTTP requests/sec per route
    • 
          
  • DB queries/sec
    • 
          
  • Cache operations/sec
    • 
        

        
rate(http_requests_total[5m])

      

      

        
🟢 E — Errors

        How many requests are failing?
        
    
          
  • HTTP 5xx responses
    • 
          
  • Unhandled exceptions
    • 
          
  • DB timeouts and errors
    • 
        

        
rate(http_errors_total[5m])
  / rate(http_requests_total[5m])

      

    

    

      

        
🔴 D — Duration

        How long do requests take?
        
    
          
  • P50 (median) latency
    • 
          
  • P95 latency (most users)
    • 
          
  • P99 latency (worst 1%)
    • 
        

        
histogram_quantile(0.99,
  rate(http_duration_bucket[5m]))

      

      

        
💡 SRE Alert Targets

        

          
Error Rate:

          
Alert if > 1% over 5 minutes

          
P99 Latency:

          
Alert if > 500ms over 5 minutes

          
Request Rate drop:

          
Alert if drops > 30% from baseline (traffic lost)

        

      

    

  


  

    
🔵 The USE Method — For Every Resource

    
Brendan Gregg's method. Apply to every infrastructure resource: CPU, memory, disk, network, DB connections.

    

      

        
ResourceUtilizationSaturationErrors

        
          
CPU% used per coreRun queue length > nCPUsThrottling events

          
Memory% of total usedSwap usage > 0OOM kills

          
Disk% of IOPS usedI/O wait > 10%Read/write errors

          
DB ConnectionsActive / max connectionsQueue depth > 0Timeout errors

          
NetworkBandwidth % of capacityDropped packets > 0TCP retransmits

        
      

    

  


  

    
📋 Full Monitoring Checklist for SRE Auto-Pilot

    

      

        
Application Layer (FastAPI)

        

          
✅ Traces: every route (auto via FastAPIInstrumentor)

          
✅ Traces: all DB queries (auto via SQLAlchemyInstrumentor)

          
✅ Traces: Redis calls (auto via RedisInstrumentor)

          
✅ Traces: LangGraph agent steps (manual spans)

          
✅ Metrics: request rate, error rate, P99 latency (auto)

          
✅ Metrics: agent diagnosis success rate (custom)

        

      

      

        
Infrastructure Layer (EKS)

        

          
✅ Metrics: pod CPU + memory (auto via Container Insights)

          
✅ Metrics: node disk I/O and network (auto)

          
✅ Metrics: Postgres connections + query time

          
✅ Metrics: Redis hit rate + evictions

          
✅ Logs: structured JSON logs correlated to trace IDs

          
✅ Logs: LangGraph agent decision trail

        

      

    

  


  

    
🔗 Log Correlation — Connect Logs to Traces

    
Add the trace_id to every log line. Then in CloudWatch or OpenSearch, you can jump from any log entry directly to the full distributed trace.

    
import logging
from opentelemetry import trace

class TraceIdFilter(logging.Filter):
    """Inject trace_id and span_id into every log record."""
    def filter(self, record):
        ctx = trace.get_current_span().get_span_context()
        record.trace_id = format(ctx.trace_id, '032x') if ctx.is_valid else 'no-trace'
        record.span_id  = format(ctx.span_id,  '016x') if ctx.is_valid else 'no-span'
        return True

# Configure structured JSON logging
logging.basicConfig(format='{"ts":"%(asctime)s","level":"%(levelname)s",'
                            '"msg":"%(message)s","trace_id":"%(trace_id)s",'
                            '"span_id":"%(span_id)s"}')
logging.getLogger().addFilter(TraceIdFilter())

# Now every log line includes the trace ID:
# {"ts":"14:32:05","level":"ERROR","msg":"DB timeout",
#  "trace_id":"abc123xyz456","span_id":"span007"}
# → Click trace_id in CloudWatch → opens X-Ray trace instantly

  







  
Module 06 · Key

  
AWS Integrations — Where Your Data Goes

  
ADOT routes each signal type to the AWS service purpose-built for it. Here's what each service does and when to use it.


  

    
🗺️ Signal → AWS Service Routing

    
TRACES  ──→  AWS X-Ray             (distributed trace UI, service map, anomaly detection)
METRICS ──→  Amazon CloudWatch        (dashboards, alarms, Container Insights)
METRICS ──→  Amazon Managed Prometheus  (PromQL queries, long-term storage)
METRICS ──→  Amazon Managed Grafana    (beautiful dashboards over AMP + CloudWatch)
LOGS    ──→  Amazon CloudWatch Logs    (full-text search, log insights)
LOGS    ──→  Amazon OpenSearch Service (complex queries, ML-based anomaly detection)

  


  

    
☁️ AWS Service Deep Dives

    

      

        
🔍 AWS X-Ray

        
Distributed tracing — the "GPS history" of your requests. Shows service maps, end-to-end latency breakdowns, and identifies exactly which database query caused the slowdown.

        
Use it for: "Find all requests that took >500ms and show me which service was slow."

        
Signal: Traces

      

      

        
📊 Amazon CloudWatch

        
Metrics, logs, and alarms. Container Insights automatically collects EKS/ECS pod metrics. EMF format lets you publish custom metrics with dimensions from your app.

        
Use it for: "Alert me when P99 latency > 500ms for 5 minutes on the production namespace."

        
Signal: Metrics + Logs

      

      

        
📈 Amazon Managed Prometheus (AMP)

        
Fully managed Prometheus-compatible service. Use PromQL to query metrics. Stores 150 days of data by default. No Prometheus server to manage.

        
Use it for: "Show me the p99 latency over the last 30 days, grouped by service version."

        
Signal: Metrics

      

      

        
📉 Amazon Managed Grafana (AMG)

        
Fully managed Grafana. Connects to AMP, CloudWatch, X-Ray as data sources. Pre-built dashboards for EKS, ECS, Lambda. Teams get SSO via AWS IAM Identity Center.

        
Use it for: "Build a unified SRE dashboard showing traces, metrics, and logs in one pane."

        
Signal: All (UI layer)

      

      

        
🔎 Amazon OpenSearch Service

        
Managed OpenSearch (Elasticsearch). Best for log analytics at scale. Built-in ML anomaly detection. Can also store traces with OpenTelemetry Trace plugin.

        
Use it for: "Find all ERROR logs from the last hour that contain 'timeout' and correlate with traces."

        
Signal: Logs + Traces

      

      

        
🔔 Amazon CloudWatch Alarms

        
Alert when metrics cross thresholds. Supports composite alarms (alert only if BOTH CPU is high AND error rate is up — reduces false positives). Integrates with PagerDuty via SNS.

        
Use it for: "Page on-call when error rate >1% AND P99 latency >500ms at the same time."

        
Signal: Metrics

      

    

  


  

    
🔍 X-Ray Features That Change How You Debug

    

      

        Service Map — Auto-generated diagram of all services, their connections, and health status. Red nodes = unhealthy. Click any node to see its traces. Zero configuration needed.
      

      

        Trace Analytics — Filter all traces by any attribute: service.name = "postgres" AND duration > 100. Find the slowest 1% of traces instantly. No Jaeger Query Language needed.
      

      

        X-Ray Insights — ML-powered anomaly detection. X-Ray learns your baseline latency distribution and alerts when traces deviate significantly. No threshold tuning needed.
      

      

        CloudWatch ServiceLens — Unified view combining X-Ray traces, CloudWatch metrics, and logs in one dashboard. Click on a slow service → see its metric charts → click on a spike → see the exact trace.
      

    

  


  

    
💰 Cost Optimization — What to Sample and What to Keep

    

      

        
SignalRecommendationWhy

        
          
TracesSample 5-10% at high traffic, 100% at low trafficX-Ray charges per trace. Most traces are identical — sampling catches the outliers that matter.

          
Errors / slow spansAlways 100% (use tail-based sampling)Never lose a trace that represents a failure. Use probabilistic sampler + tail sampler together.

          
MetricsKeep all, but use EMF dimensions carefullyCloudWatch charges per unique metric stream. Each dimension combination = new metric.

          
LogsFilter health-check and debug logs in productionCloudWatch Logs charges per GB ingested. Drop /health and /ready endpoint logs at the collector.

adot:
  enabled: true
  image: public.ecr.aws/aws-observability/aws-otel-collector:latest
  region: us-east-1
  xrayRegion: us-east-1
  cloudwatchNamespace: SREAutopilot
  prometheusWorkspaceId: ws-abc123def456

🐍 Step 2 — Instrument FastAPI (Complete)

    
# sre_autopilot/telemetry.py — new file
"""ADOT telemetry setup for SRE Auto-Pilot."""
import os, logging
from opentelemetry import trace, metrics
from opentelemetry.instrumentation.fastapi    import FastAPIInstrumentor
from opentelemetry.instrumentation.redis      import RedisInstrumentor
from opentelemetry.instrumentation.sqlalchemy import SQLAlchemyInstrumentor
from opentelemetry.instrumentation.httpx      import HTTPXClientInstrumentor
from opentelemetry.exporter.otlp.proto.grpc.trace_exporter  import OTLPSpanExporter
from opentelemetry.exporter.otlp.proto.grpc.metric_exporter import OTLPMetricExporter
from opentelemetry.sdk.trace   import TracerProvider
from opentelemetry.sdk.metrics import MeterProvider
from opentelemetry.sdk.trace.export   import BatchSpanProcessor
from opentelemetry.sdk.metrics.export import PeriodicExportingMetricReader
from opentelemetry.sdk.resources import Resource

COLLECTOR = os.getenv("OTEL_EXPORTER_OTLP_ENDPOINT", "http://localhost:4317")

def setup_telemetry(app=None):
    """Call once at startup — instruments everything."""
    resource = Resource.create({
        "service.name":             "sre-autopilot",
        "service.version":          os.getenv("IMAGE_TAG", "dev"),
        "deployment.environment":   os.getenv("ENV", "dev"),
        "k8s.namespace.name":       os.getenv("POD_NAMESPACE", "default"),
        "k8s.pod.name":             os.getenv("POD_NAME", "local"),
    })

    # ── Traces ──────────────────────────────────────────────
    tracer_provider = TracerProvider(resource=resource)
    tracer_provider.add_span_processor(
        BatchSpanProcessor(OTLPSpanExporter(endpoint=COLLECTOR, insecure=True))
    )
    trace.set_tracer_provider(tracer_provider)

    # ── Metrics ─────────────────────────────────────────────
    reader = PeriodicExportingMetricReader(
        OTLPMetricExporter(endpoint=COLLECTOR, insecure=True),
        export_interval_millis=15000   # every 15 seconds
    )
    meter_provider = MeterProvider(resource=resource, metric_readers=[reader])
    metrics.set_meter_provider(meter_provider)

    # ── Auto-instrument libraries ────────────────────────────
    if app: FastAPIInstrumentor.instrument_app(app)
    RedisInstrumentor().instrument()
    SQLAlchemyInstrumentor().instrument()
    HTTPXClientInstrumentor().instrument()

    return trace.get_tracer("sre-autopilot")

    
# main.py
from fastapi import FastAPI
from sre_autopilot.telemetry import setup_telemetry

app = FastAPI(title="SRE Auto-Pilot")
tracer = setup_telemetry(app)  # ← one line wires up everything

# Custom business metric
meter = metrics.get_meter("sre-autopilot")
diagnoses_counter = meter.create_counter(
    "sre_diagnoses_total",
    description="Total diagnostic runs by the AI agent"
)

@app.post("/api/diagnose")
async def diagnose(alert: AlertPayload):
    with tracer.start_as_current_span("agent.diagnose") as span:
        span.set_attribute("alert.type", alert.type)
        result = await run_langgraph_agent(alert)
        diagnoses_counter.add(1, {"result": result["status"]})
        return result

  


  

    
📊 Step 3 — CloudWatch Dashboard & Alarms (Terraform)

    
# cloudwatch-alarms.tf
resource "aws_cloudwatch_metric_alarm" "p99_latency" {
  alarm_name          = "sre-autopilot-p99-latency"
  alarm_description   = "P99 latency > 500ms for 5 minutes"
  namespace           = "SREAutopilot"
  metric_name         = "http.server.duration"
  statistic           = "p99"
  period              = 300
  evaluation_periods  = 1
  threshold           = 500
  comparison_operator = "GreaterThanThreshold"
  alarm_actions       = [aws_sns_topic.pagerduty.arn]
}

resource "aws_cloudwatch_metric_alarm" "error_rate" {
  alarm_name    = "sre-autopilot-error-rate"
  alarm_description = "Error rate > 1%"
  # Uses metric math: errors / total * 100
  metric_query {
    id = "error_rate"
    expression = "(errors / total) * 100"
    return_data = true
  }
  metric_query { id = "errors"; metric { metric_name = "http.server.errors"; ... } }
  metric_query { id = "total";  metric { metric_name = "http.server.requests"; ... } }
  threshold = 1
}

  


  

    
🔄 The Complete Observability Loop

    

      
👤 User request
→
POST /api/diagnose → FastAPI creates root span with trace ID

      
📡 ADOT SDK
→
Auto-instruments Redis + Postgres calls. Custom spans for LangGraph steps.

      
📥 Collector
→
Batches spans + metrics. Samples 10% traces. Enriches with k8s metadata.

      
🔍 X-Ray
→
Receives trace. Postgres span shows db.statement + 258ms duration → visible in service map.

      
📊 CloudWatch
→
P99 metric crosses 500ms → alarm fires → SNS → PagerDuty → on-call pinged

      
🤖 SRE Agent
→
LangGraph agent queries X-Ray API → finds slow Postgres span → diagnoses missing index → posts fix to Slack
