Module 10 · Full Project

  
AWS ADOT Full Project Walkthrough

  
Assumes an existing EKS cluster. Install ADOT via the AWS EKS Add-on, configure the collector, instrument your app, and get traces in X-Ray, metrics in CloudWatch + AMP, and dashboards in Grafana — every command included.


  
  

    
10
Phases

    
50+
Steps

    
EKS Add-on
Install Method

    
~2hr
Estimated Time

    
∞
Observability

  


  
  

    
🗺️ End-to-End Architecture (EKS Add-on approach)

    
The EKS add-on installs the ADOT Operator in your cluster. You then tell it what to collect and where to send via a single Kubernetes CR (Custom Resource). No manual DaemonSet YAML needed.

    

      
🐍 Your App (FastAPI)→ADOT SDK (auto-instrumented)→OTLP gRPC :4317→ADOT Collector DaemonSet

      
ADOT Collector→X-Ray (traces)+CloudWatch (logs + EMF metrics)+AMP (Prometheus remote_write)

      
AMP + CloudWatch→Amazon Managed Grafana → unified dashboards + alerts

    

    

      
✅ EKS Add-on advantage: AWS manages the ADOT Operator version — security patches and upgrades via one CLI command. No need to manage Helm repos or operator YAML yourself.

      
💡 Key difference: With the add-on you configure the collector via an OpenTelemetryCollector CR (Kubernetes custom resource), not a raw DaemonSet. The operator creates the DaemonSet for you.

    

  


  
  

    📋
    
Phase 0 — Verify Your EKS Cluster
Confirm access, set env vars, install required CLI tools — 5 min

  

  

    
💡 Starting point: You have a running EKS cluster. You do NOT need to create it. We start from aws eks update-kubeconfig and go all the way to Grafana dashboards.


    

      1
      

        
Point kubectl at your cluster

        
Replace my-cluster and us-east-1 with your actual cluster name and region.

        
export CLUSTER_NAME=my-cluster
export AWS_REGION=us-east-1
export AWS_ACCOUNT_ID=$(aws sts get-caller-identity --query Account --output text)

# Download credentials into ~/.kube/config
aws eks update-kubeconfig \
  --name $CLUSTER_NAME \
  --region $AWS_REGION

# Verify you can talk to the cluster
kubectl get nodes
kubectl cluster-info

        
✅ Verify: kubectl get nodes lists your nodes with STATUS=Ready. kubectl cluster-info prints the control plane endpoint.

      

    


    

      2
      

        
Check required CLI tools are installed

        
# All of these must return a version number
aws --version          # AWS CLI v2
kubectl version --client
eksctl version         # needed for IRSA setup
helm version           # for any Helm-based installs

# If eksctl is missing:
# brew install eksctl   (Mac)
# or: https://eksctl.io/installation/

        
⚠️ eksctl is required even though we are NOT creating the cluster — we use it to set up IRSA (IAM Roles for Service Accounts), which is the safest way to give collector pods AWS permissions.

      

    


    

      3
      

        
Check Kubernetes version (ADOT add-on compatibility)

        
The ADOT EKS add-on requires Kubernetes 1.25 or later. Note the version — you will need it when installing the add-on.

        
# kubectl 1.28+ removed --short; use this instead:
kubectl version --output=yaml | grep -E "gitVersion|minor"
# Expected: serverVersion.minor: "29" (or 28, 30 — must be >= 25)

# Get the exact k8s minor version for the add-on lookup
export K8S_MINOR=$(kubectl version --output=yaml | grep -A3 serverVersion | grep minor | tr -dc '0-9')
echo "Cluster k8s version: 1.${K8S_MINOR}"

# Check which ADOT add-on versions are available
aws eks describe-addon-versions \
  --addon-name adot \
  --kubernetes-version "1.${K8S_MINOR}" \
  --query 'addons[0].addonVersions[*].addonVersion' \
  --output table

        
✅ Verify: You see a list of versions like v0.88.0-eksbuild.1, v0.90.0-eksbuild.1, etc. Pick the latest for your k8s version.

      

    

  


  
  

    🔐
    
Phase 1 — Enable OIDC & Create IAM Role (IRSA)
Pod-level AWS permissions — no long-lived credentials anywhere — 10 min

  

  

    
💡 Why IRSA? The ADOT Collector needs to write to X-Ray, CloudWatch, and AMP. Instead of putting AWS keys in environment variables (security risk), IRSA maps a Kubernetes service account to an IAM role. Pods get temporary tokens automatically — zero secrets in any YAML.


    

      1
      

        
Enable OIDC provider on your cluster (if not already done)

        
OIDC is the bridge between Kubernetes service accounts and AWS IAM. Many clusters already have this — the command is idempotent so safe to run regardless.

        
# Check if OIDC is already configured
aws eks describe-cluster \
  --name $CLUSTER_NAME \
  --query "cluster.identity.oidc.issuer" \
  --output text
# If this prints a URL, OIDC is already set up. Skip to step 2.

# If it prints "None", enable it:
eksctl utils associate-iam-oidc-provider \
  --cluster $CLUSTER_NAME \
  --region $AWS_REGION \
  --approve

        
✅ Verify: Re-run aws eks describe-cluster --name $CLUSTER_NAME --query "cluster.identity.oidc.issuer" — it should now print a URL like https://oidc.eks.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/id/XXXX

      

    


    

      2
      

        
Create IAM policy for ADOT Collector

        
Least-privilege policy — only the exact actions the collector needs: write traces to X-Ray, write metrics to CloudWatch and AMP, write logs to CloudWatch Logs.

        
cat > adot-policy.json << 'EOF'
{
  "Version": "2012-10-17",
  "Statement": [
    {
      "Sid": "XRayWrite",
      "Effect": "Allow",
      "Action": [
        "xray:PutTraceSegments",
        "xray:PutTelemetryRecords",
        "xray:GetSamplingRules",
        "xray:GetSamplingTargets"
      ],
      "Resource": "*"
    },
    {
      "Sid": "CloudWatchWrite",
      "Effect": "Allow",
      "Action": [
        "cloudwatch:PutMetricData",
        "logs:CreateLogGroup",
        "logs:CreateLogStream",
        "logs:PutLogEvents",
        "logs:DescribeLogStreams",
        "logs:DescribeLogGroups"
      ],
      "Resource": "*"
    },
    {
      "Sid": "AMPWrite",
      "Effect": "Allow",
      "Action": [
        "aps:RemoteWrite",
        "aps:GetSeries",
        "aps:GetLabels",
        "aps:GetMetricMetadata"
      ],
      "Resource": "*"
    },
    {
      "Sid": "EC2TagsForResourceDetection",
      "Effect": "Allow",
      "Action": [
        "ec2:DescribeTags",
        "ec2:DescribeInstances"
      ],
      "Resource": "*"
    }
  ]
}
EOF

export ADOT_POLICY_ARN=$(aws iam create-policy \
  --policy-name ADOTCollectorPolicy-${CLUSTER_NAME} \
  --policy-document file://adot-policy.json \
  --query Policy.Arn --output text)

echo "Policy ARN: $ADOT_POLICY_ARN"

      

    


    

      3
      

        
Create namespace and IAM service account

        
eksctl creates the IAM role, the trust policy, and the Kubernetes ServiceAccount — all in one command. The ServiceAccount gets an annotation pointing to the IAM role ARN.

        
# Create the namespace first
kubectl create namespace adot-system

# Create the IRSA service account
eksctl create iamserviceaccount \
  --name adot-collector \
  --namespace adot-system \
  --cluster $CLUSTER_NAME \
  --region $AWS_REGION \
  --attach-policy-arn $ADOT_POLICY_ARN \
  --approve \
  --override-existing-serviceaccounts

# Verify the annotation was applied
kubectl get serviceaccount adot-collector -n adot-system -o yaml

        
✅ Verify: The ServiceAccount YAML shows:
eks.amazonaws.com/role-arn: arn:aws:iam::<account>:role/eksctl-...
This annotation is what grants the pod AWS permissions without any credentials.

      

    

  


  
  

    ☁️
    
Phase 2 — Create AWS Backend Services
AMP workspace for Prometheus metrics · SNS topic for alerts — 10 min

  

  

    

      1
      

        
Create Amazon Managed Prometheus (AMP) workspace

        
AMP is a fully managed, serverless Prometheus. No installation, no scaling, no disk management. Your ADOT Collector will remote_write metrics here.

        
aws amp create-workspace \
  --alias ${CLUSTER_NAME}-metrics \
  --region $AWS_REGION

# Save the workspace ID — needed in ADOT config
export AMP_WORKSPACE_ID=$(aws amp list-workspaces \
  --alias ${CLUSTER_NAME}-metrics \
  --query 'workspaces[0].workspaceId' \
  --output text)

export AMP_REMOTE_WRITE_URL="https://aps-workspaces.${AWS_REGION}.amazonaws.com/workspaces/${AMP_WORKSPACE_ID}/api/v1/remote_write"

echo "AMP Workspace ID: $AMP_WORKSPACE_ID"
echo "Remote Write URL: $AMP_REMOTE_WRITE_URL"

        
✅ Verify: aws amp describe-workspace --workspace-id $AMP_WORKSPACE_ID --query 'workspace.status.statusCode' returns "ACTIVE"

      

    


    

      2
      

        
Create SNS topic for CloudWatch alarm notifications

        
export SNS_TOPIC_ARN=$(aws sns create-topic \
  --name ${CLUSTER_NAME}-alerts \
  --query TopicArn --output text)

# Subscribe your email (you MUST confirm the email before alarms fire)
aws sns subscribe \
  --topic-arn $SNS_TOPIC_ARN \
  --protocol email \
  --notification-endpoint your@email.com

echo "SNS ARN: $SNS_TOPIC_ARN"
echo "→ Check your email and click Confirm Subscription!"

        
⚠️ Confirm the subscription email — until you click the link, CloudWatch alarms will say "OK" but notifications won't be delivered.

      

    

  


  
  

    🔌
    
Phase 3 — Install ADOT via EKS Add-on
One command installs the ADOT Operator + CRDs into your cluster — 5 min

  

  

    
💡 What the add-on installs: The EKS add-on deploys the ADOT Operator (not the collector itself). The operator watches for OpenTelemetryCollector custom resources and creates/manages collector DaemonSets/Deployments for you. Think of it like the ArgoCD operator — you describe what you want, it makes it happen.


    

      0
      

        
Pre-check: Is cert-manager installed?

        
Newer ADOT add-on versions use admission webhooks that require cert-manager for TLS. Without it the add-on status stays DEGRADED. Check first.

        
# Check if cert-manager is already running
kubectl get pods -n cert-manager 2>/dev/null | grep cert-manager
# If you see Running pods → you're fine, skip to Step 1.

# If nothing shows, install cert-manager (takes ~1 min):
kubectl apply -f https://github.com/cert-manager/cert-manager/releases/latest/download/cert-manager.yaml

# Wait for it to be ready
kubectl wait --for=condition=ready pod \
  -l app.kubernetes.io/instance=cert-manager \
  -n cert-manager \
  --timeout=120s

        
✅ Verify: kubectl get pods -n cert-manager shows 3 pods (cert-manager, cert-manager-cainjector, cert-manager-webhook) all in Running state.

      

    


    

      1
      

        
Find the latest ADOT add-on version for your cluster

        
# List all available versions (pick the latest)
aws eks describe-addon-versions \
  --addon-name adot \
  --kubernetes-version $(kubectl version --short 2>/dev/null | grep Server | awk '{print $3}' | cut -d. -f1,2 | sed 's/v//') \
  --query 'addons[0].addonVersions[0].addonVersion' \
  --output text

# Save it
export ADOT_ADDON_VERSION="v0.90.0-eksbuild.1"  # replace with output above

      

    


    

      2
      

        
Install the ADOT add-on

        
This installs the ADOT Operator into the opentelemetry-operator-system namespace and registers the CRDs.

        
aws eks create-addon \
  --cluster-name $CLUSTER_NAME \
  --addon-name adot \
  --addon-version $ADOT_ADDON_VERSION \
  --resolve-conflicts OVERWRITE \
  --region $AWS_REGION

# Watch the installation progress (takes ~2 min)
watch -n5 aws eks describe-addon \
  --cluster-name $CLUSTER_NAME \
  --addon-name adot \
  --query 'addon.status' \
  --output text
# Wait until it prints: ACTIVE

        
✅ Verify: aws eks describe-addon --cluster-name $CLUSTER_NAME --addon-name adot --query 'addon.status' returns "ACTIVE"

      

    


    

      3
      

        
Verify the operator and CRDs are registered

        
# Operator pod should be Running
kubectl get pods -n opentelemetry-operator-system
# Expected: opentelemetry-operator-... 2/2 Running

# CRDs must be registered before you can create collectors
kubectl get crd | grep opentelemetry
# Expected:
# instrumentations.opentelemetry.io
# opentelemetrycollectors.opentelemetry.io

# Operator logs (optional — check for errors)
kubectl logs -n opentelemetry-operator-system \
  -l app.kubernetes.io/name=opentelemetry-operator \
  --tail=20

        
✅ Verify: Both CRDs appear and the operator pod shows 2/2 Running. If you see only 1/1, wait 30 more seconds — the second container (kube-rbac-proxy) can be slow to start.

      

    


    

      4
      

        
Grant the ADOT Collector pod the RBAC it needs

        
This ClusterRole gives the collector pods (not the operator — the operator already has its own RBAC from the add-on) two permissions they need:
1. Kubernetes service discovery — the Prometheus receiver lists/watches pods to find scrape targets
2. Resource metadata — the resourcedetection processor reads node/pod labels

        
cat > adot-collector-rbac.yaml << 'EOF'
apiVersion: rbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1
kind: ClusterRole
metadata:
  name: adot-collector-role
rules:
  # Prometheus receiver needs this to discover pods as scrape targets
  - apiGroups: [""]
    resources: ["pods", "nodes", "services", "endpoints", "namespaces"]
    verbs: ["get", "list", "watch"]
  - apiGroups: ["apps"]
    resources: ["replicasets"]
    verbs: ["get", "list", "watch"]
  # resourcedetection processor reads node metadata
  - apiGroups: [""]
    resources: ["nodes/proxy"]
    verbs: ["get"]
---
apiVersion: rbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1
kind: ClusterRoleBinding
metadata:
  name: adot-collector-role-binding
subjects:
- kind: ServiceAccount
  name: adot-collector
  namespace: adot-system
roleRef:
  kind: ClusterRole
  name: adot-collector-role
  apiGroup: rbac.authorization.k8s.io
EOF

kubectl apply -f adot-collector-rbac.yaml

# Confirm binding exists
kubectl get clusterrolebinding adot-collector-role-binding

        
⚠️ Without this RBAC, the Prometheus receiver silently fails — you get no scrape metrics but no error message. The pods run fine and OTLP data flows, but the Kubernetes pod auto-discovery returns empty results. Always apply this before creating the collector CR.

      

    

  


  
  

    ⚙️
    
Phase 4 — Deploy ADOT Collector via OpenTelemetryCollector CR
Define your pipeline in one YAML — the operator creates the DaemonSet for you — 15 min

  

  

    
💡 How it works: You create an OpenTelemetryCollector custom resource. The ADOT Operator sees it and creates a DaemonSet (one pod per node), a Service (so your apps can reach it at port 4317), and all the RBAC. You only manage the CR — never the DaemonSet directly.


    

      1
      

        
Create the OpenTelemetryCollector custom resource

        
This is the complete pipeline config — OTLP receiver → processors → X-Ray + CloudWatch + AMP exporters. Replace YOUR_AMP_REMOTE_WRITE_URL and YOUR_AWS_REGION with your actual values.

        
cat > adot-collector-cr.yaml << EOF
apiVersion: opentelemetry.io/v1alpha1
kind: OpenTelemetryCollector
metadata:
  name: adot-col
  namespace: adot-system
spec:
  # mode: daemonset = one collector pod per node (recommended for EKS)
  mode: daemonset
  serviceAccount: adot-collector
  resources:
    requests:
      cpu: 100m
      memory: 128Mi
    limits:
      cpu: 500m
      memory: 256Mi
  # The full collector pipeline config goes in spec.config
  config: |
    receivers:
      otlp:
        protocols:
          grpc:
            endpoint: 0.0.0.0:4317   # apps send traces/metrics here
          http:
            endpoint: 0.0.0.0:4318
      # Prometheus scraper — picks up pods with annotation prometheus.io/scrape: "true"
      prometheus:
        config:
          scrape_configs:
            - job_name: kubernetes-pods
              scrape_interval: 30s
              kubernetes_sd_configs:
                - role: pod
              relabel_configs:
                - source_labels: [__meta_kubernetes_pod_annotation_prometheus_io_scrape]
                  action: keep
                  regex: "true"
                - source_labels: [__meta_kubernetes_pod_annotation_prometheus_io_path]
                  action: replace
                  target_label: __metrics_path__
                  regex: (.+)
                - source_labels: [__address__, __meta_kubernetes_pod_annotation_prometheus_io_port]
                  action: replace
                  regex: ([^:]+)(?::\d+)?;(\d+)
                  replacement: \$\$1:\$\$2
                  target_label: __address__

    processors:
      # Batch before sending — reduces API calls to AWS
      batch:
        timeout: 1s
        send_batch_size: 50
      # Add EKS cluster name, node name, pod name to every span/metric
      resourcedetection:
        detectors: [env, eks, ec2]
        timeout: 10s
        override: false
      # Safety valve — prevents OOM if backends are slow
      memory_limiter:
        check_interval: 1s
        limit_mib: 200
        spike_limit_mib: 50

    exporters:
      # Traces → AWS X-Ray
      awsxray:
        region: ${AWS_REGION}
        indexed_attributes:
          - "http.method"
          - "http.status_code"
          - "service.name"
      # Logs → CloudWatch Logs
      awscloudwatchlogs:
        region: ${AWS_REGION}
        log_group_name: /aws/eks/${CLUSTER_NAME}/application
        log_stream_name: adot-collector   # fixed name; collector adds pod metadata as attributes
      awsemf:
        region: ${AWS_REGION}
        namespace: EKS/${CLUSTER_NAME}
        log_group_name: /aws/eks/${CLUSTER_NAME}/metrics
        dimension_rollup_option: NoDimensionRollup
      # Metrics → Amazon Managed Prometheus (AMP)
      prometheusremotewrite:
        endpoint: ${AMP_REMOTE_WRITE_URL}
        auth:
          authenticator: sigv4auth
        retry_on_failure:
          enabled: true
          initial_interval: 5s
          max_elapsed_time: 300s

    extensions:
      health_check:
        endpoint: 0.0.0.0:13133
      # sigv4auth signs requests to AMP with AWS credentials from the IRSA role
      sigv4auth:
        region: ${AWS_REGION}
        service: aps
      pprof:
        endpoint: 0.0.0.0:1777

    service:
      extensions: [health_check, sigv4auth, pprof]
      pipelines:
        traces:
          receivers:  [otlp]
          processors: [memory_limiter, resourcedetection, batch]
          exporters:  [awsxray]
        metrics/prometheus:
          receivers:  [prometheus]
          processors: [memory_limiter, batch]
          exporters:  [prometheusremotewrite, awsemf]
        metrics/otlp:
          receivers:  [otlp]
          processors: [memory_limiter, resourcedetection, batch]
          exporters:  [prometheusremotewrite, awsemf]
        logs:
          receivers:  [otlp]
          processors: [memory_limiter, batch]
          exporters:  [awscloudwatchlogs]
EOF

kubectl apply -f adot-collector-cr.yaml

      

    


    

      2
      

        
Verify the operator created the DaemonSet and Service

        
# The operator should have created these automatically
kubectl get opentelemetrycollector -n adot-system
# Expected: adot-col  DaemonSet  ...

kubectl get daemonset -n adot-system
# Expected: adot-col-collector  DESIRED=3  READY=3

kubectl get service -n adot-system
# Expected: adot-col-collector  ClusterIP  <ip>  4317/TCP,4318/TCP

# Check collector pods are healthy
kubectl get pods -n adot-system
# Expected: adot-col-collector-xxxxx  Running (one per node)

        
✅ Verify: DaemonSet DESIRED equals your node count and READY matches DESIRED. If pods are in CrashLoopBackOff, check logs:
kubectl logs -n adot-system -l app.kubernetes.io/name=adot-col-collector --tail=30

      

    


    

      3
      

        
Confirm the collector health endpoint responds

        
# Port-forward to the health check endpoint
kubectl port-forward -n adot-system \
  $(kubectl get pod -n adot-system -l app.kubernetes.io/name=adot-col-collector -o jsonpath='{.items[0].metadata.name}') \
  13133:13133 &

# In another terminal:
curl http://localhost:13133/
# Expected: {"status":"Server available","upSince":"..."}

# Kill the port-forward when done
kill %1

        
✅ Verify: curl http://localhost:13133/ returns "Server available". This confirms the collector is running and its pipeline is loaded without errors.

      

    


    

      4
      

        
How to update the collector config in the future

        
You never edit the DaemonSet directly — edit the CR and the operator reconciles.

        
# Edit the CR directly:
kubectl edit opentelemetrycollector adot-col -n adot-system

# Or apply an updated file:
kubectl apply -f adot-collector-cr.yaml

# The operator automatically performs a rolling restart — no downtime
kubectl rollout status daemonset/adot-col-collector -n adot-system

      

    

  


  
  

    🐍
    
Phase 5 — Instrument Your FastAPI App
Auto-instrument HTTP, DB, Redis — add custom spans — deploy with OTLP env vars — 20 min

  

  

    

      1
      

        
Add OpenTelemetry packages to requirements.txt

        
# requirements.txt
fastapi==0.110.0
uvicorn[standard]==0.27.1

# Core OTel SDK
opentelemetry-sdk==1.23.0
opentelemetry-exporter-otlp-proto-grpc==1.23.0

# Auto-instrumentation libraries (zero code change for these)
opentelemetry-instrumentation-fastapi==0.44b0
opentelemetry-instrumentation-requests==0.44b0
opentelemetry-instrumentation-sqlalchemy==0.44b0
opentelemetry-instrumentation-redis==0.44b0
opentelemetry-instrumentation-logging==0.44b0

# AWS-specific: X-Ray ID format + propagator
aws-opentelemetry-distro==0.3.1

      

    


    

      2
      

        
Create telemetry.py — one-time bootstrap

        
Call this once at app startup. It sets up the TracerProvider and auto-patches FastAPI, SQLAlchemy, Redis, requests — all without modifying those libraries.

        
# telemetry.py
import os
from opentelemetry import trace, metrics
from opentelemetry.sdk.trace import TracerProvider
from opentelemetry.sdk.trace.export import BatchSpanProcessor
from opentelemetry.sdk.metrics import MeterProvider
from opentelemetry.sdk.metrics.export import PeriodicExportingMetricReader
from opentelemetry.exporter.otlp.proto.grpc.trace_exporter import OTLPSpanExporter
from opentelemetry.exporter.otlp.proto.grpc.metric_exporter import OTLPMetricExporter
from opentelemetry.sdk.resources import Resource, SERVICE_NAME, SERVICE_VERSION
from opentelemetry.instrumentation.fastapi import FastAPIInstrumentor
from opentelemetry.instrumentation.sqlalchemy import SQLAlchemyInstrumentor
from opentelemetry.instrumentation.redis import RedisInstrumentor
from opentelemetry.instrumentation.requests import RequestsInstrumentor
from opentelemetry.propagate import set_global_textmap
from opentelemetry.propagators.aws import AwsXRayPropagator
from opentelemetry.sdk.extension.aws.trace import AwsXRayIdGenerator

def setup_telemetry(app, db_engine=None):
    # OTLP endpoint = the ADOT Collector Service we deployed in Phase 4
    # adot-col-collector.adot-system.svc.cluster.local is the DNS name
    # the operator created automatically
    otlp_endpoint = os.getenv(
        "OTLP_ENDPOINT",
        "http://adot-col-collector.adot-system.svc.cluster.local:4317"
    )

    resource = Resource(attributes={
        SERVICE_NAME:    os.getenv("SERVICE_NAME", "my-app"),
        SERVICE_VERSION: os.getenv("SERVICE_VERSION", "1.0.0"),
        "deployment.environment": os.getenv("ENVIRONMENT", "production"),
        "k8s.cluster.name": os.getenv("CLUSTER_NAME", "unknown"),
    })

    # ── Traces ──────────────────────────────────────────
    # AwsXRayIdGenerator: formats trace IDs as X-Ray requires (1-{epoch}-{random})
    tracer_provider = TracerProvider(
        resource=resource,
        id_generator=AwsXRayIdGenerator()
    )
    tracer_provider.add_span_processor(
        BatchSpanProcessor(
            OTLPSpanExporter(endpoint=otlp_endpoint),
            max_queue_size=2048,
            max_export_batch_size=512,
            export_timeout_millis=30000,
        )
    )
    trace.set_tracer_provider(tracer_provider)

    # AwsXRayPropagator: reads/writes X-Amzn-Trace-Id header
    # This makes traces chain across service boundaries automatically
    set_global_textmap(AwsXRayPropagator())

    # ── Metrics ──────────────────────────────────────────
    meter_provider = MeterProvider(
        resource=resource,
        metric_readers=[
            PeriodicExportingMetricReader(
                OTLPMetricExporter(endpoint=otlp_endpoint),
                export_interval_millis=30_000,
            )
        ],
    )
    metrics.set_meter_provider(meter_provider)

    # ── Auto-instrumentation ─────────────────────────────
    FastAPIInstrumentor.instrument_app(app)  # all HTTP routes get spans
    RequestsInstrumentor().instrument()       # outbound HTTP calls get spans
    if db_engine:
        SQLAlchemyInstrumentor().instrument(engine=db_engine)

    RedisInstrumentor().instrument()

    return trace.get_tracer(os.getenv("SERVICE_NAME", "my-app"))

      

    


    

      3
      

        
Wire telemetry into your FastAPI app + add custom spans

        
# main.py
from fastapi import FastAPI
from opentelemetry import trace, metrics
from telemetry import setup_telemetry

app = FastAPI()
tracer = setup_telemetry(app)

# Custom business metrics (beyond what auto-instrumentation gives)
meter = metrics.get_meter("my-app")
request_counter = meter.create_counter(
    "http_requests_total", description="Total requests by route and status")
latency_histogram = meter.create_histogram(
    "http_request_duration_seconds", description="Request latency distribution")
error_counter = meter.create_counter(
    "http_errors_total", description="Errors by type")

@app.get("/api/diagnose")
async def diagnose(issue: str):
    # Custom span wraps your business logic
    with tracer.start_as_current_span("sre.diagnose") as span:
        # Attributes show up as X-Ray annotations — searchable in console
        span.set_attribute("sre.issue_type", issue)
        span.set_attribute("user.team", "platform")

        request_counter.add(1, {"route": "/diagnose", "method": "GET"})

        try:
            result = await run_diagnosis(issue, span)
            return result
        except Exception as e:
            error_counter.add(1, {"error_type": type(e).__name__})
            span.record_exception(e)
            span.set_status(trace.Status(trace.StatusCode.ERROR, str(e)))
            raise

async def run_diagnosis(issue: str, span):
    # Nested spans for sub-operations — appear as children in X-Ray waterfall
    with tracer.start_as_current_span("llm.invoke") as child:
        child.set_attribute("llm.model", "claude-sonnet-4-6")
        # ... your LangGraph / Claude call here ...
        return {"diagnosis": "root cause found", "confidence": 0.92}

@app.get("/health")
async def health():
    return {"status": "ok"}

      

    


    

      4
      

        
Create a Dockerfile and build your container image

        
You need a Docker image to deploy on EKS. Here is the complete Dockerfile for the FastAPI app from Step 3.

        
# Dockerfile (put this in the same folder as main.py, telemetry.py, requirements.txt)
FROM python:3.11-slim

WORKDIR /app

# Install dependencies first (layer caching — only rebuilds if requirements change)
COPY requirements.txt .
RUN pip install --no-cache-dir -r requirements.txt

# Copy app code
COPY main.py telemetry.py ./

# Run with uvicorn — expose port 8000
EXPOSE 8000
CMD ["uvicorn", "main:app", "--host", "0.0.0.0", "--port", "8000"]

        
💡 Verify your folder structure:
my-app/
├── Dockerfile
├── requirements.txt
├── main.py
└── telemetry.py

      

    


    

      5
      

        
Create an ECR repository and push your image

        
ECR (Elastic Container Registry) is where Kubernetes pulls your Docker image from. Every EKS cluster can pull from ECR in the same account without extra credentials.

        
# Create ECR repository (one-time)
aws ecr create-repository \
  --repository-name my-app \
  --region $AWS_REGION

export ECR_URI="${AWS_ACCOUNT_ID}.dkr.ecr.${AWS_REGION}.amazonaws.com/my-app"
echo "ECR URI: $ECR_URI"

# Authenticate Docker to ECR (token valid for 12 hours)
aws ecr get-login-password --region $AWS_REGION | \
  docker login --username AWS --password-stdin \
  "${AWS_ACCOUNT_ID}.dkr.ecr.${AWS_REGION}.amazonaws.com"

# Build image (run from the folder containing your Dockerfile)
docker build -t my-app:latest .

# Tag and push
docker tag my-app:latest ${ECR_URI}:latest
docker push ${ECR_URI}:latest

echo "Image pushed: ${ECR_URI}:latest"

        
✅ Verify: aws ecr list-images --repository-name my-app --region $AWS_REGION shows imageTag: "latest". Now update the deployment YAML below with this ECR URI.

      

    


    

      6
      

        
Deploy your app with the OTLP endpoint env var

        
The critical env var is OTLP_ENDPOINT — it tells your app where to send telemetry. The value is the DNS name of the Service that the ADOT Operator created automatically in Phase 4. Also includes a Service resource so you can reach the app via port-forward or LoadBalancer.

        
# Replace <YOUR_ACCOUNT_ID> and <REGION> with your values,
# or just paste the ECR_URI you got from Step 5:
# export ECR_URI="${AWS_ACCOUNT_ID}.dkr.ecr.${AWS_REGION}.amazonaws.com/my-app"

cat > app-deployment.yaml << EOF
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: my-app
  namespace: default
spec:
  replicas: 2
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: my-app
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: my-app
      annotations:
        # ADOT Prometheus receiver picks up pods with this annotation
        prometheus.io/scrape: "true"
        prometheus.io/path: "/metrics"
        prometheus.io/port: "8000"
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: my-app
        image: ${ECR_URI}:latest     # ← uses the ECR_URI env var from Step 5
        ports:
        - containerPort: 8000
        env:
        - name: OTLP_ENDPOINT
          value: "http://adot-col-collector.adot-system.svc.cluster.local:4317"
        - name: SERVICE_NAME
          value: "my-app"
        - name: ENVIRONMENT
          value: "production"
        - name: CLUSTER_NAME
          value: "${CLUSTER_NAME}"
        resources:
          requests: { cpu: 250m, memory: 256Mi }
          limits:   { cpu: 1000m, memory: 512Mi }
        readinessProbe:
          httpGet: { path: /health, port: 8000 }
          initialDelaySeconds: 10
          periodSeconds: 5
---
# Service is required — used for kubectl port-forward and internal DNS
apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: my-app
  namespace: default
spec:
  selector:
    app: my-app
  ports:
  - name: http
    port: 80
    targetPort: 8000
  type: ClusterIP   # use LoadBalancer if you want an external URL
EOF

kubectl apply -f app-deployment.yaml
kubectl rollout status deployment/my-app
kubectl get svc my-app   # confirm Service was created

        
✅ Verify: kubectl get pods -l app=my-app shows 2/2 Running. kubectl get svc my-app shows the Service with port 80. Check app logs for OTel errors:
kubectl logs -l app=my-app --tail=20 | grep -i otel — should show no errors. Spans start flowing after the first HTTP request.

      

    

  


  
  

    🔍
    
Phase 6 — Verify Traces in AWS X-Ray
Generate traffic · check Service Map · set up sampling rules · create X-Ray Groups — 10 min

  

  

    

      1
      

        
Generate traffic and check X-Ray console

        
# Get your app URL (or use kubectl port-forward for testing)
kubectl port-forward svc/my-app 8080:80 &

# Send 20 test requests
for i in $(seq 1 20); do
  curl -s "http://localhost:8080/api/diagnose?issue=cpu_high" > /dev/null
  sleep 0.5
done

kill %1  # stop port-forward

echo "Open: https://console.aws.amazon.com/xray/home#/service-map"

        
✅ Verify in X-Ray Console:
1. Service Map → your service appears as a circle
2. Traces → sort by timestamp → your 20 requests appear
3. Click any trace → see the waterfall: FastAPI span → custom sre.diagnose span → llm.invoke child span
4. Click a span → see your custom attributes (sre.issue_type, llm.model)

      

    


    

      2
      

        
Configure sampling rules (important for production cost control)

        
Default is 5% sampling. In production: 100% for errors, 100% for slow requests (>1s), 5% for normal traffic.

        
# Rule 1: Always sample errors
# X-Ray sampling rule Attributes use exact string matching — no wildcards.
# Error detection (HTTP 5xx / fault=true) is handled by the X-Ray filter expression,
# not by Attributes. Use the correct approach:
aws xray create-sampling-rule --sampling-rule '{
  "RuleName": "ErrorTraces",
  "Priority": 1,
  "ReservoirSize": 10,
  "FixedRate": 1.0,
  "URLPath": "*",
  "Host": "*",
  "HTTPMethod": "*",
  "ServiceName": "my-app",
  "ServiceType": "AWS::EKS::Container",
  "ResourceARN": "*",
  "Attributes": {}
}'
# Note: Attributes filtering uses EXACT match only — wildcards like "5*" are NOT supported.
# Error-only sampling is enforced via X-Ray Groups (see Step 3) + Insights, not Attributes.

# Rule 2: Default rule (already built in — 5% of all other traffic)
# No action needed; X-Ray creates a Default rule automatically.

# Check all active rules
aws xray get-sampling-rules \
  --query 'SamplingRuleRecords[*].SamplingRule.{Name:RuleName,Rate:FixedRate,Priority:Priority}'

      

    


    

      3
      

        
Create an X-Ray Group for SLO violations

        
X-Ray Groups filter traces and power Insights (automated anomaly detection). This group captures all traces that violate your SLO.

        
aws xray create-group \
  --group-name "SLO-Violations" \
  --filter-expression "responsetime > 1 OR error = true OR fault = true" \
  --insights-configuration InsightsEnabled=true,NotificationsEnabled=true \
  --region $AWS_REGION

# In the console: X-Ray → Groups → SLO-Violations
# After 5-10 min of traffic, check Insights for anomaly detections

        
💡 X-Ray Insights uses ML to automatically detect when error rates or latency patterns deviate from baseline — no threshold tuning needed. It fires SNS notifications when it finds an anomaly.

      

    

  


  
  

    📊
    
Phase 7 — CloudWatch: Metrics, Logs & Alarms
Enable Container Insights · create P99 alarm · composite alarm · dashboard — 20 min

  

  

    

      1
      

        
Enable Container Insights (automatic pod/node metrics)

        
One command — gives you CPU, memory, network I/O, disk for every pod, deployment, and node. No application code change needed.

        
# Install via EKS add-on (recommended)
aws eks create-addon \
  --cluster-name $CLUSTER_NAME \
  --addon-name amazon-cloudwatch-observability \
  --resolve-conflicts OVERWRITE \
  --region $AWS_REGION

# Wait for ACTIVE
aws eks describe-addon \
  --cluster-name $CLUSTER_NAME \
  --addon-name amazon-cloudwatch-observability \
  --query 'addon.status' --output text

# Verify pods are running
kubectl get pods -n amazon-cloudwatch

        
✅ Verify: CloudWatch Console → Container Insights → Performance Monitoring → select your cluster. Pod CPU and memory graphs appear within 2 minutes.

      

    


    

      2
      

        
Verify your custom app metrics arrived in CloudWatch

        
The ADOT awsemf exporter converts OTLP metrics to CloudWatch metrics with EMF format.

        
# List custom metrics in your namespace
aws cloudwatch list-metrics \
  --namespace "EKS/${CLUSTER_NAME}" \
  --query 'Metrics[*].MetricName' \
  --output table

# Get a sample value (last 5 minutes)
aws cloudwatch get-metric-statistics \
  --namespace "EKS/${CLUSTER_NAME}" \
  --metric-name http_requests_total \
  --start-time $(date -u -d '5 minutes ago' +%Y-%m-%dT%H:%M:%S) \
  --end-time $(date -u +%Y-%m-%dT%H:%M:%S) \
  --period 60 \
  --statistics Sum \
  --output table

        
✅ Verify: You see metric names like http_requests_total, http_request_duration_seconds in the output. If empty, generate more traffic and wait 60 seconds for the EMF exporter batch.

      

    


    

      3
      

        
Create P99 latency alarm

        
aws cloudwatch put-metric-alarm \
  --alarm-name "${CLUSTER_NAME}-P99-Latency-High" \
  --alarm-description "P99 latency > 1s — SLO breach" \
  --namespace "EKS/${CLUSTER_NAME}" \
  --metric-name http_request_duration_seconds \
  --extended-statistic p99 \
  --period 60 \
  --evaluation-periods 3 \
  --threshold 1.0 \
  --comparison-operator GreaterThanThreshold \
  --treat-missing-data notBreaching \
  --alarm-actions $SNS_TOPIC_ARN \
  --ok-actions $SNS_TOPIC_ARN \
  --region $AWS_REGION

      

    


    

      4
      

        
Create error rate alarm

        
aws cloudwatch put-metric-alarm \
  --alarm-name "${CLUSTER_NAME}-ErrorRate-High" \
  --alarm-description "Error rate > 1% — SLO breach" \
  --namespace "EKS/${CLUSTER_NAME}" \
  --metric-name http_errors_total \
  --statistic Sum \
  --period 60 \
  --evaluation-periods 2 \
  --threshold 10 \
  --comparison-operator GreaterThanThreshold \
  --alarm-actions $SNS_TOPIC_ARN \
  --region $AWS_REGION

      

    


    

      5
      

        
Create composite alarm (both signals = real incident)

        
A composite alarm fires only when multiple child alarms are all in ALARM state — dramatically fewer false positives than individual alarms.

        
aws cloudwatch put-composite-alarm \
  --alarm-name "${CLUSTER_NAME}-SLO-BREACH" \
  --alarm-description "CRITICAL: SLO breach confirmed — both P99 latency and error rate high" \
  --alarm-rule "ALARM(\"${CLUSTER_NAME}-P99-Latency-High\") AND ALARM(\"${CLUSTER_NAME}-ErrorRate-High\")" \
  --alarm-actions $SNS_TOPIC_ARN \
  --region $AWS_REGION

# Verify all three alarms are in OK state
aws cloudwatch describe-alarms \
  --alarm-name-prefix $CLUSTER_NAME \
  --query 'MetricAlarms[*].{Name:AlarmName,State:StateValue}' \
  --output table

        
💡 Composite Alarm Logic: P99 spikes alone could be a noisy neighbour. Errors alone could be a bad deployment. BOTH together = the service is actually broken. This is how you avoid 3am pages for transient blips.

      

    


    

      6
      

        
Test alarm end-to-end with a manual state change

        
# Manually trigger the alarm
aws cloudwatch set-alarm-state \
  --alarm-name "${CLUSTER_NAME}-P99-Latency-High" \
  --state-value ALARM \
  --state-reason "Manual test — verifying email delivery"

# Expect email within 60 seconds
# Then reset
aws cloudwatch set-alarm-state \
  --alarm-name "${CLUSTER_NAME}-P99-Latency-High" \
  --state-value OK \
  --state-reason "Test complete"

        
✅ Verify: You receive two emails — one ALARM and one OK — within 60 seconds of each command. Subject lines: ALARM: ${CLUSTER_NAME}-P99-Latency-High and OK: ...

      

    

  


  
  

    📈
    
Phase 8 — Verify Metrics in Amazon Managed Prometheus
Test PromQL queries against AMP — confirm remote_write is working — 10 min

  

  

    

      1
      

        
Query AMP using awscurl (SigV4 signed requests)

        
AMP requires AWS SigV4 authentication on every request. awscurl handles the signing automatically.

        
pip install awscurl

export AMP_QUERY_URL="https://aps-workspaces.${AWS_REGION}.amazonaws.com/workspaces/${AMP_WORKSPACE_ID}"

# 1. What metrics exist?
awscurl --service aps \
  "${AMP_QUERY_URL}/api/v1/label/__name__/values" \
  --region $AWS_REGION | python3 -m json.tool | grep -i "http_"

# 2. Request rate over last 5 minutes
awscurl --service aps \
  "${AMP_QUERY_URL}/api/v1/query" \
  --region $AWS_REGION \
  --data 'query=rate(http_requests_total[5m])' \
  | python3 -m json.tool

# 3. P99 latency
awscurl --service aps \
  "${AMP_QUERY_URL}/api/v1/query" \
  --region $AWS_REGION \
  --data 'query=histogram_quantile(0.99, rate(http_request_duration_seconds_bucket[5m]))' \
  | python3 -m json.tool

# 4. Error rate percentage
awscurl --service aps \
  "${AMP_QUERY_URL}/api/v1/query" \
  --region $AWS_REGION \
  --data 'query=rate(http_errors_total[5m]) / rate(http_requests_total[5m]) * 100' \
  | python3 -m json.tool

        
✅ Verify: Query 1 returns metric names including http_requests_total. Queries 2-4 return numeric value fields. If empty, generate more traffic: for i in $(seq 1 50); do curl -s http://localhost:8080/api/diagnose?issue=test; done

      

    


    

      2
      

        
Create IAM role so Grafana can query AMP

        
cat > grafana-trust.json << 'EOF'
{
  "Version": "2012-10-17",
  "Statement": [{
    "Effect": "Allow",
    "Principal": { "Service": "grafana.amazonaws.com" },
    "Action": "sts:AssumeRole"
  }]
}
EOF

export GRAFANA_ROLE_ARN=$(aws iam create-role \
  --role-name AMGQueryRole-${CLUSTER_NAME} \
  --assume-role-policy-document file://grafana-trust.json \
  --query Role.Arn --output text)

aws iam attach-role-policy \
  --role-name AMGQueryRole-${CLUSTER_NAME} \
  --policy-arn arn:aws:iam::aws:policy/AmazonPrometheusQueryAccess

aws iam attach-role-policy \
  --role-name AMGQueryRole-${CLUSTER_NAME} \
  --policy-arn arn:aws:iam::aws:policy/CloudWatchReadOnlyAccess

echo "Grafana Role: $GRAFANA_ROLE_ARN"

      

    

  


  
  

    📉
    
Phase 9 — Amazon Managed Grafana: Dashboards & Alerts
Create workspace · add data sources · build Golden Signals dashboard — 20 min

  

  

    
⚠️ Prerequisite: AWS IAM Identity Center (SSO) must be enabled — AMG uses AWS SSO for user login. If your account has never used IAM Identity Center, you must enable it first:
AWS Console → IAM Identity Center → Enable (takes ~2 min, one-time per account). If your org already uses SSO, skip this.


    

      1
      

        
Create Amazon Managed Grafana (AMG) workspace

        
export AMG_WORKSPACE_ID=$(aws grafana create-workspace \
  --workspace-name "${CLUSTER_NAME}-dashboards" \
  --account-access-type CURRENT_ACCOUNT \
  --authentication-providers AWS_SSO \
  --permission-type SERVICE_MANAGED \
  --workspace-data-sources PROMETHEUS CLOUDWATCH XRAY \
  --workspace-role-arn $GRAFANA_ROLE_ARN \
  --query 'workspace.id' --output text)

# Wait for ACTIVE (takes ~1 min)
aws grafana describe-workspace \
  --workspace-id $AMG_WORKSPACE_ID \
  --query 'workspace.status'

export AMG_URL=$(aws grafana describe-workspace \
  --workspace-id $AMG_WORKSPACE_ID \
  --query 'workspace.endpoint' --output text)

echo "Grafana URL: https://${AMG_URL}"

        
✅ Verify: Status returns "ACTIVE". Note the Grafana URL — but do NOT try to log in yet. You must assign yourself as a user first (Step 1b below).

      

    


    

      1b
      

        
Assign yourself as a Grafana Admin (required before first login)

        
Creating the workspace does NOT grant you login access. You must explicitly assign your IAM Identity Center user as a workspace admin. Without this step, the Grafana login page shows but you get "Access Denied".

        
# Get your IAM Identity Center User ID
# Go to: AWS Console → IAM Identity Center → Users → click your user → copy "User ID" (looks like a UUID)
# Then assign them to the workspace:

aws grafana update-permissions \
  --workspace-id $AMG_WORKSPACE_ID \
  --update-instruction-batch \
    action=ADD,role=ADMIN,users=[{id="<YOUR_SSO_USER_ID>",type=SSO_USER}]

# Alternatively via the Console (easier for first time):
# AWS Console → Amazon Grafana → your workspace → Authentication tab
# → Assign new user or group → search your name → Role: Admin → Assign

        
✅ Verify: Open https://${AMG_URL} in your browser → click "Sign in with AWS SSO" → enter your SSO credentials → you reach the Grafana home page. If you still see "Access Denied", wait 30 seconds and retry.

      

    


    

      2
      

        
Add Amazon Managed Prometheus as a data source

        
In Grafana UI: Connections → Data Sources → Add data source → Prometheus

        
# Settings in the Grafana UI:
Name:                SRE-AMP
URL:                 https://aps-workspaces.${AWS_REGION}.amazonaws.com/workspaces/${AMP_WORKSPACE_ID}
Authentication:      SigV4
  Default Region:    us-east-1
  Auth Provider:     AWS SDK Default

# Click "Save & Test" → should show green "Data source is working"

# Add CloudWatch data source:
Name:                SRE-CloudWatch
Default Region:      us-east-1
Authentication:      AWS SDK Default

# Add X-Ray data source:
Name:                SRE-XRay
Default Region:      us-east-1

      

    


    

      3
      

        
Build the Four Golden Signals dashboard

        
Create a new dashboard with 4 panels — one per Golden Signal. These are the minimum metrics every service must show.

        
# Panel 1 — Traffic (Requests per second)
# Data source: SRE-AMP (PromQL)
rate(http_requests_total[5m])
# Legend: {{service_name}} | Visualization: Time series

# Panel 2 — Errors (Error %)
# Data source: SRE-AMP (PromQL)
rate(http_errors_total[5m]) / rate(http_requests_total[5m]) * 100
# Add threshold line at y=1 (1% SLO) | Color: red above threshold

# Panel 3 — Latency (P50/P95/P99)
# Data source: SRE-AMP (PromQL) — add 3 queries:
histogram_quantile(0.50, rate(http_request_duration_seconds_bucket[5m]))
histogram_quantile(0.95, rate(http_request_duration_seconds_bucket[5m]))
histogram_quantile(0.99, rate(http_request_duration_seconds_bucket[5m]))
# Legend: P50 / P95 / P99 | Add threshold at y=1 (1s SLO)

# Panel 4 — Saturation (Pod CPU %)
# Data source: SRE-CloudWatch
# Namespace: ContainerInsights | Metric: pod_cpu_utilization
# Dim: ClusterName=$CLUSTER_NAME, Namespace=default
# Add threshold at y=80 (scale-up trigger)

# Save dashboard as "Golden Signals — ${CLUSTER_NAME}"
# Set auto-refresh: 30s

        
✅ Verify: All 4 panels show data with time series lines. The latency panel shows 3 lines (P50 < P95 < P99). Set time range to "Last 30 minutes".

      

    


    

      4
      

        
Create a Grafana alert rule on P99 latency

        
Grafana alert rules evaluate PromQL on a schedule and fire to a notification channel. More expressive than CloudWatch alarms for complex conditions.

        
# In Grafana UI:
# Alerting → Alert rules → New alert rule

Name: "SLO: P99 Latency > 1s"

# Query A:
histogram_quantile(0.99, rate(http_request_duration_seconds_bucket[5m]))

# Condition:
WHEN last() OF A IS ABOVE 1.0

# Evaluation:
Every: 1m
For: 3m   # must be true for 3 consecutive minutes before firing

# Annotations:
Summary: "P99 latency {{ $value | printf "%.2f" }}s exceeds 1s SLO"
Description: "Check X-Ray traces for slow spans: https://console.aws.amazon.com/xray"

# Notification policy: route to your email/PagerDuty contact point

      

    

  


  
  

    🧪
    
Phase 10 — End-to-End Verification
Load test · trace a slow request · confirm all 4 observability layers are live — 15 min

  

  

    

      1
      

        
Run a load test and watch all layers respond

        
# Install hey — choose ONE of these options:
brew install hey                          # macOS (easiest)
# sudo apt-get install hey               # Ubuntu/Debian
# go install github.com/rakyll/hey@latest # if Go is installed
# curl -L https://hey-release.s3.us-east-2.amazonaws.com/hey_linux_amd64 -o hey && chmod +x hey && sudo mv hey /usr/local/bin/  # Linux binary

# Get your app URL
kubectl port-forward svc/my-app 8080:80 &

# 500 requests at 20 RPS
hey -n 500 -q 20 "http://localhost:8080/api/diagnose?issue=cpu_high"

# While it runs, open these in parallel:
# 1. X-Ray Console → Service Map (updates in ~30s)
# 2. Grafana → Golden Signals dashboard (updates every 30s)
# 3. CloudWatch → your dashboard (updates every 60s)
# 4. kubectl top pods (live resource usage)

kill %1

        
✅ Expected after load test:
• X-Ray Service Map: your service circle with avg latency and 0% error
• Grafana Traffic panel: spike to ~20 RPS
• Grafana Latency panel: stable P99 line
• CloudWatch: http_requests_total sum rises

      

    


    

      2
      

        
Trace a deliberately slow request end-to-end

        
Add a temporary endpoint with artificial latency. Find the exact span causing it in X-Ray — this is the core debugging workflow.

        
# Add to your app temporarily:
@app.get("/api/test/slow")
async def slow_endpoint():
    with tracer.start_as_current_span("slow.db.query") as span:
        span.set_attribute("db.statement", "SELECT * FROM events LIMIT 100000")
        span.set_attribute("db.system", "postgresql")
        await asyncio.sleep(2.3)   # simulate slow query
    return {"status": "done"}

# Redeploy and hit it
kubectl rollout restart deployment/my-app
curl "http://localhost:8080/api/test/slow"

# In X-Ray Console:
# 1. Traces → filter: responsetime > 2
# 2. Click the 2.3s trace
# 3. Expand waterfall → see "slow.db.query" span = 2300ms
# 4. Click that span → see db.statement attribute with the slow SQL
# 5. This is how you find slow queries in production in 30 seconds

        
💡 The real value of distributed tracing: Without it you see "P99 = 2.3s" in Grafana. With X-Ray you see exactly which span, which function, which database query is slow. Mean time to root cause: 30 seconds instead of 30 minutes.

      

    


    

      3
      

        
Upgrade the ADOT add-on (Day 2 operations)

        
When a new ADOT version is released, upgrade with one command. The operator rolls out changes with zero downtime.

        
# Check if an update is available
aws eks describe-addon-versions \
  --addon-name adot \
  --kubernetes-version 1.29 \
  --query 'addons[0].addonVersions[0].addonVersion'

# Apply the upgrade
aws eks update-addon \
  --cluster-name $CLUSTER_NAME \
  --addon-name adot \
  --addon-version v0.92.0-eksbuild.1 \   # replace with new version
  --resolve-conflicts OVERWRITE \
  --region $AWS_REGION

# Watch the rollout
kubectl rollout status daemonset/adot-col-collector -n adot-system

      

    


    

      4
      

        
Quick Reference: day-to-day commands

        
# ── ADOT Operator (EKS Add-on) ──────────────────────────
kubectl get opentelemetrycollector -n adot-system    # CR status
kubectl describe otelcol adot-col -n adot-system     # detailed state
kubectl get daemonset -n adot-system                 # collector pods

# ── Collector health & logs ───────────────────────────
kubectl logs -n adot-system \
  -l app.kubernetes.io/name=adot-col-collector --tail=30
kubectl top pod -n adot-system                       # memory/CPU of collector

# ── Reload after config change ────────────────────────
kubectl apply -f adot-collector-cr.yaml              # operator reconciles
kubectl rollout status ds/adot-col-collector -n adot-system

# ── Query AMP (CLI) ───────────────────────────────────
awscurl --service aps \
  "${AMP_QUERY_URL}/api/v1/query" \
  --region $AWS_REGION \
  --data 'query=rate(http_requests_total[5m])'

# ── View X-Ray traces (CLI) ───────────────────────────
aws xray get-trace-summaries \
  --start-time $(date -u -d '30 minutes ago' +%Y-%m-%dT%H:%M:%S) \
  --end-time $(date -u +%Y-%m-%dT%H:%M:%S) \
  --filter-expression "responsetime > 1" \
  --region $AWS_REGION

# ── CloudWatch alarms ─────────────────────────────────
aws cloudwatch describe-alarms \
  --state-value ALARM \
  --query 'MetricAlarms[*].{Name:AlarmName,Reason:StateReason}'

OpenTelemetry · Start Here

      
OpenTelemetry — From Zero to Expert

      
The vendor-neutral standard for traces, metrics & logs. Learn to see inside any system — no prior observability knowledge required.

    


    

      
👶 ELI5

      
Your app is a sealed factory. Things go in, things come out, but when it's slow or broken you're standing outside guessing. OpenTelemetry (OTel) puts sensors on every machine inside the factory — every request, every database call, every error — and streams that data out to a screen you can watch. This guide takes you from "what is observability?" all the way to instrumenting a real Python service three different ways and reading the traces like a pro.

    


    

      
🎯 What You'll Be Able to Do

      

        
🔭
Explain OTel
Articulate observability vs monitoring and why a vendor-neutral standard matters.
Concepts

        
🧩
Know the signals
Traces, metrics and logs — what each is, and how they correlate.
Fundamentals

        
⚡
Instrument code
Auto, manual and hybrid instrumentation in real Python.
Hands-on

        
🚀
Ship to a backend
Run a Collector and read traces in a Jaeger-style UI.
Production

      

    


    

      
👥 Who This Is For

      

        

          
✅ Perfect if you are…

          
    
            
  • A developer who has never instrumented anything
    • 
            
  • A DevOps/SRE engineer wiring up observability
    • 
            
  • Coming from vendor SDKs (Datadog, New Relic) and want the open standard
    • 
            
  • Curious how traces, metrics and logs actually work
    • 
          

        

        

          
🧭 Prerequisites

          
    
            
  • Basic Python (you can read a Flask route)
    • 
            
  • You've used a terminal and pip install
    • 
            
  • That's it — everything else is taught from scratch
    •
